THE RED PAGE.
[A note is to hand from the Secretary to the Society for
" ell }q Rather Tired of Things. ‘ Dear Bulletin,— At the
weekly meeting of this society*, held on Tuesday evening
last, it was unanimously resolved : “That we are Rather
JLired of Burns.” Kindly notice and oblige.’ And
George Black agreeably closes the controversy.)
George Black further writes—
career or my ‘ ponderous uninspired
verses been the subject of debate, the sympathy
shown by the Red Page for my failings, trials and
struggles might have blunted the point of my
skene-dhu. But you quite mistake
the issue. I am not on trial ; the Red Page is—
for defaming Robert Burns; whose ‘ handsome
face it had the desire, if not the skill ‘to spoil.’
TThis is the interesting method of the politician who
atnrms what he is supposed to prove, and drowns protest
with leather lungs—a method of Misrepresentation plus
Vociferation. If any body is on his trial it is obviously
Burns; and G. 8., if he will, may call himself counsel for
the defence—or devil's advocate.]
I want justice for Burns; I ask no quarter for
myself ; I never sought it in my life; I never re-
ceived it ; I never required it less than I do now.
Nor can the patronising condescension of your
reviewer induce me to extend it to him ; for I
shrewdly suspect him to be more concerned about
the safety of his own literary complexion than
about mine. If that be not his motive, then how
blinded by conceit must be the offender who
gravely takes credit to himself (the criminal) for
the clemency he shows towards me—the counsel
for the prosecution. Let me say now, that had
he, no matter how successfully, followed out the
plan of attack devised at his eagle’s nest —decor-
ated doubtless with the shank-bones of slain poets
it would in no way have cleared him from the
definite charges brought against his journalistic
honesty. So far, he has not attempted to defend
himself His mouth has been full of the in-
coherent prevarications of the detected cul-
prit. His replies, being his, are, of course,
satisfactory to him, but his satisfaction cannot be
so great as mine. Their weak evasiveness proves
the strength of my case.
[More Vociferation. Three specimens of what passes
for Burns’s work were printed on Dee. 4—“ The Dusty
Miller,” “ A Red, Red Rose,” “ Auld Lang Syne"—
together with the originals from which Burns horrowed.
G. B. did not—he could not —question theaecuracy of the
presentation, or destroy the damning inferences. What
he could and did do was to cloud argument with a dust
of side-issues. And he kicks up a very effective dust
regarding the Vamps which were not paralleled with
the originals—for the reasons that readers tire of 9ueh
parallels ; and a host of other topics clamor for treat-
ment ; and, anyway, a yard of space so devoted is as good
as a mile when Hanley’s notes are available by the curious
in respect of the other 1759 yards. Also, discussion is in-
terminable as to whether, in respect of any particular
Vamp, Burns is entitled to 90 per cent, of credit or merely
to 10 per cent. : that is a matter on which everyone must
make up sis own mind according to its lights.]
To meet his misrepresentations, I must recapi-
tulate : Burns’s reputation, according to acknow-
ledged authorities, is based on his Poems and his
Satires. They assert, nevertheless, that he lifts
his head, high as the tuneful morning stars, among
the world’s song-writers, on the footing of songs
indisputably his. Here the worm that bores into
reputations gets a chance. Burns, purely for
love of country, endeavored to enrich her min-
strelsy by collecting for publication the old bal-
lads that ran like fire along the country-side at
merry-making times. Some, wedded to beautiful
airs, were unprintable; these here-wrote. Others,
word-perfect, were twined with poor melodies;
for these he found fresh music. In other cases, a
felicitous phrase, line, or verse was embedded in a
mass of vulgar verbiage. Burns liberated the gem
and set it in a circlet of jewels. He did more ; in
every case he forwarded copies of the originals
[This should be ‘in many cases’—there is no evidence
to show that Burns forwarded his originals * in every
case.’ For a would-be precisian in language, G.B. is
singularly unprecise.]
with his versions to the publishers. (Since his
day unscrupulous men have traded on his name,
and published editions which contained songs he
never wrote and never claimed. This caused a
confusion which was increased by the fact that
Hogg, Cunningham, Stenhouse, Buchan, and
others were so regardless of Burns’ reputation
as to forge ‘originals’ to songs that were unde-
niably Burns’. By the way, instead of taking
away from Burns, Henley has actually restored
to him songs which were wrested from him by
these forgeries. How many more of these so-called
Burns’- ‘ originals ’ are forged?) Had Australia
possessed an unwritten song-literature, how highly
would, the poet, say Henry Lawson, have been ex-
tolled in your columns, who played towards it the
part of a Burns, and rescued it from extinction.
[Doubtless ; but there would have been preserved a
proper discrimination in extol in ent. Lawson, as a vam-
per or editor, would have got a vamper or editor’s credit,
and no. more. The real Burns, sp far as is known, rari-lv
claimed more; but for the legendary Burns, the Scottish
M umbo-Jumbo, the credit of full authorship is often
claimed—by statement or by suppression. Whence these
tears.]
But how does ‘‘The Bulletin’ reward Burns for
this great mass of laborious and gratuitous service
to Scots', nay, to universal literature? He is
treated like a detected thief; he is denied all
originality ; he has done little, says Red Page, but
[Misrepresentation again. Burns was never here
‘denied all originality.’]
milk other men’s cows into his own bucket. Your
[Misrepresentation again. What was said was (R.P.
Oct. 23): ‘The later Burns survives in virtue of the
ability with wliioh lie milked other men’s cows into his
own bucket’—a very different statement. Admire the
Black art of perversion !]
reviewer is so badly cornered by me on this point
that, seeking a mouse-hole of escape, he endeavors
to apply what I said concerning some of Burns’
contributions to Johnson’s and Thomson’s collec-
tions to all the poet ever wrote, and claims, by the
detachment of certain phrases of mine from their
context, to prove that he and I are agreed that
Burns is ‘a mere vain per, mender and tinker, and
little more.’ lam not in any way concerned about
[Nothing of the sort. * According to G. 8., Burns took,
built Up, put new words to, collected, adapted, borrowed
—other fellows’ songs or fragments of songs.’ (R.P.,
Feb. 5). There is no general implication in this state-
ment, which obviously had force only so far as G.B.’s own
particular admissions gave it force.]
his alleged opinion of my opinions. Let those
who desire to pass judgment refer to your issues
of Oct. 25 and Dec. 4, 1897, and Feb. 5 and 19,
1898.
[Here two paragraphs not concerned with Burns—and
replies thereto—are reluctantly excised for reasons of
space,]
Youv reviewer accuses me of misleading, be-
cause, he says, I apply what he wrote of ‘ the
average Scotchman ’to Burns’ countrymen. I
accuse him now of quibbling. I took the words
‘average Scotchman,’ as I do now, to signify the
bulk of the people. I quoted them merely because
they occurred in a sentence which strikingly con-
densed much that Red Page said before and after.
Let me show that I was not unjust to him by
quoting a preceding sentence. Therein he said ;
‘A graver injury has been done by Burns’s
countrymen to Burns himself.’ Throughout, as
will be proved in the succeeding paragraph, he
railed at Scotchmen in the aggregate—their lofty
notions of Burns, their meanness, their lack of
humor, their love of whisky. When he argues
that the average Scot constitutes ‘a class ’ ; when
he accuses me of misrepresentation while neglect-
ing to ascertain what he had said, he proves his
weakness in controversy, and not mine. Let him
adapt his cheap sneers to his own unhappy predi-
cament.
[Again, Vociferation comes to aid Misrepresentation.
To reiterate is tiresome, but necessary. The actual state-
ment wasiß.P., Oct. 23): * The average Scotchman does
not know how good or how bad Burns’s verses are, and
he does not know how much Burns plagiarised.’ Instead
of quoting that, G.B. preferred to attribute this (R.P.,
Feb. 5): ‘Condensed, your arguments were these: (1)
That Burns, for his own uses, stole the ideas, and even
words, of preoeding writers ; (2) ThXt his countrymen
are wholly unaware Of this alleged fact.’ That is (it
was rejoined), what is said of a particular class of
Scotchmen G.B. applies to the whole body: a quali-
fied allegation of ignorance he perverts into a charge
of complete ignorance. G.B. shirks the second half
of the rejoinder, and defends the first half by quot-
ing the third sentence preceding the relevant sentence,
which has no bearing on that sentence’s particular
statement. Once more, his method is characteristic.
The expression ‘ the average Scotchman ' ohviously ex-
cludes the Seotohman who is not average ; hilt by mis-
quotation G.B. makes argumentative capital from
suppression of the here implied and admitted fact that
the uot-average Seotohman does know something of
Burns’s plagiarisms. And, instead of admitting his error,
he dubs himself dullard or worse by repeating it.]
I have made a random guess that probably but
one-third of the published editions of Burns con-
tained ‘copious notes.’ Your reviewer quibbles
therefrom that the alleged ignorance of the
average Scot of Burns’ alleged plagiarisms is due
to the noteless two-thirds. This is lovely, coming
from one who throughout eight columns revelled
[There were not ‘eight columns,’ but only ‘eight
columns’ less a substantial body of advertisements; and
the alleged ‘ revel ’ certainly did not extend throughout
the not-eight columns, of which a large part was occupied
in quotation. The point is unimportant; but. it serves to
show that. G. 8., who exacts such accuracy from others,
cannot himself be accurate even in small tilings. And
since he will hang himself, he shall have not only his own
rope, but also his own pack-tiiread.]
in the idea that Henley’s edition —published at
two guineas net—would constitute a revelation to
the Scottish nation and would obliterate ‘The
conception of Burns which is legendary and
Scottish.’ Ha so gloated over the disillusionment
this fatuous race was about to experience, that he
said , ‘ One can conceive the horror of the foolish
people who have transformed Burns into a sort of
Scottish Mtunbo-Jumbo.’ Therein, he betrays
his belief that the ‘foolish people’—foolish because
they admire Burns —who were inaccessible to
former annotated editions from 50 to 100 in num-
ber, were suddenly to be shown the error of their
ways by Henley at forty-two shillings per shook !
By the way, what about my misrepresentations
as to the scope of the term ‘ average Scotchman ’
now ?
[More Misrepresentation. The suggestion regarding
* the horror of the foolish people ’ (1t.1’., Dec. 4) specifi-
cally referred to Henley’s essay on Burns, wbicli was cer-
tainly inaccessible ‘ in former annotated editions from 50
to 100 in number.’ So Q.B.'s perversion is once more
pointless—and his gibe.]
But your reviewer excels himself in one state-
ment whose slap-dash audacity seems remarkable
even when its source is remembered. He boldly
says—
*So far as their borrowings are concerned,
Burns, for the most part,' gilded refined gold ;
Shakespeare made gold out of lead.’
Such ravings might convince the credulous or
delude the ignorant; they cannot impose on those
accustomed to weigh words. In Burns, the use of
‘ a lyrical idea, that is a rhythm, a burden, a
drift,’ already utilised, constitutes ravishing,
filching, clean-stealing, and a gilding of refined
gold. Shakespeare gets different treatment.
When lie lifts, as he does almost without excep-
tion, his Plot, Situations, Characters, Colors and
Atmosphere, from the Gone-Before, lie does not
filch ! Oil, no; he only turns lead into gold. The
[Really, G.B. is in a very elementary intellectual state.
Of course Shakespeare ‘filched’; it was never said or
implied he did not; the statement that he turned lead
into gold implied that he did. But while Shakespeare,
for tile most part, and so far as js known, filched quantity,
the song Barns filched quality —and a considerable quan-
tity as well. That was the difference which was sought
to be expressed, and wliioh was expressed.]
unprejudiced, those qualified to judge, will agree
with me that the plays of a dramatist thus
equipped by his predecessors, are more than half-
written. But the alchemy which enabled Shake-
speare to turn lead into gold, did not end with his
borrowings of cruoibles for the melting and
moulds for the shaping of his precious ores.
What shall be said of Sir Know-All with his prate
about Shakespeare’s transmutation of the base into
the royal metal, if it be shown that the dramatist
not only stole his apparatus, but also two-thirds
of the golden coins he professedly minted. Un-
[G.B.’s instinct for Misrepresentation is ineradicable.
Shakespeare stole many coins ; but there is no evidence
to show that two-thirds of his golden coins were stolen.
What evidence there is goes to show that, if his silver and
copper were others’, his gold was mostly his own. Con-
trariwise, the song-Burns horrowed most of his gold from
others. One need not quarrel with G.B.’s previous meta-
phor of the circlet of jewels and the central Gem. But
one asks, with Henley, what would have been the worth
of the jewels without the Gem.]
fortunately for me, Boswell’s Shakespeare, with
Edmund Malone’s notes, is not accessible. So,
ignoring all Shakespeare’s eonveyings from the
Frenoh and Italian, let me quote Emerson, of
whom your Red Page may have heard —
Shakespeare esteemed the mass of old plays waste
stock, in which any experiment might be freely tried. . . .
He owed debts in all directions, and was able to use what
ever he found. The amount of his indebtedness may be
inferred from Malone's laborious computations in regard
to the First, Seoond and Third parts of Henry VI., in
which, says he, ‘ out of 6043 lines, 1771 were written by
some author preceding Shakespeare ; 2373 by him, on the
foundations laid by his predecessors, and 1899 were
entirely his own.’ Malone’s preceding investigations
hardly leave a single drama of Shakespeare’s absolute
invention.
[Unfortunately for G.B. ‘Henry Vl.’ is not one of the
plays for which we chiefly esteem Shakespeare.]
What of the lead now ? Aye, and what of the
gold ? Go to, thou vain man, and learn, again
from Emerson, that :
Great men are more distinguished by range and extent
than by originality. If we require in them the originality
which consists in weaving, like a spider, their web from
their own bowels; or in finding clay and making bricks,
and building the house ; no great men are original. The
greatest genius is the most indebted man.
This is Emerson’s apology for Shakespeare. It
With the exception of the final sentence, which is ques-
tionable, ‘Emerson’s apology for Shakespeare’ goes.
Thoughts can no more do without ancestors than bodies
can. In ourselves we assimilate and perpetuate our
parents. The trouble with Burns is that many of his
songs' not only assimilate and perpetuate the songs of his
predecessors, hut even swallow them, raw and holus-
bolus. Wherefore, aye and nay, also nevertheless and
notwithstanding, go to, thou vain G. 8., and learn that no
man may eat his father.
may serve in a lesser degree for Ben Jonsen, who
[As G.B. wrote ‘Jonsen’ in the copy and revised
‘Jonsen ’ in the proof, it is lawful to (sic) ‘ Jonsen ’ as an
admeasurement of the uneasy fit of G.B.’s plagiarised
erudition. If great men cannot be original they might
at least be unoriginal correctly.]
paraphrased ‘To Celia’ and * Drink to Me ’ from
the Epistles of Philostratus ; to Herrick, who also
borrowed from that Ancient; to Chaucer, whose
obligations to Boccaccio and the French are
obvious ; and every other English writer of emin-
ence. For who shall estimate what any modern
owes to the past? Literature is a mass of stored
impressions. The man whose impressions are all
self-gathered is not half-informed. The wise
glean experience side by side with their study of
the stores of the sages. Your hermit is the only
original—when he can’t read.
The writer on the Red Page cannot mitigate
his chastisement by quoting against me the fact
that lam Scotch. While lie fails to answer my
arguments that will not reduce his discomfiture by
one iota. But no Scot was ever less clannish than
I. No Burns-worshipper, I had mentally assessed
him in a much lower place than has Carlyle,
Matthew Arnold, Taine, Gosse, Henley,
Francis Thompson, and many others, superior in
weight and authority to my opponent. And so
[Then G. 8., in all probability, ‘assesses’ Burns in a
muoli lower place than he is entitled lo hold.]
little thought of clan or clique have I in this mat-
ter that, had Burns not been in hand, I would
have dulled a nib in defence of Tennyson. I hate
to see any man unfairly attacked, and particu-
larly one who cannot defend hitnself. Nor does
[As no dead man of letters can defend himself, it follows
that criticism of all dead men of letters (a considerable
number, at last accoun's) should stop short when G.B.
imputes unfairness. The idea seems swelled, somehow.
Also, query ; If the work of a dead man of letters can’t
defend itself, what exactly is a defender’s status ? Also,
another query (for next Sydney Univeisitymatriculation-
paper in Eng. Literature): ‘l. Contemplate by and large
the spectacle of g.b. defending BURNS, and state in as
decent language as you can what BURNS would be likely
to say about it.]
my Jekyll-llyde paragraph afford any proof
of your reviewer’s impartiality. Both sides of the
shi-ld were never shown. Jekyll devoted some
thirty or forty lines to out-of-place kindly refer-
ences to Burns. These, Hyde utterly contradicted
and completely overwhelmed, qualitatively and
quantitatively, throughout the remainder of the
eight columns. Jekyll’s remarks were but as
scanty specks of lustre on the rusty side of the
shield presented by Hyde.
But, let me again point out, it is not my com-
petence to judge which is in question—it is his.
He is charged with bearing false witness against
Robert Burns., A strong case has been made out:
let him clear hitnself—if he can !
[This is merely G.B.’s amusing way of putting things.
Another way : ‘G.B. is charged with bearing false witness
in favor of Kobert Burns. a strong qase has been made
out: let him clear himself-if he can !’]
Nor can the writer of the Red Page evade re-
proach by his specious plea that the meagre space
afforded by eight columns necessitated a recourse
to generalities which led to misconception as to
his true meaning. The writer who cannot mak
himself understood in 148 inches of solid matter
has mistaken his vocation. But, apart from that,
I ignored him in the general; I dealt with him in
the particular. For instance, I challenged him to
state why he charged Burns with stealing ‘The
Primrose,’ when the poet had expressly dis-
claimed its authorship. For instance, I chal-
[The original statement ran: ‘ln the same Clean-Steal
category’—of songs not written by Burns, yet neverthe-
less included in editions of his work—‘ are “ The Prim
rose ” (ravished from Thomas Carew—Burns himselt
calls it “ an old English song, which I dare say is hut
little known "—wherefore he ravished) . . .’ This
made it clear that Burns did not disguise his ravishing
from his editor ; but the poem as it appears in editions of

Somebody-else’s silver spoons have ho right to be

‘ claimed not the maker’s honors, hut was content with the

lenged his right to say that Burns stole
‘ Laddie Lie Near Me,’ when the poet had
expressly said, over his signature : ‘This song is
by Blaclclock.’ For instance, I demanded proof,
[lf Burns says so (G.B. gives no reference) Burns was
mistaken ; for Blackloek certainly did not write the

if Burns distinctly says the song is by somebody else,
why is it included in apparently all locally-availahle anno-
tated and unannotated editions of Burns on the same

Burns ‘stole ’ the song ; hilt that it came ‘iri the Clean-
Steal category’—which gives a very different implication
when taken in connection with the context, and with the
twice-repeated disclaimer (see above). If G.B. will have
‘ Blacklock’s ’ song taken out of ‘Songs by Robert
Burris ’ this little matter will square itself. While it re-
mains there his indignation is surely misplaced.]
or withdrawal, of the cruel calumny chat.'
* Burns’s friends and relatives, with intent to
heighten his reputation, deliberately destroyed
many of the old ballads from which he borrowed.’
This is so far from being true that Mrs. Burns
actually disclaimed her husband’s authorship of
certain songs, and Gilbert Burns, his brother, dis-
owned the following—‘ As I was a Wandering
‘ The Winter It is Past; ’ ‘A Bottle and a
Friend ;’ ‘The Battle of Sheriff-Muir ; ’ and
[‘ln our Notes to individual songs, we have done our
best to trice his ’—Burns’s— ‘ connexion with the past.
This is now the more difficult because (I) much of the
material he colleoted (including the origins of the publi-
cation known as The Merry Muses and a good deal else)
has been destroyed—by his relations, or by Currie, or by
later owners—in the interests partly of mords,
partly of that cheap decorous chromo-lithograph (ns it
were) which bids fair to supplant the. true Burns—ardent,
impulsive, generous ; but hypochondriacal, passionate,

others. On such definite ground Red Page will
not meet me ; he is safer in the regions of blathe”
and bluster.
[ln his chosen instances G.B, has been met; and he can
he met even more decisively in every other instance.
But what is the use of wearying readers? The contro-
versy about. Burns has degenerated into a controv- rsy
about the controversy—‘and thank goodness,’as W. S;
Gilbert sings, ‘they’re both of them over.’ G.B. may
take the last word.]
Let me again conclude with a quotation* this
time from Charles Whibley—
Burns, who rescued half-a-doz°n measures from unde-
served obscurity, is one nf the few singers that the wnrld
has known. ... To say that he was a plagiarist is to
misunderstand his method. . . . He laid his hand upon
whatever mateiial he found suitable, but he himself was
the first to declare liis indebtedness, and the genius which
transformed a hasty sketch into a marvel of music was
all his own. In undertatinr, therefore, to discover the
origins of Burns, Messrs. Henley and Henderson have
but completed the task indicated bjf himself.

J "tion in the throat, and giving strength to the voice, and
it never allows a cough or asthma to become chronic. No
house should be without it, as, taken at the beginning, a
dose is generally sufficient, and a complete cure is certain.
“ Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir, —I had a bad attack
of Bronchitis for six months, with dreadful hacking cough
day and night and great quantity of phlegm. Night
sweats came on and pains in my chest, and I got so weak
that I could not leave my bed. At this stage I was pro-
nounced to be in consumption. I had a lot of medicines

reading in the Age of your ‘Bronchitis Cure,’ I got a
bottle, and after taking two doses I was much improved.
After taking two bottles the cough and night sweats left
me, my appetite returned,- and I was able to get up; and
am now in good health, for which I am truly grateful, and
shall recommend your Medicine to all my friends.—Your;
truly, E. SMITH, Halpin Park, Nhill, Victoria.”

Various Verses,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“EDUCATION.”
The infant soul lies in its shell,
Unknowing all and all unknown,
And, ere it wakes, a Fetish fell
Has round it cords of bondage thrown.
Through childish dawn and youthful morn
Mid-day of Manhood blossoms out,
Its Sun of all its glory shorn —
Befogged by sombre clouds of Doubt.
So in the darkling afternoon,
When Joy of Life should swell to prime,
The man can only false creeds croon,
Or soothe his soul with lies sublime.
And senile night, Death’s peaceful porch,
Grows hideous with silhouettes
Cast by Religion’s mocking torch
Upon a scene of vain regrets.
P.M.
TO A LADY.
The Devil once wrought out a woman, the best
Ever fashioned below —with the subtlest of
touches
He modelled her form ; and with infinite jest
He dowered her with gifts would have suited a
duchess,
Could the germ of a duchess (O mad supposition !)
Be ever prepared in so strange a position 1
He finished her lips ; and he cackled with glee,
So pleasing her lips were on closest inspection.
He spent one whole month on an ankle ; a knee,
With a dimple bewitching, was “ simply per-
fection.”
And her hand! —I must say as a chronicler
dutiful,
That the gods never fashioned a member more
beautiful.
And he chafed, and he sweated with troublesome
thought,
Ere he settled the pigments which colored her
iris;
And for her eyes’ bright’ning a live coal he brought
From Phryne’s apartment, where hell’s hottest
fire is ;
And he quivered with Let this strange fact be
delivered :
It was not at all cold, yet I’m sure that he
shivered !
And the curves of her bosom were such as might
drive
An anchorite daft. Let the catalogue end. as
To name all her beauties I ne’er could contrive.
Her like is held over—ad Orcecas Kalendas.
Then with life and the joy of it quick he inflamed
her,
And, for want of a sweeter name, Inez he named
her.
And the parts that she plays on the earth might
be told
In a latter-day epic—most curious reading !
She owns two divinities, Passion and Gold ;
Well-taught by her master she follows his lead-
ing— . .
For the Artist who planned every exquisite part
of her
Maliciously, wantonly left out the heart of her.
FRANK MORTON.
LAY OF THE PRACTICAL MAID.
“ The most serious want in the character of the Aus-
tralian youth is a lack of ideality and imagination.
Especially is this observable in the young women of our
country.” —Brisbane Weekly.
A graceful youth was he,
So straight and tall and slim;
A dainty maiden she
Who only lived for him
And Love,
Who only lived for him.
His brow was once so glad,
Her smile was once so sweet;
But late so sad, so passing sad,
They gaze whene’er they meet
So sad,
They gaze whene’er they meet.
“ O dearest! ” quoth the swain,
“ A hapless pair are we —
Our parents say we two are twain,
And twain shall ever be,
My Love,
And twain shall ever be.
Come, place thy hand in mine,
We’ll wander side by side ;
We’ll seek old Ocean’s bitter brine
And plunge beneath the tide,
My Love,
And plunge beneath the tide.
Then spake the maiden low
All in the gloaming dim,
“I would, my dear, but then, you know,
I really cannot swim,
My Love,
I really cannot swim.”
The sky grew black as pitch,
The clouds did lower and loom ;
He gave his pants a mournful hitch
And all again was gloom,
Ye ghouls,
And all again was gloom 1

P. LUFTIG.
Another tight place. A 50-ton slab of
rock fell from the roof of a working in the British
Broken Hill mine the other day. It killed four
men ; but a fifth was doubled up and so fitted into
a crevice in the fallen mass, while a 10 x lOin.
piece of timber, projecting from under the stone
at that point, allowed air to reach him until, with
the help of screwjacks, the burden was taken off
and the prisoner was liberated,
Ed. Bulletin. —You say Yic. Govt, has
suddenly discovered that Lake Tyrrell is lower
than the Murray, and is proposing to fill it.
Now, as Lake Tyrrell is one of the greatest salt-
beds in Australia —thousands of tons of salt can
be had for the shovelling—what will be the use of
the water if it is filled? In Feb. and March the
lake is a sight worth seeing, the salt glistening
like snow, while any shrubs that grow there are
covered with salt after the style of alum-work. —

SATURDAY, MARCH 26, 1898.
The Man who Owns Victoria.
The Federal Convention has just finished its
draft Constitution, and the first voice of loud
dissent comes from Victoria. The Age, if
its utterances of last week are a reliable
guide, is against the proposed Constitution,
root and branch, and the Age, to a great
extent, runs its little corner of the continent.
David Syme wipes out old Ministries and
orders new ones. The long, wordy Shiels used
to slither round to his office for instructions.
The ukase that Speight was to go came from
the same building. So did the command
that the country should have no more of
Shambles Patterson and his Ministry of
weeds tottering in the wind. The order
that the Turner Government is to “go
it ” is issued from there also, and as all the
other commands have come to pass, the
chances are that this one will also, and that
Turner and co. will go it. And David
Syme, the old rich autocrat of Victoria, is
obviously thinking of issuing his order that
Victoria is to stand out of the Federation. If
he does the probabilities are that Victoria
will stand out, and that future generations
will curse their servile ancestors, and their
ancestors’ tin-god, Syme, with such a curse
as has not been known this generation.
* * * •
The four principal reasons why, according
to the great boss organ of Melbourne, Vic-
toria can't afford to adopt the proposed
Federal Constitution are—
1. The Victorian Railway Ruin.
2. The final denial of State Bounties.
3. The utter confusion wrought in the State finances.
4. The final voice and veto in money powers given to
the Senate.
In these respects the voice of the Daily
Syme js -raised to point out that Victoria has
been especially conspired against by the
other provinces, and that they are bent on
the ruin and downfall of that small angular
corner of the land. In which respect the
Age is very like the Little Cornstalk Party
in New South Wales, which holds that New
South Wales is to be ruined by a malignant
combination of the other States. It is also
very like Minister Throssell, of Westralia,
who laments that Federation on the system
proposed will lead to the utter blotting-out
of agricultural progress in Sandgroper Land.
And, later on, when the parochial parties in
Tasmania and South Australia have time to
assert themselves, and to decide that the
whole Federal movement is a conspiracy
against their little patch of dust, the Age
will be very like them also. Also, it is just
like Brisbane Telegraph, which has some-
how discovered that the whole purpose of
Federation is to somehow enable Sydney
and Melbourne co annex the Brisbane trade,
and make the City of Flies and Floods a
ruin.
• * * *'
The “railways ruin,” even according to
the Age itself, proves to be a very small
affair. Under the proposed Constitution it
is probable that New South Wales will be
allowed, under the “development” clause,
to give specially low rates to attract the
trade—mostly wool—of the outlying parts of
its own territory to Sydney. Victoria, on
the other hand, probably won’t be allowed
to quote special rates to attract the trade of
southern and western New South Wales to
Melbourne. It is reckoned that this will
mean a loss of £40,000 a year in railway
freights, and £160,000 a year in trade profits
at Melbourne— or £200,000 a year in all.
But the freights aforesaid are only gained by
carrying wool at from 38 to 66 per cent,
below the regular rates, and if the avail-
able evidence goes for anything the trans-
action is a dead loss. According to the
returns for 1895—the latest which the writer
has before him—for every £25 earned by
the Victorian railways £l5 went in work-
ing expenses. Therefore it would seem
that the freight which is carried at 40 per
cent, reduction just about pays expenses,
and the trade at 66 per cent, reduction is a
disaster. Anyhow, both this item and the
alleged £160,000 are mere speculation. Even
if there was a loss of £200,000 a year it
would only be a speck upon the aggregate
national receipts. The private incomes on
which income-tax is payable amount to some-
thing like £9,000,000 a year, and reckoning
the great multitude of small incomes as well,
the total probably reaches £20,000,000. The
£200,000 a year profit said to be made by
carrying Cornstalk wool at a heavy loss isn’t
much by comparison.
* « * *
The Constitution prohibits the States from
offering bounties in aid of local industries—
this right is reserved solely to the Federal
Government. Therefore, Victoria will stand
in exactly the same position as the other
provinces, and why it shouldn’t stand in the
same position as the other provinces no one
knows. The fact that separate States are
not allowed to give bounties without the
permission of the Federal Legislature is just
as much a grievance and a disaster as’the
fact that the county of Bogong or Tambo or
Benambra has never had the power to do so
without the permission of the State Legisla-
ture. As regards the “ confusion wrought in
the State finances,” no one really knows yet
whether they will be confused or not. It is
provided that the Commonwealth may retain
one-fourth of the Customs and Excise
revenue to cover the Federal expenditure,
and to make good losses on postal, telegraph
and other Federal services. The balance is
to be handed back to the States, so if the
Federal Government requires £2,000,000
Customs revenue a year to pay for its own
pomp and glory, and to cover posts, tele-
graphs, military and naval expenditure, cost
of mints, lighthouses, quarantine stations,
and dozens of other things, it will have to
provide £6,000,000 a year to divide among
the provinces. This is some kind of
security, at all events. The next security is
that the States will probably not be foolish
enough to elect Federal delegates who will
do things to ruin their finances any more
than they would elect local members of the
same deranged variety. There is, of course,
no guarantee that the representatives which
Victoria, S.A., Tasmania, and the other
provinces send to the National Legislature
won’t do things with the finances that will
leave these provinces insolvent and gener-
ally upside - down. There is also no
guarantee, and there never has been any,
that the representatives they send to their
local Legislatures will not act likewise, so
things will simply be as they were, and as
they always have been, and as they always
will be. The only remedy is to insert a
clause in the Constitution that no politician
shall be a darned fool-, and no way of en-
forcing such a clause has yet been discovered.
* * * *
The strangest feature in the case, however,
is that a Victorian—any kind of a Victorian
—should call on his province to stand out of
Federation because of the alleged “final”
veto of the Federal Senate over money bills.
The Victorian House of 48 Rich Landlords
also has a veto over money bills, and if Vic-
toria doesn’t federate that part of the veto
over its cash which the Federal Senate would
hold will continue to be held by the 48 Rich
Landlords as heretofore. If the Federal
Senate refuses to give way over a money bill,
it may be squashed by the vote of the Lower
House at a joint sitting, so its veto isn’t
final. When the Victorian Upper House
absolutely refuses to give way over a money
bill the thing ends there ; there is no legal
process on earth which can compel it to shift
from that position. If Victoria stands out
of the Federation because the Federal Upper
House would have partial authority to
override the more popular Chamber on
money matters, then the alternative is to
remain wholly under an Upper House which
has entire authority to do the same thing,
and which has been doing it ever since Vic-
toria began.
* m * *
The cheapest and easiest of all bricks to
heave at the Federal Constitution is the
statement—which forms, practically, the
whole of the Age’s case— that, if Australia
federates, no one knows what may happen.
No one does know. Also, no one knows
what may happen if Australia doesn’t
federate, so the same argument applies either
way. The best feature about the Constitu-
tion is that it contains at least a reasonably
workable provision for its own amendment
when amendment is found necessary—as it
certainly will be. But the fact that amend-
ment will certainly be wanted is no reason
why the Draft Constitution should not be
accepted to begin with. If the provinces held
Conventions for 40,000 years, and then feder-
ated with all the light of 40,000 years hard
consideration brought to bear on the Constitu-
tion, experience would promptly show an
urgent necessity for amendment just the
same. Every provincial Constitution in
Australia has been desperately in want of
amendment since it started, and is still in
want of it. And, whatever may be wrong
with the Federal Constitution, it certainly
looks better than any provincial Constitution
now existing. The partial veto on money
matters proposed to be entrusted to the
Federal Upper House may be an evil, but,
as every Provincial Upper House has a
whole veto, and has always had it, this at
least makes things a little better than they
are. It is no doubt a grievance with each
province that it isn’t sure of having the
Federal capital within its borders, but, if
Federation is rejected on that account, each
province will still be left without the Federal
capital, for the sufficient reason that there
won’t be any. If the Federal inter-State
Commission prevents Victoria carrying
Riverina wool to Melbourne at ruinously low
rates, it, at least, can never alter the fact
that Melbourne is the nearest port, and that
the trade will always have a natural tendency
to go there. And the possibilities of finan-
cial disorganisation can never be either more
or less than they are now ; the Federal
legislators will disorganise the finances if the
people elect legislators of the disorganising
kind, and that is just what is the matter
with things now. But, in addition to these
negative virtues, the Federal Constitution
provides a good possibility of ultimately
making a uniform national railway system
under one management ; of consolidating
the public debts ; of making a nation with a
national spirit, a national political centre,
and a national system of defence ; so that it
promises in some respects to make things a
great deal better, while in others it can’t
make them any worse. And, if it provides
Victoria with a share in a political system
which isn’t wffiolly bossed by David Syme,
that may not be an unmixed calamity.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Railways and Industries.
Last w'eek a new scrap of railway was opened
at Condobolin, and Young, of the N.S.W.
Dog-fearing Ministry, orated considerably
about the advantages of things in general under
the Joliyandfat regime :
At the end of 1897 there were 26391 miles of railway
under the control of the Commissioners. The total
capital expended in construction was 1 37,369,205, and the
total earnings per annum were £3,004.742. After all the
working expenses for the year had been paid there was a
balance of £1,413,524, a percentage of profit of £3 15s. lid.
They could borrow money now at a fraction over 3 per
cent. It was evident that the railways of the colony had
got beyond the non-productive stage. Nearly 40 millions
of money which had been expended by the people was
recouped by those who used and were benefited by the
lines.
Mr. J. H. Young addressed a large crowd from the
verandah of the Royal Hotel in the evening. Alderman
Tasker, the Mayor, was in the chair. He said that . . .
there were now more people employed in the many differ-
ent industries than ever before, and he believed that the
Government would come back after the election stronger
than ever.
The Bulletin respectfully asks Minister
Young to drop that old, stupid, stereotyped
lie about the paying condition of N.S.W. rail-
ways. It is a lie which every Ministry seems
to inherit from its predecessors, and it is time
it was exploded. The alleged “ profit ” on
N.S.W. railways, according to the Audi-
tor-General, who is the last court of
appeal in financial matters, is simply arrived
at by not allowing for a large number of ex-
penses, and anything could be made to show a
profit the same way. The Auditor-General’s
figures are only published up to 30th June,
1896, but that year is as fair a criterion as any
other. The Railway Commissioners and the
State statistician claimed that the railways
then yielded .£1,268,529 above working ex-
penses and thus paid .£3 Bs. lOd. per cent, on
their cost. The Auditor-General showed the
net revenue as only £1,154,703, or about
£3 2s. 9d. per cent, on cost—an awful discre-
pancy of £114,000. He showed the one year’s
deficit as being £321,690, and the accumulated
loss as £7,735,212. The N.S.W. railway
system is still a non-productive concern and
has never been otherwise. Also, the fact that
money can be borrowed now at 3 per cent,
doesn’t make any difference in the fact that
the money with which the railways were
built is borrowed at about 3| per cent.
The dismal fake about the productive con-
dition of the N.S.W. lines was formerly
only a harmless delusion. Now it is a public
disaster. One notable reason why the railways
are not to be put under Federal management
was the opposition of N.S.W., and a great part
of the N.S.W. opposition arose from the stupid
Ananias, which Minister Young still circulates,
„that the Cornstalk system is paying its way,
and therefore the province would lose heavily
by pooling its lines with those of adjacent pro-
vinces which don’t pay. The railway delusion
is thus a national question—the other dismal
whopper about more people being now employed
in industries than at any previous period is
purely local. A certain aspect of probability
is lent to this latter tale by the circum-
stance that a factory now includes* places
employing as few as four hands, whereas
the minimum used to be five, and thus a
great many places which were legally
“ factories ” in 1896 and afterwards were not
legally factories in 1895 or in any previous year,
and were not included among the industries
about which statistics are available. But the
State Statistician’s latest returns,,\vhen placed
on the old basis (ms shown on page 485 of the
Statistical Register) show 45,222 employes
in ’96 against 46,135 in ’9l, and 45,564 in ’B9.
Therefore, The Bulletin estimates Minister
Young as an ignorant person talking at large,
or else a rather unscrupulous individual who
doesn’t much care what he says so long as he
helps along the Calico Jimmy Government,
which supplies him with a billet.
The Melbourne Tramway Grind.
After a slow and gradual fashion the Victorian
authorities are holding an inquiry into the
Melbourne trams. Melbourne possesses a more
complete and efficient tram service than any
other Australian city, and its system is only
excelled by, at most, one or two cities in the
world. It is swift, marvellously cheap, and
spreads to almost every remote corner of the
city. And it is just beginning to dawn on the
local public that the cheapness is probably
gained by an awful grinding of human flesh and
bone. Up to a certain point low prices may be
secured simply by good management and
a clever application of machinery, but, when
everything has been done in this direction, and
more cheapness or bigger profits are still
wanted, then sweating has to be resorted to.;
There is nothing else to resort to. The inquiry
isn’t yet far advanced, but the evidence already
evoked is startling. One witness, who had
worked on cable-trams in the U.S., said that
the Melbourne Tramway Co. paid lower wages
for longer hours and harder grind than any
other tram co. that he knew of in the world.
Stories were told of men who worked 84 hours
a week for 355., with variations up to 100 hours
a week on special occasions. Conductors and
gripmen belong to no recognised trade,
and have no trades-union behind them,
and every attempt to form a union of
their own has resulted in the men who
were conspicuous in the matter being “not
wanted any longer.” The “ spotter ” appears
to be an institution of great weight in the Mel-
bourne tram service, and, as a “spotter” is
paid to discover wrong-doings on the part of
the employes, and the one who doesn’t discover
things is likely to be set down as a very bad
“ spotter,” and to lose his billet, there seems
some reason in the assertion of the men that the
co.’s hired spies discover a good many things
that do not happen. The Orange Lodge ele-
ment is also strong, as it is in every other
institutioninsnufflesomeMelbourne. Oneex-em-
ploy6 claims to have been told that he would
“ never get any good out of the service until he
joined a certain official’s lodge.” He refused to
join, and thereafter things were discovered
about him continuously till he was shunted
out. Another ex-employ 4, whom the manager
admitted to have a perfectly clean record,
summed up the tram-service generally as the
“ worst job ever created.” The whole story, so
far as it has been told, is a tale of tremendously
long hours, miserable pay, petty tyranny,
and the life of a cheap dog generally.
One feature of the case which appeals
to the public pocket is this : The trams
compete with the suburban State rail-
way system. By a process of awful grind
they save so much in expenses that they can
afford to carry passengers very cheaply. The
State has to give its employes decent hours and
decent wages, and at the same time it has to
keep its suburban fares down to the tram level
or lose all the business. Therefore, many of
the suburban railways don’t pay, and the
country has to make up the loss—of which loss
Sprigg’s bloated corporation is, to a consider-
able extent, the indirect cause.

Concerning a <£lo Note.
A tALE of a now going around Sydney
is one of the many things which seem to call
for official explanation. The recipient—so
called because he didn’t get the money—was
laid up by an accident in Little Bay Hospital,
and afterwards in Rookwood Destitute Asy-
lum. He left nearly penniless, and dressed
only in a few rags. After his departure he
learned that a brother in Scotland had sent him
<£lo while at the asylum, and as the letter and
the money had never reached him he proceeded
to enquire. He continued inquiring, and hang-
ing about public offices, and asking explana-
tions from public officials, for many days',
and at last learned that the coin had been
officially intercepted, as explained in the follow-
ing letter: —
January 24, 1898.
Office of Director of Government Asylums, etc.
To
I have to inform you, in reference to your application of
the 11th inst., that in view of the fact that your main-
tenance cost to the Government exceeds the amount
(£10) forwarded by your brother, no refund can be made
to you. The sum due amounts to t 9 for treatment in
Little Bay Hospital, and £4 11s. for your maintenance in
Rookwood Asylum, you will therefore observe that you
are still indebted to the Government in the sum of e3los.
(Signed) E. HANSON, Acting Director.
This looks like one of the most bowelless acts
of routine going. Undoubtedly a man who
is in a position to pay for his maintenance in a
State institution should pay for it, but when a
sick man has only ,£lO between him and want
he can’t fairly be classed as a person who is in
a position to pay. Moreover, the money was
not strictly his ; it was some one else’s money,
and the sender certainly intended it to help his
brother out of a very tight place—not- to pay
the Government for his maintenance. In
fact, it was morally, if not legally, a
trust fund which the State has annexed-


And it would be interesting to know whether
the money was addressed to the asylum
officials, or whether it was addressed to the
patient and simply intercepted. Anyhow, it
appears that the patient wasn’t informed that
the money had arrived, and had been seized
upon to pay his keep ; it seems rather that
it was grabbed surreptitiously, and the ex-
planation was only supplied when the recipient
—so-called because he didn’t get the money—-
began to clamor horribly at the outer gate.
The ,£lO will no doubt help to reduce Treasurer
Reid’s next deficit—still this aggressive seizure
of a man’s little donation to bis poor sick
brother looks a dreadfully mean way of reduc-
ing a deficit.
Crank and Cat.
At Melb. City Court, lately, one Henry,
charged with utterly motiveless indecency in a
railway-carriage, was shown to have been twice
convicted before on similar charges, and to have
served two years, with a whipping, in ’93. The
P.M. said that the bench was satisfied to find
its judgment borne out by prisoner’s previous
character, and had no hesitation in imposing a
severe sentence. Accused would be gaoled for
two years, and receive 12 lashes. Mr. Kane
pointed out that accused was a man of
emaciated frame, who would not be able to
endure the cat, and that he must be, in any
ease, something of a lunatic ; whereupon the
bench loftily averred that it was “ the best
judge of that.” It was the law of the land, and
the “ magistrates were there to administer it.”
It would never, of course, occur to a lot of cow-
wilted jaypees, abjectly submissive to a wooden-
souled autocrat of a P.M., that the two previous
convictions of the gaunt and miserable Henry
for utterly purposeless indecency not only
furnished reasonable evidence of his insanity in
one direction, but demonstrated also the utter
incompetence of such punishment, as he was
now for the second time to undergo, to deal
with such an unnatural idiosyncracy as his. It
was long a fiction of the raw-meat advocate
that a man once flogged never returned to the
triangles, but when a half-demented weakling
like Henry will chance a second scarifying
merely for a momentary gratification of his dis-
torted sexuality, it is evident that the cat is as
much a failure as an ameliorator of dementia as
it is in its other alleged capacity as a
deterrent from crime. Modern civilisation
exclaims with horror against the good old
practice of flogging lunatics at the cart’s tail by
way of curing them, yet in the same breath it
orders many of its own cranks to be gaoled and
flayed, and then turned loose again to earn
more gaol and more cat by acts for which they
are apparently no more responsible than the
average jaypee is for having been born blatant
and bull-headed.
More “ Tattersall.”
The Bulletin will be glad to have the follow-
ing statement contradicted by the parties on
whom it reflects. After a certain horse had
won a big handicap, the people who drew it in
“ Tatt’s ” No. 1 sweep received a letter from a
man professing to write on behalf of a syndi-
cate of five who drew the winner in No. 2
sweep, which only half-filled. The writer
made an appeal to the No. 1 syndicators to pay
a share of £750, said to have been extracted
from the No. 2 quintette under these alleged
circumstances :
Reliable information was obtained on the evening
before the race that was to be scratched, and saved
for the handicap, owing' to the fact that the holder
in No. 1 sweep had not approached the owner. . . . See-
ing the danger, we at once sought the owner, but an
interview was only obtained by travelling to Sydney by
the 5 o’clock train on the morning of the race, and it was
only by laying him £750 to nil that a start was arranged.
. . . From this you can see that owing to the energetic
manner in which we worked, and by offering a
price that secured a start, you won £4500 clear,
which, had you been available to the owner, would
have been reduced one - third, as that is the
amount you would have had to lay to secure a run. Had
we not moved in the matter you would have divided a
few pounds of “ non-starters’ ” money instead of the nice
little plum you receive. These are the plain, ungarnished
facts of the case, which either owner or Mr. Adams of
Tattersall’s can confirm.
This letter, which is in The Bulletin’s pos-
session, professes to be an attempt by a
“ squeezed ” drawer of a winning ticket to get
a partial refund of the money squeezed out of
him from the holders of a more valuable ticket
who hadn’t been “available to the owner.” It
is well for the public to know the taxable
character of prizes in “Tatt.’s” sweeps.
In this case it is alleged that the oilier reason
why the horse in question wouldn’t start unless
the owner was laid a large sum to nothing was
that he had another horse in the race, and the
drawer of that horse had laid him a large sum
to nothing. Therefore —so it is alleged—the
writer of the letter had to lay a larger sum to
nothing to get a show. Thus things get back
to the old place—the whole trouble arises be-
cause “ Tattersall” draws his sweeps before the
race instead of on the day of the race. If they
were drawn on the day of the race the owner
of the horse couldn’t “approach” the drawer,
nor could the drawer of one horse “ approach ”
the owner, and thereby make it necessary for
the drawer of another horse in the same stable
to “approach” him still more. “Tatter-
sail’s” alleged great reason for drawing
sweeps before the race is to give winners a
chance to “ lay off.” But there is nothing in
his contract about the winner having a chance
to lay off. The contract simply is that the
winner shall get his money less 10 per cent.,
and, as the result of drawing the sweeps before
the day of the race, he very often doesn’t. Why
should “ Tattersall ” be so anxious to keep the
promise he doesn't make that the promise he
does make must be sacrificed to do it ?
Sacerdotal Savagery.
The refusal of Rookwood (Sydney) cemetery-
authorii ies to allow the interment of the homi-
cide Cusack in “ consecrated ” ground was
probably merely in accordance with standing
regulations. But that such regulations should,
in these days exist at all is evidence enough
that our “ advanced civilisation ” is-still bill a
tliin veneer upon the superstitious savagery of
primal man, as well as a proof that the wide
charity of Christ has practically nothing to do
with any of the Churches which masquerade
under His name. And, as for the man-
slayer, it is at once pitiable and ludicrous to
recollect that, whereas the professional and
deliberate taker of human life upon a gigantic
scale will be marshalled to his marble tomb
with pomp and circumstance and full priestly
rite, the—probably insane—doer of a solitary
frenzied act of bloodshed must not even—
as in Cusack’s case—be furtively hurried into
his dishonored hole within the ordinary hours
of burial. A miserably humiliating mind-
picture to him who has hopes of the intellectual
emancipation of mankind is that thrice-banned
corner of Rookwood cemetery in which lie the
murderer and the suicide, with between them
and hallowed ground is the no-man’s-land of
abject poverty the criminals we know of
fenced off from the criminals we don't know by
the unregarded bones of the pauper !

One other expedient for raising an 80,000 —or
higher—vote in favor of Federation in N.S.W.
at a period outside the general election is to
make the vote compulsory. A bill providing
for this should meet with no opposition from
those who say the country is against Federa-
tion. A compulsory vote will show clearly
where each party stands, and will render
nugatory the scheme which now makes 80,000
affirmative votes, without any regard to the
negative ones, a sine qua non. Compulsory
voting has been tried with success at several
epochs. Virginia (U.S.A.) fined non-voters for
many years. With 280,000, or thereabouts,
enrolled voters in N.S. Wales, a bill mulcting
in a reasonable fine all who neglect to east a
ballot on the Federation measure submitted
would elicit an answer as nearly national as can
be conceived.

“ Ponte ” discourses to The Bulletin on the
N.S.W. fish-question :
The only success of the trawling experiment lies in the
proof afforded that there are outside grounds capable
of being trawled. That it may be done profitably
is yet to be proved by experiment under practical
conditions—it cannot be shown by a gold-topped Govern-
ment steamer. Frank Farnell and his merry men
are still tickling Ocean’s depths with varying results.
Each dip is chronicled, and the capture carefully counted.
The number is recorded by the Executive officer in
Roman numerals in a brass-bound ledger, certified to by
the captain and initialled by the mate. The non-edible and
underweight fish are then hove overboard, leaving very
often more than enough for breakfast. Oil one memor-
able occasion several baskets were sent to Sydney
markets for sale, and the inspectors were instructed
not to prosecute where they w ere below' legal weight,
as the public wanted to buy small souvenirs of the ex-
periment. Frank went round later on to get his cheque
in order to refund to the Government the J6SOU granted,
and seemed surprised that there wasn’t more than
that amount. However, he has now’ undertaken to alter
everything, and put fish in the consumer s pan at 2d. a
lb. The fishermen average 35 per cent, of the retail price
at present —and starve. With the same proportion of 2d.,
what will they do? Still, it should be possible to place
fish before the public at a reasonable figure without
starving the men. Last week sea-mullet, averaging from
21b. to 31b. (first-class food, if cooked properly), were sold
at threepence a dozen, or ten pounds offish for a penny.
Later in the day, the same fish were hawked in the streets
aXfourpence and sixpence each. Under such circum-
stances I didn’t wonder, strolling along a fishing village
beach, to see bushels of mullet given to whomsoever
would take it. And yet the cry is raised that the fish
supply is inadequate—for where in Sydney can you buy
fish at less than sixpence per lb. ? A basket, of fish weighs
from 80 to 901 b.—in cleaning it loses 12J per cent.
“ Good ” fish can be bought all the year round at 10s. per
basket, and it costs 2s. for freight and charges—l2s. per
basket of 701 b. cleaned fish, for which the retailer
will get 35 shillings ! Shade of Moses ! What a profit !
The fisherman will jump at 10s. per basket (l£d.
per lb. !), for often they do not get ss. from
the Markets. Again, watch the guards and por-
ters play pitch and-toss with the baskets of fish,
and you will cease to wonder at the large percentage of
fish condemned. Why can’t the Railway Commissioners
put on refrigerating-cars on the fish-train? They pro-
mised years ago to have fish-trucks built —with tiers —-
but, so far, they are not. Fancy fish—the most perish-
able of all foods—piled basket on basket, with the slime
and blood and fly-filth oozing and dripping in a close,
unventilated car! It would decompose cast-iron ! If
Farnell wants to be canonised, let him resign command
of the Thetis and devise means of placing the fish in the
market in good condition, and find better methods of
distribution.
A significant Sydney dally paper item :
Members of the N.S.W. Legislative Assembly are said
to be anything but pleased with the Constitution as em-
bodied in the final draft of the Bill. One of them stated
yesterday that all the Labor-party were opposed to the
scheme, as it was not sufficiently democratic, and that
fully three-fourths of the 125 representatives in the
Assembly were out of sympathy with it. The principal
objection is stated to be against the provision providing
for equal representation of the States in the Senate,
which, it is contended, will be fatal to the interests of
N.S.W. A meeting of members of Parliament has been
called for 2.30 p.m. on Tuesday next at Parliament
Buildings. Mr. Haynes, who is an active promoter of the
opposition to the measure, states that steps are to be
taken to point out to the electors the conservative char-
acter of the bill, and the evil which it is believed will
follow the acceptance of the measure. Mr. Haynes feels
confident that the 80,000 votes in favor of the Constitu-
tion Bill will not be obtained.
Of course three-fourths of the M’sL.A. are nob
pleased— The Bulletin pointed out in its issue
of 20th November, 1897, under the head-
ing of “ The 46 Iscariots,” that they would
not be pleased, and the reason why. When
the Federal Parliament starts, the biggest
men will go there. The small men will be left
behind with diminished power, little dignity,
possibly with reduced salaries and perquisites,
in a mere provincial legislature, and those who
know that they must needs be left behind curse
at Federation—quite naturally. The Bulletin
doesn’t for a moment believe that it is the equal
State representation in Lhe Federal Senate which
is the trouble. In the first place, the joint sitting
in a ease of deadlocks gives the Lower House
power, in case of serious disagreements, to sup-
press the Senate. In the second place, the
Federal Senate, even without that, promises to
be far more representative than the unreformed
N.S.W. Council, or the reformed Council
which Reid proposes to substitute for it. See-
ing that Haynes and co. haven’t done one
serious kick against Reid’s monstrously Tory
scheme for “reforming” the N.S.W. Upper
House till it is just as bad as before, or worse,
their plaint about the undemocratic character
of the proposed Federal Senate is a shriek of
comedy.
About the biggest “contempt of court” The
Bulletin knows of attaches frequently to
N.S.W. law itself or to the administration of
the law. What a howling absurdity it was, for
instance, that, in order to award the wife-
shooter, Holmes, what is considered the ade-
quate punishment of three months’ gaol
about as much as would attach to the
stealing of a very lean chicken —it
should have been necessary gravely to
convict him of attempted murder and solemnly
sentence him to be hanged by the neck until he
was dead ! Also, as an instance of trying to
make the wheels work the other way, what
about the delegation of John Norton’s criminal
libel-trial to Judge Backhouse —a man who
might have to rely for early promotion to the
Supreme Court bench upon a Ministry which
was virtually Norton’s prosecutor ? As unfair
to Backhouse as it was to Norton. Melb.
Age enlarges upon this latter point in a vitriolic
leading article —an article of the sort which
you never, by any chance, see in any Sydney
daily paper. A brand of shame upon
the Sydney daily press is that it is, and ever
has been, prepared, in time of political
hatreds or social panic, to unprotestingly
tolerate, and even to encourage and applaud,
the grossest actions on the part of the courts
and of high-placed personages who set the
courts in motion. Many instances of this will
readily suggest themselves to anyone with a
memory. It should gall N.S. VY. citizens, who
yearn to see the administration of justice above
suspicion, to find that the only daily-newspaper
thunderblasts against such outrages as the first
Dean trial and the appointment of Judge Back-
house to hear the Norton case, come from the
Melbourne morning papers.

Two or three things are very conspicuous about
the Federal railway-rates squabble: The N. S. W.
Foreign-trade party is dead against the grant-
ing of bounties to aid any industry, yet it in-
sists on its right to grant a bounty to the back-
blocks wool-grower in the shape of a huge dis-
count off his railway freights. Said party
believes that trade should be allowed to flow
freely in its natural channels, and so on, but, if
the natural channel of the out-back wool-trade
is towards any port except Sydney, it will take
strong measures to prevent it going there.
And yet, by travelling with one foot on sea and
one foot on land, and trying to be consistent
and inconsistent at the same time, the Shoddy
party has all along failed in its purpose. It has
outraged its principles for years by granting
bounties, in the shape of ruinously-low
railway freights, to the far-out pastoralist
to force his wool to Sydney, with the result
that a lot of the wool goes to Melbourne
and Adelaide just the same, and N.S.W.
has lost about three-fourths of the far-
out wool-trade and has lost money on carrying
the other fourth. If it had outraged its prin-
ciples about an inch further by putting an
export duty of 3d. per lb. on wool crossing the
border overland it would have kept the trade,
and it could probably, by reason of the extra
traffic, have given the pastoralist the same
rates at a profit which it now gives him at a
loss. But, after banging its fiscal fetish hard
on one ear, the party lacked the courage to
wallop it on the other ear also. Yet if it had
aggressively gone the whole swine, its scheme
must have succeeded ; it is no good going only
half an apologetic swine under any circum-
stances.
New South Wales, owing to the increase from
50,000 to 80,000 of the minimum affirmative
vote required to carry Federation, is the pro-
vince where the National cause speins to have
the worst, or very nearly the worst, chance.
N.S. W. is the only province which has a general-
election on at the same time as the Federal
referendum. Therefore, the obvious way out
is to take the two votes together ; a general-
election, when public interest is at fever-heat
and the community is rolling up to the poll in
any case, is just the time when it would be pos-
sible to get 80,000 or 100.000 votes on the
Federal issue. Also, by taking tlie two votes
on the one day, the expense of a second election
would be saved. But I’rem'er Reid says re-
gretfully that he can’t take this obvious course
because he promised the Opposition that he
wouldn’t do so. This is a very touching epi-
sode, but, so far as can be discovered from
Hansard, Premier Reid didn't promise any-
thing of the kind. Certain members desired to
raise the minimum affirmative vote to 100,000,
and Reid said that, if it was raised to 100,000,
lie might feel called upon to take the Federal
poll along with the general-election so as to
have a chance of getting the required number
of suffrages. But he didn’t definitely promise
anywhere, so far as the writer can discover,
that if the minimum Federal vote was fixed at
80,000 he wouldn't have the two elections
together—the thing was only implied, at the
most. The spectacle of Premier Reid regret-
fully sacrificing Federation in order to keep
the promise that he never really made is a
very touching spectacle.
“In N.5.W.,” says ex-Premier Dibbs, “the
Federal Bill is dead.” And Dibbs ought to
know—he has himself been politically dead so
long that he should be a good judge of deadness.
It is a solemn affair, though, to see the political
corpses foregathering to decide a point like
this—the political skeletons sitting bleaching
on the fence, watching the live thing go by, and
telling each other that it is defunct. And it
sets one thinking of the last time Dibbs decided
that something was dead—it was Payment of
Members that time, and the alleged corpse rose
up and killed Dibbs. After all, it is just pos-
sible that the bones of ex-Republican D., as
they hang on the nail of neglect and rattle in
the cold wind of oblivion, may not be the best
possible authority on such a subject.
Open inquiries into the relations between
powerful organisations and their employes are
almost, without exception, farcical, when, so
far as the employes are concerned, they are
not tragical; and the current inquiry by a
Vic. select committee into the tramway men’s
grievances is not one of the exceptions. The
superlative of cool cheek was about reached
when tramway-boss Sprigg, who represents the
co., protested against any person discharged
from the service giving evidence—unless, in-
deed, F. Madden’s support of the contention
were not a little cooler. Anyhow, even sup-
posing the evidence could be gob, the com-
mittee is so frankly partisan that its re pork
will, in ordinary circumstances, be next to
valueless. Employers, in fact* ought to know
by now—and doubtless they do—that if they
want to justify their policy they can do it
most effectively and at the public expense by
goading some impulsive workmen’s representa-
tive into securing the appointment of a Royal
Commission or a select committee. By the way,
the steps that led to the appointment ofthe select
committee to enquire into the conditions of
labor in the Broken Hill mines have never yet
been disclosed, notwithstanding all the curiosity
that has been expressed.
Minister Young at Condobolin (N.S.W.)
Mr. Coghlan’s independent statistics' proved not only
that the number of manufactories had not decreased, but
that there had been an actual increase since the duties
were removed, both in the number of factories in opera-
tion and the number of persons employed. In the boot-
trade alone there were 20 more factories working- during
1896 than previously. (Applause.)
The Bulletin briefly replies that Minister Y.
is an ignorant and unscrupulous whatsisname
and a blatant thingumbob. The number of
boot-factories in N.S.W. is returned by Cogh-
lan as 68 in 1894, 66 in 1895, and 82 in 1896,
therefore, the increase, even on paper, isn’t 20,
or anything like it. Also, there is a footnote
to Coghlan’s table : “ For 1896 establishments
not using machinery have been accepted if em-
ploying not less than 4 hands ; prior to this
year the minimum was 5 hands.” In other
words, the apparent increase was 16 factories,
not 20, and it wasn’t a real increase ; the differ-
ence was simply that small factories which
existed for years previously were first included
in 1896. The 82 factories reckoned in 1896
employed 244 less hands than the 66 in 1895,
and the output w;t* 154,000 pairs fewer-.
Therefore, unless Minister Young has been
very badly reported, he is an even worse
thingumbob than this paper has already called
him.
N.S.W. Lands-Minister Carruthers thinks
it rough on a man who has had to look in at the
Insolvency Court that he should be barred from
political life. Said that he had even known a
Premier who had to invoke the aid of white-
wash. Downer, however, is of opinion that
until an insolvent gets his discharge he is a sort
of criminal in the eyes of the law. Then Reid
pointed out that there was nothing to bar a
criminal from a seat in the Commonwealth
Parliament, and a ray of sunshine illumined
some faces. Reid thought that if they were
too particular in this matter, it would be so
worked at elections that a man of eminence,
whose disappearance from public life was de :
sired, might be made insolvent as a political
rather than commercial necessity. However,
it was no go. Future candidates for Common-
wealth representation must have a clean sheet
of discharge.

Presumably from a woman comes the sugges-
tion- (in Sydney Sunday Times) that female
co-respondents in divorce cases should be no
more exempt from punishment than male
offenders. It is the “other woman,” asserts
this daring innovator, who is nearly always at
the root of the evil, and who ought, failing the
finding of security for good behavior for 12
months, to be gaoled for not less than that
period. The law, however, in the case of
adultery, apparently makes tacit assumption
that the man is the prime offender ; and, in any
case, there is another much more important
respect in which what is sauce for the gander
is apparently not sauce for the goose. Prompt
prosecution for perjury mostly awaits the man
who deliberately swears to what the jury, by
its verdict, afterwards declares to be false,
but when is any attempt made to punish the
woman (and she isn’t scarce) who, upon oath)
deliberately tries to fasten upon a man a crime
which the jury subsequently says he has not
committed? The celebrated Baron Penne-
father’s long experience taught him that 80
per cent, of women’s charges of sexual offence
against men were false—a natural result of the
practical immunity enjoyed by the female per-
jurer.
For Launceston (Tas.) institutions Ben had
lots of praise, until he poked his inquisitive
nose into the local benevolent asylum and had
the concern explained by the management.
Then he appears to have passed a sarcastic
remark about the wonderful economy displayed
by the authorities, and forthwith the news-
papers stated that Ben Tillett had expressed
admiration of the institution :
Mr. Ben Tillett. —Among the places visited by Mr.
Tillett since his arrival in the city was the Benevolent
Asylum. The visitor was shown through the buildings
and over the grounds, and expressed admiration at their
management. When informed what the Government
grant was, he was surprised, and said that he thought
the board of managers looked after the inmates exceed-
ingly well, and thoroughly studied the art of economy.
A few chunks from Ben’s disclaimer :
I think the institution a shameful one, and discreditable
to Tasmania.
The greatest meanness imaginable—much below the
worst of our home workhouses. Wretched accommoda-
tion -asphalt floors for old men . . . is inhuman, cal-
lousness even to-madness . . . barrack and prison-like
conditions, the dress, absence of any comfort and separa-
tion from their wives in old age, is heartless . .
God-forsaken hole you put your old pioneers in. . . .
The men and their wives who had little or no risks of
health or life to run, are able to end their days in fatted
idleness, as a reward for land-grabbing, while these poor
old veterans are imprisoned, and their caretaker turned
into a gaoler.
. . . The ration is a disgrace to civilisation . . . the.
cost runs to less than 3s. per head, exclusive of official
salaries. Old England allows more than that rate in the
poorhouse for official attendance to each pauper.
I hope that the mean creatures of officialdom may never
have to seek the cold comfort of your benevolent (?)
house.
Tillett not being a Tasmanian prophet, but.
an aggressive British person, his vigorous re-
marks have disturbed the slumber of those who
dwell ’neath the shadows of the System,- And,
as no Britisher has ever done the like before,
Tasmania may be expected to smash the silken
bonds at any minute.

Alexandra Dan. Barry’s Dramatic Co.
The above are the leading features of the
plot of “ Claudian,” the ancient Byzantine drama,
which is Wilson Barrett’s second effort at Her
Majesty’s. It is a very fine plot, which wouldn’t
drag so much if it were better worked out and
adorned with more incident; still, the general
effect is good, and the mounting is gorgeous in
the extreme. All the lavishness that the big stage
can well hold is spent upon it, and the pomp and
glitter of Claudian’s palace is something to be re-
garded with reverence. Wilson Barrett makes a
very brilliant Claudian in the early scenes, where
that wicked, bloated nobleman is bursting with
arrogance and scorn, and his angry scoff blows
the brown leaves rightover the border into Greece.
As a wicked nobleman with magnificent stout legs
he could hardly be improved upon. Later on he
is the repentant Roman who wants to do good but
dare not, because Holy Clement’s curse is upon
all his efforts, and he can’t even remonstrate with
a man for ill-using a horse without the animal
immediately bursting lengthways and perishing
in great agony. He is less satisfactory in
this character, yet, taking him all round,
Wilson Barrett is a fine elocutionary hero, and he
wears the brief white towel of the stage-Roman
with an imposing presence, and his big muscles
and his Caesar-like profile stand him in good
stead.
• * * •
Claudian, by the way, is a patrician of
incredible wealth, whose presence obscures the
landscape. Yet when this tremendous individual
goes on living for 100 years and grows no older,
the feat doesn’t seem to attract any attention. If
Salisbury had been premier of Britain for, say, 95
years and was as young as when he started, or if
Stoddart had been at the wicket for 24 months
straight ahead and had compiled 1,725,329 runs
without giving a chance, The Bulletin has an
impression that these men would be known, and
wouldn’t require to elaborately explain them-
selves. This item doesn’t matter so very much
among the general magnificence ; still it is a trifle
curious. The co. in general shows to better ad-
vantage in “ Claudian ” than in the “ Manxman.”
Miss Maud Jeffries looks beautiful in her
Roman drapery, and plays the part in a way
that goes straight to the soul of the audience.
Miss Lillah M'Carthy, who looks remarkably like
Miss Jeffries, does excellent work as the slave
girl who fetches an unreasonably high price in the
prologue. Horace Hodges shows forth as a
comedian of great ability, and Percyval, who in
the “Manxman ” looked like half of nothing that
was not feeling well, now looks rather like Li
Hung Chang instead. Also, there are elements of
really commendable wickedness about his Tetrarch
(it seems a tradition of Roman drama, by the way,
that the hopelessly wicked Roman shall wear
flowing robes, as witness Nero, Tigellinus and the
Tetrarch, while the Roman with possibilities of
good in him exhibits his legs cheerfully to the
cold blast of criticism). Also, Carter-Ed wards
(as Holy Clement), Clitherow and Miss Belmore
have various good points; and the sea, and the
palace, and the earthquake, and the ruins that
come after the earthquake constitute an immense
amount of glitter.

The new edition of “ Jack the Giant-
Killer ” was announced to start at Sydney Tivoli
on Tuesday, too late for notice in this issue. A
few well-chosen remarks about the same will ap-
pear next week,
** * *
“ The Terror of Paris ” is in good health
at Sydney Lyceum, and great multitudes go to
see him lead his benevolent existence with his un-
exploded bomb in his hand. Last week this
paper felt called upon to inquire how this kindly
anarchist, who never really blows anybody up
after all, ever came to be a Terror, but, no light
having since been thrown on the conundrum, it
gives it up. Anyhow, Miss Wheeler, dying
beautifully in the moonlight with her arms round
her anarchist’s neck, and with a general under-
standing that he will be a good Terror and try to
meet her in Heaven, is a very pretty picture, and
one which deserves support on its artistic merits
alone.
** * *
Nothing has happened at Sydney Opera
House since last week to throw any light on the
circumstances under which the very old man was
the very young man’s office-boy some ten years or
more before the drama opens. Still, if “Tracked
Down ’’serves to warn any intending villain of the
foolishness of hiring an aged and astute office-boy
who may bowl him out in after years, it won’t
have lived quite in vain. But the very bad man
at the Opera House is such a weak and ineffective
and utterly clumsy piece of infamy that he repels
sympathy. When he butchers the squatter and
recovers the incriminating pocket-knife a grey arm
with whiskers on it darts from behind a curtain
and snatches the property. Any prompt ruffian
would sprint after that arm at once and recover
the goods, but this futile assassin spends several
minutes in a long oration in which he asks nobody
in particular if this is a vision, or words to that
effect. Then he begins tosuspect the poor old body,
which is lying quietly in quite a different direction,
of having jumped at the pocket-book, and by the
time he does decide to bound gradually after the
intruder, that person—not having stopped to de-
liver any lengthy recitations —is naturally gone.
And the crude, raw foolishness of the bad man in
having his name on his revolver, and carefully
leaving it on the stomach of the deceased as evi-
dence, is something beyond expression. Even an
eighteenpenny tariff doesn’t justify the manage-
ment in having such a person on the premises.
