The second series of Albani concerts com-
mences at Sydney Town Hall next Wednesday
(30th).
** * *
Harmston’s Circus, which is due at Sydney
next Saturday, is winding up its Brisbane season
with a huge benefit to the hospitals and otl er
charities. When the horse and tiger and the
fragrant lion do exert themselves in the cause of
charity, the results are generally good; and the
big charity ben. is a fine advertisement anyhow.
#* * *
If there be any idea of producing Albani
in Italian opera manager Williamson had better
pause and reflect. Melbourne, for instance, is
already disposed to remember that the last operatic
company imported by Simonsen, and headed by
Signora Cuttica, was vastly superior, even for
concert purposes, to the Albani combination.
Cutfcica’s beautiful voice let itself go for a shilling
at Melb. Exhibition building—the cheapest
musical entertainment ever offered in the city.
The “star” was supported by her husband (a
delightful tenor when in form), a couple of power-
ful basso singers, and, likewise, two baritones, one
of whom, Sisco (now deceased), had a more
imposing voice and appearance than Pauli. And
these people were, ail of them, accomplished
operatic artists. Their performance of “ Faust ”
at the Princess’s, after Simonsen’s season had
failed, was a treat treasured in the memory of
Melbourne’s musical public.
** * *
Arthur Chanter, who was one of Marshall
Hall’s first pupils at Melb. Conservatorium, and
has acquired a splendid musical audacity from
that fervent professor, intends to take a farewell
benefit-concert at the Athenaeum Hail, Melb.,
April 4th. Arthur Chanter’s idea is to set Europe
ablaze, and reduce Gilbert and Sullivan to ashes,
in a manner of speaking. He has composed
numerous songs and light operas—and, perhaps,
an oratorio or two— also, he is his own librettist
and ballad writer. Furthermore, he sings in pub-
lic on slight provocation. Every ambitious all-
round young man deserves a fair chance to get
along, and, if the projected benefit should succeed
in getting Chanter along to London, he will find
the capital of Great Britain a promising field for
his enterprise. Several specimens of his work
will be played, warbled, and printed on the pro-
gramme at the concert next Monday week.
** * *
“A Marriage of Convenience”—most de-
lightful of naughty comedies —continues to unfold
its gilt-edged tale at Melbourne Bijou, and gets
about half the applause it deserves from audiences
that ought to be bigger. Sydney Grundy’s dia-
logue improves on acquaintance, likewise Dumas’s
airy situations, and the dresses, stage-setting, and
—to some extent—the acting. Miss Edith Morley,
particularly, has gained confidence and warmth
since her first appearance. She was little more
than an attractive young bride at the start,
whereas now she begins to shine as an
artless coquette, the turn of her head
being calculated to knock out the strongest
male resolution in one round. Charles Thursby
does not improve his handsome bridegroom per-
formance by raising his voice a tone or two, but
the writer is given to understand that the young
man’s “pair of soft-speaking eyes” place him
above criticism. The serio-comic Chevalier is
satisfactory in everything bar the fit of his coat,
and Charles Cartwright has added some artistic
touches to his family portrait. Cartwright’s
versatility takes full advantage of the double-bill
opportunity. As the old-fashioned General in
“A Marriage of Convenience” he is a strik-
ing contrast to the flippant reprobate in
“ Dream Faces,” now played as a curtain-
raiser instead of an afterpiece. He realises
a quite different cut of reprobate to the
one that Titheradge of yore presented to Bijou
audiences, and his interview with Miss Beatrice
Lamb saves the stagey little piece from failure.
The great fault of “ Dream Faces ” is a strange
child of 20, which unreal character is played by
Miss Noni Rickards on little Cissie Denver lines.
Australia has lost the one girl, Ethel Haydon,
who might have impersonated an artless young
thing of marriageable age.
** * *
“ The Gay Parisienne ” started a second
innings at the Princess’s on Saturday night. “ The
Parisienne ” is rather less popular in Melbourne
than “The Maid,” but seeing that the “ Cocky-
doodledoo ” can only be sung for a limited
number of nights, and the cast of the
alleged musical comedy is partly new busi-
ness is likely to remain brisk at the Firm’s
headquarters until “ Babes in the Wood” takes
up the running. Lauri successfully replaces Cole-
man as the husband who flees from home in kilts ;
Howard Vernon is a more festive Major than his
predecessor ; Mrs. Bryer makes an appalling Mrs.
Honeycomb, and Miss Carrie Moore warbles the
tiresome ballad of Mary Jane’s top note in four
verses. “ The Parisienne,’’ under these altered
conditions, is quite as lively and disconnected as
ever. Miss Ada Reeve plays the name-part with
undiminished spiceness, and the fury of Bert
Gilbert’s humor has not abated. The cleverest
co. on earth couldn’t make “The Gay Parisienne”
appear a brilliant entertainment because it isn’t
built that way, but in the character of a variety
show it is very praiseworthy. The most popular
“ turn ” is supplied by Albert Whelan, who
repeats several of his cleyer imitations.
** * *
Grattan Riggs, at Melb. Royal, is now
(D.V.) filling in the last two nights of a pretty-
lucrative season with “ The Irish Detective.” At
time of writing, he was engaged upon “ Shin
Fane,” a drama that appears to have been blown
together on behalf of the Riggs aforesaid. There
is a lovely long-haired villain in “ Shin Fane,”
also an agonised father, and a sort of heroine, and
a faithful Molly, and numerous people who drop
into the plot without offering any apology
for the intrusion. Grattan Riggs loosens his
connection with the narrative as time goes on.
When the villian is ultimately arrested by a plump
sea captain, on a charge of oceanic arson, the
principal character happens to be disguised as the
landlady of a hash-house, where the boarders arise
en masse at the sound of the dinner bell, and fly
for the vittles like birds of prey. Riggs in the
hash-house, and other places, is a melodrama
beyond all understanding. You merely take the
things as they come, and laugh according to your
lights—and liver.
* * *
“When London Sleeps,” described as a
raging success, will rage at Melb. Royal on
Saturday night. Much scenery has been made
ready for this exciting drama, and Melbourne
is just now full of lurid handbills depicting the
cremation of a yellow-haired lady (in her night-
gown) on the altar of an Indian god.
** * *
At the Opera House they have returned to
the old, original form of Rickards’ entertainment,
with every indication of the 0.0. success. The
programme on Saturday included the Three
Hegelmanns, John Coleman, Austin Rudd, Whit-
burn, Frank Yorke, Michael Nolan, a new first-
part, and Philip Newbury. Anyone who can find
fault with the quality of this variety-show, at
prices ranging from sixpence to half-a-crown, is at
liberty to stop away. At the Alexandra a struggl-
ing management blesses the night when it let
loose “ The Kelly Gang ” upon a crowded house.
“The Kelly Gang ” has brought about a state of
affluence at the Haleck, and Dan Barry threatens
to stage his next nine-act tragedy regardless of
expense.
** * *
Another Albani concert is due at the Town
Hall on Saturday next (2Gth), after which Miss
Elsie Hall will, it is understood, appear again in
the municipal edifice. The gifted young pianiste
gave a first concert there last Saturday, when a
large audience expressed its admiration in more
than the usual manner. Miss Hall is a fine per-
former, with a lack of savdir faire quite extra-
ordinary in a professional musician. She was so
bewildered by her “ floral tributes ” on Saturday
night that her nose began to bleed upon the piano,
and W. J. Turner had to come to her assistance
with a large bundle of pocket-handkerchiefs.
Meanwhile, Lady Brassey had sent the Govt.-
House door-key to be applied to the artist’s back
if necessary.
*» * *
Max Nordau promises that ere many
generations of mankind come and go all the
frauds, and loafers, and diseased members of
society will have been wiped off the face of the
earth. Which is comforting news for the news-
paper reader who suffers from musical critic.
This peacock ass struts about on his dungheap
offensively enough at ordinary times, but when a
star like Albani comes this way he turns on an
extra tap of balderdash. When the musical
critic is wound up by an Albani or a Melba,
he lets us know that we are even as pigs
listening to the lute of Orpheus. And when we
howl with joy because an honest old ballad hits us
right through the breast-bone, the musical critio
curls his superior upper lip and insinuates to the
folk—without whom the scribe couldn’t live—that
they are only necessary to art in that they pay
for it.
*# * *
Be Melba’s rumored intention of a trip
down under—by the way, why not “ downder ? ”
the British Australasian prophesies for her in
that case the greatest ovation that has ever been
extended to any person—prince, professional, or
politician—by the enthusiastic Australians. “ The
countless instances,” it is added, “of generous
encouragement extended by this illustrious singer
to all her compatriots, and the magnificence of her
artistic triumphs, have made Nellie Melba the
idol of Australia. To have given the world a
great prima donna is a piece of fortune of which
Australians are naturally proud, and the romantic
circumstances under which this young singer
entered the profession have always added a special
charm to her success. As the proposed Australian
visit is more a matter of sentiment than business,
it is Madame Melba’s intention to have the sup-
port of the finest quartette in England—an
entirely unnecessary extravagance.”
*# * *
In view of the unqualified eulogies which
have been so long lavished on Melba in the Eng-
lish and Australian press, The Bulletin, which
likes to look at things when they are stood on
their heads, thinks it well to print—strictly with-
out prejudice—the gist of a batch of New York
press opinions which some nialioious and blas-
phemous p' rson has taken the trouble to collate
and forward.
In “ Aida,” according to N.Y. Times, “ Madame Melba
attempted a part utterly unsuited to her voice, style, and
temperament. . . . She shows poor judgment in attempt-
ing supremely dramatic parts. . . . She did not achieve
an artistic success, and she even phrased badly at times.”
“ It would be idle to forget,” says N.Y. Daily Tribune,
“ as Madame Melba and her friends appear to have done,
that it is better to do well the things that lie easy and
naturally within the bounds of possibility than to strive
for other things whose attainment is dubious. The
earnestness and ambition of the artist are .... almost
pathetically admirable; but her wisdom is doubtful. She
sang without sparing herself last night, and in the last act
her voice sounded delicious, even though she failed to
infuse into her singing any of the real dramatic spirit
which the part and composer call for.”
Of her Rosina, in “ II Barbiere,” the same paper
says:—
“ Of reverence for the text there was not a suspicion,
and the brilliance with which Madame Melba rendered
her interpolated numbers did not mark her performance
in Rossini’s music. She made an amiable effort, both in
singing and in acting, but the result was sadly lacking in
character. Nevertheless, it was most comforting again
to hear her voice in music to which it is adapted.”
Of the same performance, says the N.Y. Evening
Post :
“ Although she sang the part of Rosina with consider-
able brilliancy, yet neither in beauty of tone nor spon-
taneity and fluency of execution could she convince a
critical listener for a moment that Patti has found a
successor. The part of Rosina requires also much grace-
ful and arch acting, but Mme. Melba’s actions sometimes
suggested the Australian corroboree rather lhan a Spanish
drawing-room. Her song interpolations were in no better
taste than Patti’s used to be ; but she played her own
accompaniment on the piano.”
Of the “ Ai'da ” rendering again, the Post says :
“ Mme. Melba is showing a laudable disposition to
enlarge her repertory. Unhappily, in doing so, she has
to swim against the tide of musical taste . . . the current
at present runs so strongly toward the dramatic grand
style that there is nothing for her to do but to try and
be dramatic, too. In this she will never succeed, as her
effort to impersonate Aida last evening once more
proved. She worked hard to make a success, but with-
out avail. The nature of her voice as well as her tem-
perament are against her. She sang sweetly, in several
places with some intenseness, but never with true
dramatic feeling; nor is her voice big enough for a
dramatic climax with full orchestra. But the greatest
obstacle to her success as a dramatic singer is her in-
ability to act or express emotion facially. Her make-up
was extraordinary, her tightly-laced bodice being posi-
tively ludicrous in view of the fact that she is supposed
to impersonate an Ethiopian slave. She has only three
or four poses and gestures, and her repertory of facial
expressions consists mainly in the raising and lowering of
her eyebrows, which does duty not only for surprise,
anger, distress, but for a dozen other emotions that are
not physiognomieally related.”
The old mistake —not Laying to Your Book 1
It is in old Byzantium, 1000 years ago,
That Claudian, in his palace, lives a life of sin
and woe ;
There he buys a lady offered in the market-place
for sale,
But she flies to Holy Clement in his cavern down
a vale.
And that aged bearded hermit is slain in her
defence,
And he loudly curses Claudian ere his soul is
hurried hence;
And bids him keep on living while the ages in
their flow
Go flitting o’er Byzantium 1000 years ago.
Then ages pass, and Caesars, like shadows drifting
slow,
Pass o’er the page of history, in Byzantium, long
ago;
But Claudian, the immortal, sees these Caesars
rise and fall
And holds the pleasant billet of Prefect to them
all.
He tries to be a good and moral Roman in his
time,
But a blight is on his efforts, and his path is
stained with crime.
And his soul-wail goeth forth afar on all the winds
that blow
Brown paper through Byzantium 1000 years ago.
He swears off wine and wassail; he forgives his
bitterest foe ;
He walks a chalk-line in Byzantium 1000 years
ago ;
His soul is full of kindness, so he helps the sick
and old,
But they drop down dead immediately they touch
his tainted gold,
Or their little children perish, or their parents
fade and die,
Or their relatives in general peg out in agony,
And in some cemetery, whose address we do not
know,
They are buried in Byzantium 1000 years ago.
The girl he loves goes blind and sadly wanders to
and fro,
A lost maid in Byzantium 1000 years ago ;
So they share the curse—until, one starry night
beside the sea,
Claudian’s palace drops like thunder, and its walls
of porphryry
Are split around like fragments of some jerry
tenement,
And the city of the Constantines is all asunder
rent.
Then the curse is somehow lifted, and, with music
sad and low,
Claudian dies, much disrespected in Byzantium,
long ago.
At Poverty Point .
Veteran Buzzi, who came to Melb. with Simonsen’s first
opera co. and remained there as a teacher of singing for
10 years, is returning to his native Italy this week. The
palpable fault in Buzzi’s tutoring was his foreign pronun-
ciation of such words as “love,” which became “ loaf” in
the mouths of his pupils. He was popular among the
Melb. girls, but somehow never had the luck to get his
name associated with a really promising voice.
Miss Fanny Wentworth has engaged Napoleon-Boffard,
the French tenor, to support her drawing-room entertain-
ment around Maoriland. But The Bulletin expects to
hear of Nap.-Bof. entering a violent protest atrainst Miss
W.’s rendering of “ I Dreamt that I Dwelt ” in ze French
mannare.
Maggie Moore and co. have done great business at
Ballarat and Bendigo (turning money away), and are now
about to ensnare the golden boodle in Adelaide.
The Firm’s production of “The Scarlet Feather,” in
London, appears to have been somewhat “ messed about ”
in the interests of Nellie Stewart. The low comedian,
Seabrooke, was deprived of his “ fat,” Florence Young,
the best voice in the co., had only four lines of solo to
sing— nobod.y save Nellie Stewart got a chance to shine.
And Nellie, it would seem, didn’t shine overmuch.
Calcutta papers report bocmsome business for Carl
Hertz, at the Theatre Royal, where he was producing
eggs and things from his hat eight times a week, when
the mail left. Some of the laudatory notices contain
internal evidence of having been compiled by Edwin
Geach, but the genuineness of the wizard’s success is cer-
tain, all the same. At latest date, he had engaged the
old Australian pusher, Harry Lyons, as advance-agent
for the show. Lvons seems to have earned quite a mas-
cotte reputation in India.
Durward Lely, the Much-Travelled Smythe’s new musi-
cal star, opens’ in Dunedin, and, after doing Maoriland,
is due in Australia about May. Lely is Scotch, hence
the policy of beginning the tour in the haggis-strewn
wilds of Otago. His name was originally Leslie—at least,
The Bulletin dimly remembers having heard so, or else
it dreamt that it heard it; and it vaguely speculates that
possibly Durward is really Donald disguised. Anyhow,
Lely brings a good reputation out with him, and, with
Smythe’s luck behind him, he should do well.
The McMahon firm runs Easter shows at Sydney, New-
castle and Bendigo, and is developing into a cosmopolitan
affair. It has 20 new, or comparatively new, English plays
yet to produce, and The Bulletin bets, in a regretful sort
of fashion, that Maud Williamson will “ fall ” in every
one of them, and that. Diver will flatly refuse to make her
an honest woman in the whole twenty. The “ Terror ”is
almost the only play since the season began in which
Diver hasn’t explained that he will see Miss Williamson’s
broken heart dashed first.
Why should an actor who has “fallen out” with his
wife delight in making a hysterical display of his domestic
trouble ? In such cases, the most admirable art is that
which conceals heart.

The WILDCAT
COLUMN
The Victorian Government has devised a scheme
for spending £2,500,000 of borrowed money on more or
less reproductive public works, so Syme’s order that
Turner and co. are to have a vigorous progressive policy
has evidently had its effect. The best-looking item in the
list of works is £BOO,OOO for a permanent water-supply in
the mallee country. But if that money is wisely expended
it will add a lot more than £BOO,OOO to the selling value of
the land thereabouts, so the cost ought to be repaid out
of the land receipts. To add such an item to the perma-
nent debt of the province is pure swindle.
The W.A. revenue for February (,£238,967)
shows a dismal fall of £87,274 as compared with the re-
ceipts for February, 1897. For the eight months of the
financial year that are gone the expenditure is ahead of
the revenue by £868,774, which is a truly awful-looking
shortage—far worse, in proportion to the size of things,
than that even of N.S.W. The great trouble in fixing up
a workable scheme of Federal finance was to make proper
allowance for the abnormal customs revenue of Westralia,
but the W.A. customs returns are dropping so heavily
that by the time the Commonwealth really gets started
(if it does start) they may only stand on the same level as
those of Tasmania. At latest returns the Westralian debt
was about £4B 10s. per head, so that, though the popula-
tion has trebled since ’9l, the debt has increased so much
faster than population that the liability per head has risen
from £3O to the above-mentioned figure.
• * * *
During the 12 months ended 31st Dec., 1897,
the Westralian Govt, spent £392,000 more than it re-
ceived. The total ordinary revenue was £2,843,775, and
the loan expenditure was £2,609,669. Therefore, W.A.
Govt, borrowed between £l6 and £l7 per head to keep
the boom going (a similar loan expenditure in Victoria
would have been £20,000,000 for the year), and even with
that the plaint about depression and want of work was
loud and lamentable. Also, it now depends almost as
much on loans as on ordinary revenue. There are signs
of great trouble ahead in Westralia.
• • * » •
The Norwich Union Insurance Co. of England
has arranged (subject to the consent of the shareholders,
which is, presumably, only a matter of form, and not very
much even of that) to take over the South Australian Fire
Insurance Co. bodily. S.A. has now only one bank of its
own, and one fire office —the one which is about to be
blotted out. Ten years ago it had four prosperous-look-
ing fire offices and two banks of its own. If the deal
comes off, this will make four Australian fire-insurance
co’s. swallowed up by English concerns in three months,
and, at the present rate of progress, it looks as if Tas-
mania and Maoriland will soon be the only provinces
where the insurance business isn’t wholly in British hands.
It should be worth doing something, by dumping a big
absentee-tax on the foreign offices, or by starting a
State fire-insurance department on the compulsory
principle, or otherwise, to preserve this dying industry.
During the last two years, though the Australian offices
held barely half the business, and though losses were
heavy and premiums unusually low, the balance-
sheets available up to January last showed £60,000
a year profit out of the insurance business alone, quite
irrespective of interest earned by the investment of the
companies’ capital and reserves. If the whole business
was held by local offices they would probably make double
that amount. And, in any case, several of the provinces
struggle very hard to protect local industries which yield
a far smaller profit than this ; £60,000 a year is equal to
£2,000,000 in immediate cash, and an industry which,
even in bad times, is worth £2,000,000 amounts to some-
thing.
The South Australian insurance Co. is a very
respectable concern with £62,500 paid-up capital, and
(until the recent big fire) £69,000 of reserves. It pays
steady dividends of 10 per cent., and though it was
struck heavily by the great Melbourne fire, a loss of
£17,000 or so seems a very insufficient reason for this
hurried giving up of the ghost.
* * * •
The New York Life Insurance Co. shows a very
prosperous state of things in its report for 1897. The
premium income for the year is £6,530,928 ; and the total
income, including interest, rents and consideration for
annuities, is £8,599,400. During the year £2,876,983 was
added to the funds, which now stand at £41,295,152. Ex-
penses are a shade over 20 per cent, of income, which is
certainly high, though the very large new business done
partly accounts for this. The really notable feature of
the New York Life, however, is not its abnormal size but
the vast amount of precaution taken to guarantee its
absolute safety and solidity. Its accounts, like those of
all American co.’s, are certified by the State Auditor,
who reports that it has £6,800,000 of good assets over
and above all liabilities. In addition to this, it publishes
annually a list of all its securities, with the value set
against'each, the exact location of every item of property
and the price set against it in the books, and the
particulars of every mortgaged property with the sum
advanced in each case. And, as it would be practicaffy
impossible to inflate the value of any one of its securities
without some of its multitudinous policy-holders being
able to spot the circumstance, this is an additional
security of the greatest value. The Bulletin would very
much like to know how some Australian and a whole lot
of British offices would get through a State audit and
State valuation, and what a complete list of their secu-
rities would look like.
The New York Life now bases all its estimates
on the assumption that its accumulated pile will only
bring in 3 per cent, interest in future. The Liverpool and
London and Globe does the same, so far as concerns the
greater part of its business at all events, but otherwise
the New York Life stands alone in this respect among
offices doing business in Australia. The 3 per cent,
estimate means a vast amount of extra security. A life
office is under agreement to pay certain sums to its
policy-holders at dates which can be very accurately
averaged by the tables of mortality. And the test of its
solvency is that the cash on hand, with future premiums
and the interest which the office may reasonably expect
to earn, should make a sufficient fund to cover the
amounts payable when they fall due. If an office pos
sesses any cash at all, it can always make itself solvent
on paper by assuming that the said cash will accumulate
in future at a big enough rate of interest. The lower the
rate of interest assumed in the calculations, therefore, the
greater the margin of security. The New York Life
absorbs £3,332,000 of its surplus by reducing its assumed
interest to 3 per cent., but even this leaves it with
£3,534,000 above all liabilities, which is certainly a large
enough rim for all purposes.
In the happy-go-lucky land of Australia there
is no legally-fixed standard of solvency. A co. may esti-
mate future interest at anything it pleases. It may also
estimate the amount of its present liabilities at anything
it pleases, if it hires a complaisant actuary. It can also
include anything it likes as assets ; it may even include
the office cat, and call it a mortgage, and put any value it
likes against the cat, provided it hires the right kind of
auditor. Considering some of the very remarkable
assurance concerns which are beginning to arise under
this very remarkable system, the American idea of State
supervision must needs be adopted here, sooner or later.

* * • *
About the most confiding insolvent on earth
appeared in Melb. bankruptcy court, the other day, to ask
for his discharge. He said that he and Mrs. Taylor, wife of
the once-famous boom-banker, were joint owners of a
certain property. At that time he had an overdraft of
£6OOO at the Land Credit Bank. In 1885 Taylor, he al-
leges, informed him that the property had been sold for
£17,000 and that his share of the money would be paid
into the bank. He therefore signed the required docu-
ments, and for six years he took it for granted in a bliss-
ful sort of way that the money had been paid and his
overdraft duly squared up. Then he casually discovered
that the document he signed without looking at it was a
transfer of his share of the property to his partner for
£2518 : also that the property was not sold till 1890 and
then it fetched £27,000 instead of £17,000 ; and that his
overdraft was still due ; and, as Taylor is now in gaol, and
Mrs. Taylor is supposed to be in America, and the money
is quite untraceable, the only thing to do was to go
broke. The same day another man applied for his
discharge in the same court, and his trouble also was that
he habitually signed documents without looking at them,
to oblige a friend he had known from childhood, which
documents proved to be mortgages for £l2OO and other
things Tiie Bulletin begins to have an idea that no
transfer or mortgage should hold good unless signed
before a public functionary, who attests that he got each
party separately in a room, and explained to him exactly
what he was making himself liable for, and that the said
party was sober and wide awake and said he understood
all about it. And if the party is a woman the matter
ouo-ht to be explained to her three times, and if the
functionary has any doubts about her comprehension he
should have authority to have her head shampooed to
brighten up her intellect.
. * * *
One matter about which The Bulletin has
complained a great deal and for a long time is that audi-
tors’ certificate which says that the balance-sheet is cor-
rect “ according to the books.” The absolute worthless-
ness of that statement is too conspicuous to require com-
ment ; it simply declares that if the books are right the
balance-sheet is right, and if the books are a hopeless
fraud, intended to disguise the fact that the concern is
utterly bankrupt, then the balance-sheet is ditto. The
following are some of the Australasian institutions which
publish this supremely useless variety of auditors’ certifi-
cate : Queensland National Bank ; Bank of N.Q. ; . E.S.
and A Bank ; A. J.S. ; London Bank of Australia ; Bank
of Australasia ; and the Union. The Victorian banks are
now required to publish a certificate which says in so
many words that things are, or are not, correct, so they
are out of the above category ; a few N.S.W. banks have
also kept out of it voluntarily. And the above lot would do
well to follow their example.
• * • *
A question about the identity of Stacy was an
oasis in the dry bankruptcy case of Cyprian Stanton,
late manager of the City Bank of Sydney. A good part of
Stanton’s disaster consisted of ten cheques—seven of
them for over £lOO each—payable to “self, but alleged
to be loans to Stacy. A good part of the assets consisted
of £9BO worth of Stacy’s cheques (eight months old and ap-
parently unpresented) on the Commercial Bank of Aus-
tralia Perth. Therefore, the Insolvency Court "naturally
wanted to find Stacy. The insolvent had no letters from
Stacy, and the only address he knew of was G.P.0.,
Brisbane.” Stacy was often in Sydney, and, when there,
stayed somewhere in Newtown, address not known. The
insolvent simply wrote to him at the G.P.O. Couldn t say
how he first caine to know Stacy. Didn’t know anyone
else in Sydney who knew him. Didn t know any-
one in Westralia who knew him, though Stacy
had mining properties there, and his cheques
were payable there. Had no copies of his letters
to Stacy. Hadn’t heard from him for six months. Didn t
know where he was now. Stacy had never had an office
in Sydney, and he (insolvent) didn’t really know how he
could prove Stacy’s existence, or anything else about him.
So the painful subject dropped, and the hazy man, who
apparently never had an address and couldn t be identified
bv anybody, is still remembered only by his signature on
those two unpaid cheques. The present writer is darned
glad he never lent any money to Stacy, anyhow—not be-
cause he would have any fear about repayment, but
because the beastly reticence of a man whose only address
is “ G.P.0.” would hurt his feelings.
* * * *
Much to the disgust of the reporters, the half-
yearly meeting of Mutual Store Ltd. (Melb.) last week
passed over without either a row or the usual adjourn-
ment for irate speakers to recover breath enough to con-
tinue yelling at one another. The reporters hope, how-
ever, to be overloaded with good copy when Anthony
Hordern gets started in the city, and Foy and Gibson s
branch in Prahran gets into working order.
A certain bank, which is owed over -£70,000
(only partially secured) by a trading- co., has, for the last
two years, been holding in bond £60,000 of goods con-
signed to the co., but which the bank has paid for. As a
result, the bank has not only been out of interest all that
time, but must have largely increased the cost ot the
goods by having to pay storage. These charges have, as
a matter of fact, increased the co.’s liability to the bank
quite £lO,OOO. At last the bank has awakened to the lacq
that merchandise is only worth what it will sell for, and
that a trading co. must have something to sell, so the
bank released the whole of the goods, and the co. s credit
has been galvanised.
* • • 9
The story goes that Sydney’s big universal pro-
vider really is about to invade Melbourne, having bought
a large business frontage in Brunswick-st., Fitzroy. nut
tackling established Melbourne rivals is not like a battle
with Sydney minnows. In Smellbourne the big guns in
the line have not learnt their business in Australia, but
have transplanted British and Yankee methods and
adapted them to Australian requirements. However, the
public will be the gainers no matter which firm floats or
fails.

N.S.W. Govt. Savings Bank officials have now
received absolute instructions always to refuse cheques
tendered by depositors, so that people only having
accounts in the Govt, institution have the utmost aitn-
culty in getting rid of “crossed” cheques. As there is
no conceivable way of defrauding the savings bank with
worthless paper, this seems to be simply a very stupid,
and unnecessary regulation.
* * * *
Grenfell (N.S.W.) sends along the first cheering
item that has come from that former home of big mining
operations for a long time. Tributors in the Young
O’Brien have struck the reef 4ft. thick and carrying fine
gold, with a shoot a foot thick that will go 4or soz. This
at the 200 ft. level in dry country. There seems no doubt
as to their being “on it” this time. Ninety tons are on
the surface, which will stand handling and inspection.
Local facilities for dealing with it are very one-horse. One
battery, in the hands of the Cyanide co., doesnt crush
outside stuff. The other hung up indefinitely, while
owner finds a mislaid spanner or goes kangaroo-shooting.
Old workings of mine unsatisfactory, and machinery very
primitive. No water making, however, where work is
now going on, and very little at the 250 ft.
* » * *
More big things said of Booth’s United mine,
Coolac, N.S.W., for which a 40-head mill is going up.
The chairman of the co. cables from London : ‘‘l have
sold mine. English co. will be registered in 17 days.
Capital, £240,000; working capital subscribed, £lO,OOO,
and £lO,OOO worth shares reserved. Vendors receive
130,000 fully-paid shares and £60,000 cash, payable (and
here conies the rub)-“£5000 from first proceeds gold,
balance payable 50 per cent, gross yields.” Analysed,
and what does it all really amount to ? That for under-
taking to find £lO,OOO, or, rather, subscribing for it, the
promoters get 100,000 shares, leaving 10,000 for further
issue, if more money is wanted, or, rather, can be got,
which entirely depends on the condition of the mine at
the time the coin is required. That is really underwriting
£1 shares at 2s. each. All the cash must come from the
gold The first £5OOO worth the vendors take, and half
afterwards ; but suppose the time comes when the mine,
so handicapped, won’t pay to work ; will the promoters
put up the money to supply the deficiency, or will the
vendors be again approached ? The writer has been there
himself ; and will supply the answer on easy terms. Why
go to London at all to place the mine under such a dead-
weight of capital as £240,000 with such conditions ?
Whatever its crushings, it is, in some respects, already
crushed.
* * * *
One Interested : “ As usual in N.S.W., where
most mining things are done upside-down, the new co.
formed to take over the old Chesney mine at Cobar is
made the victim, and has to pay the penalty, of the partial
exemptions with which it had nothing to do. It has been
allowed one month to find room for some 60 men, and
meanwhile has to employ 20, though incorporation is not
complete, and the meeting* of the old co. to ratify the
contract has just been held. It has been advised and
correctly advised —that it is impossible to find remunera-
tive work for so many men before the new system for the
working of the mine has even been designed, and more
than one capable mining man has declared that even the
work being done by the 20 men is so much wasted money.
Nevertheless, the Warden has declared otherwise, while
quite a number of * wild cat ’ leases floated in the Peak
boom are allowed to remain idle month after month. The
reply that the Chesney has already spent over £30,000 on
the leases—by far the most spent on mining work on any
Cobar mine outside the Great Cobar—and, therefore,
should be made to spend as much more, would be in keep-
ing with the incomprehensibility of the whole business.”
* * * *
“ L. 8.” is informed that the writer did not ask
why the Illawarra Smelting-works “ were not located at
Sydnev,” but he asked why they were not put “some-
where" on the deep waters of Port Jackson.” Works of a
more objectionable nature can be found on these fore-
shores, and room could easily have been found for smelt-
ing-works, and no protest made. The 2s. 9d. tonnage-rate
from Darling Harbor to the works means the same
rate for many thousand tons of flux as well as. ore, and a
much higher rate for the return of bullion, in re which
“ L. 8.” is silent. “ The Illawarra works would have been
completed by the same people but for the action of the
Govt, in bringing on the Mt. Kembla bill. That will do
for the writer’s case. He begs again to assert that the
fixino- of the site of these works at Dapto «os a gigantic
mistake, which the co. has already discovered, even
though it has not officially admitted the fact. There are
SO me° people who would even regard it as “ fatuous ”to
accept the testimony of men who are in a measure
responsible for these works, but quite right and proper to
take the assurance “that the selection was a wise one
from persons who were largely interested in the sale of a
site, through the disabilities of which the smelting-works
now occupying it will be for ever handicapped.

“ Nothing cheaper or better to buy now than
Girilambone shares,” declares the Sabbath tipster, and, as
in innumerable other cases, the same tip was given when
the shares were three times the present price (then the
song was that they were soon going to £5). The Bulletin
trusts that some of the Sydney brokers will get killed in
the rush for Girilambones. A few Sydney brokers would
not be missed. Sudden death would put the poor wretches
out of their misery.
* * * *
Broken Hill Block 10 seems to have at last got
its new milling plant to run about as smoothly as can be
expected of any of these jiggers ; and for the second week
in March 564 tons concentrates were got from 2381 tons of
ore. The product runs to 30oz. silver, 64 per cent. lead,
with 8.81 per cent, zinc per ton. This is a record. But
underground, though the reserves are big, developments
have not been proportionate.
* » * *
The all-English-owned Broken Hill Consols has
followed up a rich but very patchy and small lode forma-
tion by striking a silver slug weighing 1721 b, and assaying
70 per cent, fine silver. This Consols mine is at the bot-
tom of a flat three miles or so from the Broken Hill mines ;
and, though no connection between the two has been
traced, there is little doubt that the Consols patches have
been leached out from the parent lode. It was much the
same along the Comstock, where semi-detached bodies
were found hundreds of feet away from the main “ chim-
neys.” The Consols, by the way, has given at least two
minerals new to science.
• * • •
The copper production of the Great Cobar mine
is now averaging over 70 tons a week ; that, in round
figures is worth £3,500. Another £lOOO at least may be
added for gold, and the total, £4500, may reasonably be
divided—one £2250 carrying the whole cost of working,
including royalty to the old co, and the other moiety
divided amongst the lucky tributors. Thus five men
make among them £125,000 a year or so on an original
investment of, it would appear, much under £lO,OOO. A
third blast furnace is at work, a fourth is to be put m,
and another erected at the tributors’ refining furnaces
at Lithgow, to treat slag. Ye gods !

pen-wielder, breaks out in the daily press in favor of the
Newcastle coal-miners, who demand that all coal-skips
shall be weighed. And yet, so far, no one has heard of
W.E. A. being a general-election candidate.
* * » •
That mighty organisation, the London and
Globe, is now fixing up a subsidiary co. to take over that
awful list of alleged mines with which it has, in one way
and another, been burdened—such as Hannan’s Golden
Group, Golden Treasure, Golden Dream, and others bear-
ing names equally satirical. Capital, £1,500,000, of which
a modest million will be absorbed in purchase-money and
the balance for working-capital. The new co. will take a
big interest in the British-American co. promoted by the
L. and G. last year, and that appears to be about all the
consolation it will have.
• * * *
The W.A. Mines Minister says the Govt, has
not the slightest intention to remove the alluvial regula-
tion restricting workings to 10ft. from the surface. “ They
had taken up a position from which they saw no reason to
depart. The Govt, was open to receive any suggestion
for the solution of the difficulty, but so far nothing prac-
tical had been submitted.” Of course not. The W.A.
Govt, is badly addicted to tying itself and the province
into a knot and then asking to be untied. In the face of
innumerable warnings they allowed the dual title to
creep into the new Mining Act. At that particular time
the principle was causing little trouble, and it might have
been done away with without much injury, but that it
would sooner or later cause trouble was well known.
However, the absurd regulation has been declared only a
temporary expedient till Parliament has dealt with the
subject. Obviously a dual title for the same land is im-
practicable, and the question for W.A. Parliament to
determine is how to remove this anomaly from the Mining
Act with the least amount of injury to persons with
divided interests. The density of the law on the subject
of alluvial was instanced at Bulong some weeks ago, when
a party of diggers claimed that the formation struck at
130 ft. was alluvial, and demanded to take advantage of
the reefing lessees’ shaft and level in working it.
* » * *
The last crushing heard of from the Empress of
Coolgardie—so tons for 4oz. Inasmuch as many persons
and papers are very fond of blaming the writer for not
observing the richness of the Great Boulder when it
looked quite as ugly a surface show as the Empress, he
again begs to remind the Monarch of Managers —Zeb.
Lane—that in his report he foreshadowed a bigger future
for the Empress than for the Boulder. And where is that
future V
* * * *
Having been much abused for saying the Tor-
nado mine, W.A., was over-capitalised, Menzies Miner
rejoices over the co.’s first and final div. The liquidator
has so accurately worked out the fractions as to make it a
23rd part of a penny per share. A man with 1000 would
receive 3s. 7d. !
* » » *
Clarke & Co.’s March circular contains an article
on the Norseman goldfield by “ Uitlander,” late of Melb.
Argus. He declares that, of all W.A. fields, it has been
the most fortunate, and that bad management, insuffi-
ciency of capital—same old wheeze—and difficulty of
access, have been the chief hindrances. Though in many
cases a better state of things rules to-day, the mistakes of
the early times have proved fatal to the prospects of
several properties. Much of the capital obtained was
grossly misapplied. Last year brought forth its surprises
and disappointments. While progress has been slow, it
has been sure. It would be vain to look forward to out-
puts going up by leaps and bounds, but certain of the
co’s should materially increase their returns, while new
batteries will be started on the Break-o’-Day line, and
possibly elsewhere. He says not a word for or against a
railway from Esperance.
A marvellously rich find of telluride is reported
to have been made in the Australia, one of the Associated
mines at Hannan’s. “ Pieces were broken off weighing
lcwt., containing 45 per cent, gold.” The telluride vein
in one sentence is described as 2ft. thick, and in the next
we have it that the whole of the bottom of the winze is in
telluride ore. And yet the shares only moved two or
three shillings. Marvellous 1
*m * •
Queensland Menzies, W.A., output to date —
4422 tons for 17,5660 z., or just under 4oz. per ton. This,
says one paper, is a record which no W.A. mine outside
of Hannan’s can touch. It has paid a number of divs.,
and the shares are still lower than before it paid any.
Efforts have been made to sell the mine to Bull promoters,
but, in the present state of the market, Bull only
buys at such terms as would make the present co. a
fool to part. It is a quartz-mine, and, like nearly every
other in the country, the gold chutes are short. They are,
however, very rich. With short chutes it is hard to
approximate the life of a mine, and now that lying is at a
discount and truth at a premium, the show has to be
worked for all it is worth, like those of Charters Towers,
Gympie, Bendigo, &c.
* * • *
Announced that the management of the General
Gordon, W.A., has been removed to Adelaide. The time
seems to have arrived when some London mining journals
think something nasty might be said of this mine with
safety. It is only when the money has all gone that the
average mining journal wades in. And who is going to
apologise for the many nice things published concerning
this frightful “ cat ” ?
* • * *
Mr. Cuthbertson, “the fine figure of a man,”
who has gone to Klondyke with much Sydney daily-paper
marmalade re his Australian exploits, was leader of an
expedition of' Johnnies who came from London to Wes-
tralia about four years ago. They had an enormous
amount of baggage, a great many rifles and other shoot-
ing-irons, with about a ton of ammunition. Their tents
were lined with green baize, and their general outfit
pai-alysed the province as the most humorous thing that
had come in. Also, they bought hundreds of pounds’
worth of new stuff at Perth, including “ bathing-suits for
Lake Austin.” The expedition moved out of Geraldton
at the rate of nearly a mile a day, and, for the sake of
velocity, it had to throw off a lot of dead weight by means
of an auction sale.

THE TROUBLES OF A NEW-CHUM.—"DRAFTING.”
S. M. Herald advt.
CjT. MATTHEW’S CHURCH, ALBURY.
0
A CURATE, if possible young and unmarried, re-
quired for Alburv from first Sunday in April ; town
and country work. Choral Services in parish church.
Hospital, Religious Instruction in Schools, Visiting,
C.E.T.S., and other parish organisations, &c. Stipend
equal to about £l6O p.a.
Address, Rev. Canon SPENCER, Albury.
It would surely have to be a very young and very
unmarried curate who, for =£3 a week, would at-
tempt to run parish church, C.E.T.S., Dorcas
Society, mothers’ meetings, the annual bazaar,
and all the rest of the saintly shows—risk his
health in hospital-visiting, swill afternoon tabby
tea, and deprive himself of every recreation that
could possibly interest a man. But on second
thoughts the natural and habitual interest of the
Dear Girls might compensate for everything. A
young and unmarried curate is the only Australian
allowed to spoon ninety-five nice maiden, at once.
That inconsequent aboriginal animal called
Woman continues to conduct herself m a manner
defying common sense. At Capella (C.Q.), the
other day, one of her species, enraged at the al-
leged infidelity of the man she was living with,
poured kerosene over her dress, lit herself, and
straightway departed to the faithful grave by a
route of great agony.
She filled with kerosene a little bottle holding about a
couple of inches ; then she let it drip down on her dress
in front; then she took a letter in her hand and got four
matches, and stood at the front door awhile ; then went
outside the door, shook hands with her child, kissed it,
and said “ Good-bye.” She then kissed witness, and said
“Good-bye.” She put her hand half-way down her
skirt, and as she was about to apply the match, witness
said “Look out, Mrs., you’ll burn your clothes.” She
then replied, “It’s all his fault, he is only to blame.”
She then touched her clothes with the match, and
Will it not be spoiling Woman to try and turn
her into a rational creature by way of the suffrage ?
Is she not infinitely more artistic as she is ?
* * Sc
Mrs. Brownlow, of the Wesleyan Foreign
Mission, tells Melbourne that there is a Social
Purity Society amongst the natives of Dobu in
Papua, and that the native girls are most earnest
and devoted in the Cause. How too awfully con-
soling ! Think of the Papuan damsel, attired in
two moral tracts and a nose-ring, out on the war-
path with her little auger, and with an inflamed
eye open for evidences of the lack of the new
mission-made morality amongst the blackest sheep
of the black flock. A Social Purity Society in
Papua—think of it! Yesterday the natives were
naked and unashamed; to-night they fox each
other under the trees. After all, the missionary
is very like the Australian rabbit-inspector who
provides for his future by carefully breeding bun-
nies. The former creates the sins he professes to
cure, and hangs on to Mrs. Grundy’s apron-strings
wherever he goes.

* * * *
G.B. : Rabbi Abrahams (Melb.) in the
Age says that the Jews do not object to inter-
marriage with Christians. He is at pains to
point out that the Rothschilds and other gilded
daughters of the Lord’s Chosen have married any
amount of betitled Christian swells. Which is ail
true, and proves that Abraham’s further conten-
tion that the Jews are loyal to their religion is
rubbish. The Jews, as Heine points out, have no
religion of the soul. It has its origin in the spine,
The Jew is a materialist—always was, and always
will be. And so his religion, and law, and dogma
may go hang when material advantages come
along. Baron Hirsch, standing in his salon to
receive his titled guests, said to his nephew, ‘All
these people will some day be our relations, or
our servants.’ The Jew is very patient. He
accommodates himself to his environment. And
he comes out on top.”
Dear B.—l support “Q.” re criminal
physiognomy. The criminal is no uglier
than the “strong,” gritty man in lawful
paths ; in fact, there’s a vast resemblance.
It’s a case of extremes meeting. In the
first place, the criminal is mostly made
after birth, and it’s the pick of the cradles
(big-jawed, full-blooded men) who run to
extremes either way; hence the likeness.
The plain, small-jawed, uninteresting law-
abider and the petty larcenist bear a family
resemblance also. The only difference,
generally speaking, is that one is too fright-
ened to steal and the other is too lazy to
work.—O.P.

The tough colour - question a
backblock N.S.W. township lately
raised, by objecting to aboriginal child-
ren at the local State school, was raised
by Captain Neitenstein with regard to the
Sobraon many years ago. Then Neitenstein suc-
cessfully resisted the authorities when they en-
deavored to force convicted aboriginal boys on
board his training ship.

There must be great depression in the
footpad branch of the thieving profession in Melb.
just now. The other evening two men waylaid a
N. Melb. citizen, and, after half-murdering him,
robbed him of a black bag he had been carrying.'
Shortly afterwards they went into a shop and
asked the proprietor to let them have two cooked
rabbits on the security of the bag. He gave them
the rabbits and sixpence, which they promptly
spent in beer. On the credit side of the trans-
action appear two cooked rabbits and two long-
beers ; on the debit side—probably two years in
Pentridge fpr each rabbit and a year for each
drink.

Frank A. Nankivell, who sojourned for
frf m Japan h ‘ s / ’ f
proceeding to Murka, gives a pretty bad account
of Kerristian mission work in the Mikado’s
country. The first thing that struck him on
arriving at Nagasaki was a palatial structure on
the Bluff, built by the local missionaries for the
accommodation of their blessed selves, with money
raised by the Methodist Episcopal Church of
America. The real purpose of this huge sub-
scription was a mission house; the actual result
was a missionary club (for lioth sexes), where the
Board’s anointed live like fighting cocks, whilst
dispensing the Word in a shabby little place hard
by. During the three hottest summer months
they enjoy a high old time in the mountains, along
with a handful of pupils. Lady missionaries
make no pretence of unworldiness. One of them
went into violent raptures over some unset pearls
which Mrs. Nankivell incautiously showed her.
They were such sweet loves of pearls that the
Kerristian spinster borrowed them to show to a
friend, and none of the precious articles came

DEAD BEFORE ITS TIME.
THE JUBILEE KANGAROO.
A LETTER FROM PRINCESS HELENA.
—Age Headlines.
They caught her on the ranges near the fields of Ballarat*
A socialistic savage, Austral-bred,
And they set themselves to train her to a good aristocrat
And a credit to the toney troop she led.
But it always was a source of secret trouble to Adair
That she wasn’t half as swagger as the Mounted Rifles
were.
They fSted her and feasted her, and paragraphed her well,
Around the town with lords and earls she rode ;
The Adair and she got chummy with the royal English
swells
And within the Windsor Palace she abode.
But she didn’t thrive with princes and the caste of Yere
de Vere,
For she wasn’t half as haw-haw as the Mounted Rifles
were.
So her eyes grew dim and glassy and her tail began to
lag,
And her somersaults grew weaker day by day,
Till at last upon her vitals the death-angel got a drag,
And while an empress wept she passed away.
And her soul, released from bondage, through 12,000
miles of air
Fled back to lowly Ballarat and Corporal Adair.
But the Duchess -Dressed the button till her secretary
came,
Thro’ whom Aaair was duly localised ;
And she sent a touching letter (in the secretary’s name),
Which has since been very richly advertised.
And Daisy’s soul is now a link which binds the Throne
and Heir
To the loyal Mounted Rifles and their Corporal Adair.
WEDDING HYMN
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cold May gives place to rainy June,
And priests eomplot with nurses ;
Dull shades will mar Love’s frescoes soon,
Harsh notes will spoil his dainty tune,
Dead echoes haunt his verses.
Ah me, the times are fit for rhymes
With melancholy threaded,
The Muses moan in monotone,
For Mary’s to be wedded !
Had Love not played his cards so ill,
Fate’s footsteps would have faltered,
And then the light no years could kill
Had reigned in Grecian glory still,
Undying and unaltered.
Men’s modem lives are full of wives ;
Why could not one have tarried ?
Astarte weeps where Sappho sleeps —
For Mary’s to be married!
Vio.

Breweries seem to be a favorite form of
surreptitious investment among the unco’ guid of
holy Adelaide. The Cold-tea brother who is now
in parts unknown, when confronted with a hulk-
ing deficiency in his secretarial accounts, handed
over a brewery to the horrified trustees ; and, a
few years back, an ex-Wesleyan parson, who left
the church to carry on share-dealing on Godly
lines, was, when he came to grief, found to have
been chief shareholder in a suburban beer-factory.
Still another large brewery-shareholder in that
city a little while ago startled the travellers in a
railway sleeping-car by publicly going upon his
knees before he turned in, and praying long and,
by all appearances, with great fervor. Beware of
the man who prays publicly !
Talking of things to drink. Australian brandy
we know all about; where does the Australian
whisky go to? Who sells it, and under what
label is it put out? There is far more whisky
than brandy distilled in Australia, yet who ever
heard of Australian whisky in a public-house ?

No wonder setter-carriers, &c., occasion-
ally annex postal-notes. One such, prosecuted at
Brighton (Melb.) police-court, March 11, was
shown to have been in the P.O. seven years, and,
though nearly 30, was paid the princely wage of
235. per week. But “ there is no dissatisfaction
in the service,” says Minister Duffy.

When Alderman John Norton set out to
liven things up in Sydney City Council, he
couldn’t have hit upon a more-promising buck-up
than the City-organist. The camelopardian Wie-
gand was right on the job at £very question, and
the emphatic “ Jamais! ” with which he repu-
diated every suggestion of his being actuated by
the slightest regard for any interests save those of
Auguste Wiegand was positively refreshing in
these days of bogus altruism. Not a whit more
reticent, either, was the ingenuous A. W. about his
opinion of his own musical merits. Save himself,
he was reported as saying, there are no good
organists in Australia, but this, he now asserts,
was a misapprehension, due to his “no spek Eng-
lees mooch.” What he intended to convey, he
explains, was not any absolute estimate of the
merits of the local “grinders,” but merely that
none of them are in the same street with such top-
nofchers as Best, Guilmant, Stainer, and—pre-
sumably— Wiegand. But the good old “no
savee!” device will hardly convince the public
that in thus —even, as he makes out, indirectly—
reflecting on the musical reputations of other
able players, Mons. Wiegand has not been really
reflecting on his own. All the same, how-
ever, the Belgian gentleman differs only from
the ordinary long-haired man of music in that he
isn’t afraid to say straight-out about himself what
the other fellow only thinks about himself. But,
then, Wiegand wears his hair short 1
Dear Bulletin. —Evident that the enquiry into
the Sydney city organist’s position was born of
the falling-off in receipts at the organ-recitals.
The reason is not far to seek. Organist Wiegand’s
gavottes, operatic transcriptions, &e., and even his
storm-effects are palling upon the public. The
council must do as other caterers for public
amusement do—introduce novelties if they wish
to make money out of the recitals. A few sug-
gestions. A number of shower-baths to be fixed
up in the Town Hall ceiling so that the organist,
by drawing a stop, can turn them all full on at
the proper moment. A quantity of stage-light-
ning to be procured. A canvas-scene represent-
ing a huge ark to be erected in front of the organ.
The city-organist, got up as the patriarch Noah, to
perform a fantasia, invented by Clement Wragge
and transcribed for the organ (by Wiegand) to be
called “ The Deluge.” It would add much to the
effect if the aldermen marched in two by two, to
represent the animals and creeping things enter-
ing the ark. Another matter. If the city council
has given Wiegand the opportunity of expressing
his opinion upon local organists, it has also given
certain local organists the opportunity of express-
ing their opinions upon Wiegand ; and as it is
only fair that the reverse of the medal should be
thus shown, I beg to suggest that a committee of
local organists be formed and given power to
, examine the aldermen as to their private and
candid opinions of each other both as men and as
administrators of civic affairs. Such opinions
would form interesting reading to the ratepayers.
— J. A. Delany.

“In Auckland, M.L.,” writes a
full-flavored liar to The Bulletin,
“ the helplessly-drunken man is so nu-
merous that the police will arrest only
those of him who lie across the pave-
ment, establishing an obstaclefor short-
sighted old ladies with heavy parcels
to fall over. The drunk who carefully
lays his incapacitated carcase parallel
with the street rests unmolested by the
weary ‘trap.’”
E.K. : That’s a touching story
of the Victorian engine-driver seeing,
too late, the doomed, smiling child up
on the line. I know another one, but
not so touching. Two train-men, cros-
sing a closed road over a railway-line,
found a man spread across the rails.
He was dead—drunk. The last train
was just due. They lifted the body
gently, carried it to a place of safety,
and revived it. Its first words were :
“You pair of damned thieves! Where’s
my hat ? ” Then the train-men re-
treated : and the drunk followed,
swearing he would have their blood or
his hat. The men say that the look
upon that man’s face will haunt them
till they snuff out.

A Southern suburban jeweller
grumbles that the wife of a certain
Important Personage shows too much
consideration for the feelings of com-
mon shopkeepers. The other week he
took quite half -a - crown’s - worth of
trouble in repairing a trinket for the
lady in question, who wouldn’t think
of insulting him, she said, with an
offer of payment for a “ little job like
that.” If she had thought of insult-
ing him, the poor man would have
swallowed the affront thankfully.
At Sale (Yic.) assizes “a respectable-
looking young man ” pleaded guilty to stealing
two letters from the Foster P.O. Accused said
he had been nine years in the service and ranked
as a telegraph-messenger, though he acted as an
operator and had handled much money in connec-
tion with the P.O. Savings Bank. His salary
was £o per month, and he had applied to be re-
moved to a place where living was cheaper ; he
paid 18s. 6d. a week for his board, and sent 10s. a
month to his mother, which left him about 2s. a
week wherewith to clothe and enjoy himself. Then
he fell and got 18 months’ without the option of
the First Offenders’ Act, Justice Hood stating
that it was necessary to come down on him heavily
to prevent others “ falling.” How can a man be
honest on starvation wages and with hundreds of
pounds passing through his hands ?
* * • *
Yic. Customs-department is just now suffer-
ing from the ill effects of a consignment of bad
salmon. The shipment was arrested on suspicion,
and the few tins that were subjected to examina-
tion made the establishment unapproachable for
many days. The department’s difficulty lies in
the fact that it cannot allow the fish to go into
consumption, and it cannot condemn it without
proving every tin to be bad, and it can’t prove
every tin to be bad without opening thousands
and thousands of them, and it can’t open these
tins because the resulting smell would blight even
Smellbourne, and spread disease and death
throughout the land. An attempt has been made
to sell the salmon by auction, but no one wanted
that vast accumulation of decayed fish, and there
seems nothing left for the customs but to re-label
the stuff and sell it to the Chinese population as a
new perfume.

Sydney Daily Telegraph's new young
man, laboriously working up the agony over the
Rook wood ease :
DISCOVERY OF THE CRIME.
Two men on the front verandah, waiting for their
orders, sat talking in light-hearted tones of what the
day’s work was to be. They were grave-diggers.
The increasing struggle among the daily press to
write up to the standard of the “ Dead wood Dick ”
novelette makes all other professions seem joyous
and laughter-crowned. The light-hearted grave-
digger ! The gay, spontaneous, volatile child of
nature ! Discussing the prospective day’s work —
trifling airily with suggestions of many stiffs, to
come, and exchanging badinage on differing
methods of ramming ’em down.

FT, ; “ The most common fallacy is that
people will not subscribe money for religion. Fact
is they are only too ready, when they have an
idea that the cash is for something tangible—not
merely to provide more baby-linen for the teeming
wife of the parson. Take the India and China
Mission, head-quarters in Melb. A month or two
ago they sent away 30 young people—a good
majority ladies. Now the funds are heaped up so
again that the mission calls for volunteers. The
strange thing is that nobody is asked for money.
No bazaar canoodles. Only the prayer-wheel is
trundled, a la Muller, of the Bristol Orphanages.
The money comes in big gulps, chunks, like ‘ One
hundred pounds in thanksgiving for the birth of
a daughter.’ Fact.”
* * * *
Litigants concerned in a District-court case
to have been held in a N.Q. town recently were
put to much trouble and expense through the non-
arrival from Brisbane of the Judge who was to
preside over it—reason being that, after boarding
the steamer, his Honor objected to the fact that
she carried a few stud-rams on the forecastle. If
the sheep were not shifted, the functionary
said, he wouldn’t sail in the vessel. And they
weren’t, and he didn’t, and people up North are
wondering how much more of this sort of thing
the camel’s back will stand.

Dear Bulletin. —There is a cottage-hospi-
tal in my little town, governed by a board
composed largely of local storekeepers. The
chairman is the contractor for the supply of
groceries, bread, &c. ;'the secretary, who, in addi-
tion to a salary, is entitled to a percentage of the
money he collects, is also a governor and
contractor for the supply of drapery.
Other people are allowed to tender for
these supplies if they think it worth
while. The nurse can send back to
the contractor any inferior articles
supplied if she thinks it worth
while. I never heard of the go-
vernors accepting any tenders ex-
cept their own; I suppose they
reluctantly rejected the others
as informal. Also, I never
heard of the nurse sending
anything back. The annual
meeting was held recently,
and five governors were elec-
ted for the ensuing year. The
chairman stood at the door,
and decided off-hand whether
the people who wanted to
come in were really sub-
scribers or not. One man
'was ejected on the ground that he had only
for the erection of the new wing—
he wasn’t a subscriber to the whole hospital, but
only to a part of it. That chairman was re-elected
—a man who had such a gift for picking the voters
could hardly help being re-elected. This hospital
receives Government aid on the £ for £ principle.
—Down South.

AT THE POST-OFFICE.
The blazing street was all aglow ;
A crowd, three-parts cremated,
Stood clustered round the G.P.0.,
And girls predominated.
I sought the reason of the rush
Of skirts and hats and veils,
One answered with a dainty blush—
“ We’re waiting for the males !”
