A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, March 23, 1898.
Mi Deak Myee—
The Ladies’ Club clashed tea-cups last week for
the first time this season. The hostess was Mrs.
Kearney, owner and promoter of the Jubilee Ode
that found its way to Windsor, despite Baron
Carington’s graceless refusal to swell the
cwts. of loyal tributes from Victoria’s adoring
poets and artists. A wintry-looking afternoon
would have lent a touch of sombreness to the
Salon suite, if the floral and other decorations had
not bethought themselves to cheerfully repeat the
ode in blue-and-red chiffon and red roses. The
sombreness was not lifted much by the dressing,
which struck one big black note, for the most part.
Why do women rush into black frocks whenever
the sun goes under a cloud, and miss their only
chance to blend a little crimson or orange into the
faded landscape ?
The crush in the little tea-room was hot and
strong, and the search for new premises has not
begun too soon. Visitors poured in through the
afternoon and were treated lavishly to singing
and pianoforte playing. Miss Edward Deane,
Mrs. Edwards, and Mrs. Kingsmill-Shaw carolled
amiably at intervals. The hostess, at the special
request of her guests, recited two touching poems,
“ Rock me to sleep, mother, rock me to sleep,”
and “Told in the Work-’us,” by “ Billy’s Rose ”
Sims. Which recalls that no Sydney woman has
yet shown herself sufficiently emancipated to
declaim Sims’ “ Ostler Joe ” in public. The
gathering included many well-known leaders of
cults and chiffons, the cults winning by a neck.
The title, Victoria Salon, has gone a little rusty
of late. “Ladies’ Club” is the handle by which
the nettle is most often grasped. But perils lurk
in the seeming simplicity of the latter label. As
a high-class Englishwoman pointed out to her
sister-clubbers, the other day, a ladies’ club is
exposed to the same dark danger that damages a
man’s. Whenever a mercurial male has a tur-
quoise tale to tell he exonerates himself by saying
that he “heard it at the club.” If women once
begin to talk scandal and give the name of their
club, not necessarily for publication but merely as
a token of good faith —I forget what the nice,
clean Englishwoman said was to happen then, but
it’s something worth avoiding, even if you are
only the one scandalized.
Florence Schmidt, the urilliant native-born
Queensland soprano, whose auricomous tresses
join with Arny Sherwin’s in loudly proclaiming
the arrival of the copper-boom, has been hard at
work since her last concert in Sydney. The out-
come of her work is a concert-tour round her
native Bananaland, which took her off by boat
last Tuesday, and will keep her away from Sydney
till the end of May. With a pluck—ginger for
pluck—worthy of so good a cause, she has
arranged the whole of this trip herself, is her own
manager all over the country, and has no . one to
back her up anywhere. If successful in Queens-
land she will leave Sydney for Europe before the
end of the year. There are rumors of a benefit
for her later on, and she deserves one, if only for
making a name without a patron’s hall-mark.
Apart from the beauty of her voice, Miss Schmidt
has made herself celebrated in Sydney by cour-
teously but definitely declining to sing even ’* one
little song to please me ” when off her concert-
platform.
Much criticism, chiefly feminine, follows such a
oourse as naturally as a cat follows a mutton-pie-
man. But who would ask an artist for a picture,
or an author for a book, “just to please me”?
The clamorers for free songs support themselves
under the assertion that it’s no effort for a singer
to sing, forgetting that songs, though fleeting, are
as marketable artistic wares as poems or pictures
—aye, more marketable. The fact is, that when
an artist has once put herself and her work
on .the exchanges, the public would show better
taste in asking her for her brooches, or her boots,
than for her singing.
In “The Manxman,” Maud Jeffries walked
about her native island with a swagger and a roll
that would have graced a good old salt after a
three months’ voyage and no shipwreck. All her
weight was cast heavily on to one small foot; then
a lurch and a heave-to, and a slow and solemn
bringing of it round to the other foot. That fas-
cinated me even in the face of the most adorable
old Methodee ever let loose from a den of saints and
humbugs. I couldn’t make up my mind whether
the roll, and the heavy awkwardness of gait, in
action and repose, were specially invented by Miss
Jeffries for the part of the rustic Kirry ; and held
over judgment till I saw “Claudian.” But in
“ Claudian ” the roll was almost a baby-rocking.
Not even blindness, that keeps most people still,
could induce Almida to trust her weight to two
feet at once.
Miss Jeffries shows her beauty to more advan-
tage as Almida. than as Kirry Kate. The
long dark hair frames her charming face more
suitably than Kirry’s falling knob, and the wreath
of vine-leaves is more fetching than the rustic
sun-bonnet. In the prologue, where she is only
Serena, pale-blue draperies testify to the fact that
dark-haired women should wear blue, if their eyes
are anything approaching cerulean. (The pro-
gramme, by the way, asserts that Miss M‘Carthy
does Serena, but I decline to credit the statement.
If one lady could look so like another jlady with-
out being the other lady, then neither lady can
possibly know which lady she is). Later on,
when the drama begins, and she is Serena Almida,
a classic robe, that blends becomingly primrose
and marigold yellows, makes a bright adjunct to
the betrothal scene. After her blindness she ,
wears grey, of course. All blind stage-ladies do.
But it is a mistake, for such unanimity of color
makes you feel that they can see a little out of one
eye. Pretty Daisy Belmore is a well-dressed
incongruity beside her dirty little Belos. As for
the slaves—well, I’ve always had a weakness for
slaves, but the only one among these who was
dressed according to her station was the girl
in a pair of striped window-curtains with the
fringe cut off and sewn on again for trimming.
The males were all extinguished in the light of the
great, high, awful Claudian and the curse that
follows him about like a valet. Indeed, the whole
play seemed to me like one big elocution made to
give W.B. a show. I came home happy, though,
after I had seen that the Philip of the “ Manx-
man ” is punished for the melancholy spectacle he
made of himself in that stage of life by being
transmigrated into a Chinese Jap. in this one.
A cheerfully-crowded house watched Monday’s
performance, but the d.c. shuffled uneasily under
the glances of a lady in the first row, with a life-
sized octopus standing upright on her golden hair.
The creature was feeding on a white sea-weed that
might have been lilies-of-the-valley if you got near
enough. Latest reports on hair-dressing don’t
mention the octopus, but we must set a few
fashions of our own. Hair crimped all over is
still de rigueur, and the dressing is of such arca-
dian simplicity that you have only to pin your
hair to the top of your head and put your hat on.
Nothing shows but the crimps. As for the hats
themselves, everything is to be velvet, and every-
thing is to be small. Toques will be run to death
this winter—they can be so easily imitated with a
snippet of velvet and a feather from the old wliitB
hen—but they are to be worn until they die, and
brilliant blues and crimsons are the leading colors.
For once Russia has a say in the world’s dressing,
and the moujik reigns supreme this season. The
orthodox coat and skirt gives way at last to a
Russian bodice that pouches back and front, and
is belted any way you like. It is a little terrify-
ing to wonder what we’ll wear when Germany
takes the lead.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Albani recently wrote to Melbourne’s chari-
table widow-in-chief expressing a desire to visit
the local Women’s Hospital. The committee
duly arranged that the artist, with one or two
other ‘ tony ’ people, should be greeted with after-
noon tea and a beautiful basket of flowers, doubt-
less in anticipation of the song to come. But
somehow the diva, after shedding a sympathetic
smile on the patients, most of whom are too poor
to attend concerts at any time, failed to take a
hint that her warble would be thankfully received.
Antoinette Sterling, of yore, erred in the other
direction. When Antoinette visited hospitals she
was apt to sing until the sufferers felt worse instead
of better.
“ There was a murmur that Albani was going
to sing at St. Patrick’s Cathedral. Straightway
a religious boom set in. No church but St. Pat.’s
was high enough for fashionable Melb. on that
particular Sunday. The verger was ecstatic, as
wave after wave of the Very Nicest swept down
the aisle. Probably he imagined the heretics were
at last seeing the error of their ways. But no
Albani sang ! Even the dulcet tones of Arch-
bishop Carr compensated not for the disappoint-
ment.
“ Social Melbourne is very eager to know whom
Uncle Tom will bring back with him as aides-de-
camp. Long Dicky Neville talks of staying on,
but is not iu himself sufficient to make “a staff.”
Brassey should get certificates of moral character
with his next batch of aides, and have them war-
ranted (by at least a bishop) to keep going in good
order under the temptations of Government
House life. The average vice-regal aide, like the
cynic in the comedy, seems to be able to resist
everything but temptation.
“ A certain bride’s and bridegroom’s ears must
have tingled the whole of last week, for they were
the sole topic of tea-table tattle, and, of course,
everybody knew all about everything to the most
private details. It was related how he had been
trying to wriggle out of it, and had even written,
a few weeks ago, asking the lady to release him,
but without avail. Another rumor had it that
the lady was the struggler who pined for freedom,
because she had a better catch in view. Anyhow,
everybody was quite certain one was an unwilling
victim, so they rushed to see the sacrifice, and
watched every detail of it with great gusto, as the
dear creatures do, for, despite the veneer of
civilisation, there is still the dash of cruelty in
their natures which caused them in old times to
enjoy a gladiatorial combat or witch-burning.
“ Cricket has not been the only pastime the
English cricketers have enjoyed out here. Several
of the team have been keen in their pursuit of
easier game. One at least takes home a legal
partner, and the captain drops hints that he may
take a little jaunt out here a few months hence on
particular and purely private business connected
with a lady, and will not return alone. Now the
firls are agog to find out who the charmer is, for
toddy knows how to hold his tongue, and will
.not gratify curiosity and reveal her name. The
best - informed waver between two, and are
busily trying to ferret out whether it is that fetch-
ing Sydney girl, or a smart Melbourne widow.
One of the younger amateurs can quite hold his
own with the fair sex, but does not seem to have
been positively snared, and at least two of the
professionals are quite Lotharios with the ladies.
If they are not hooked, one by a Melb. girl, the
other by one from a sister capital, it is not for
want of attentions on the players’ parts.
“English cricketer Maclaren’s wedding was
simply a shindy, quite appropriate on St. Patrick’s
Day. Being a half-holiday, every little factory-
girl was able to get away, and I think nearly
everyone started out for Christ Church, for
a rumor was persistently circulated that
Ranji was going to appear as best-man in
full Indian dress, and great was the desire to see
him so arrayed. Every approach to the church
was blocked an hour before the time, and Maclaren
and his best-man had the pleasure of running the
gauntlet amid perfectly audible remarks such as
‘Poor chap, he looks pretty miserable,’or ‘Ain’t
he a toff!’ Disappointment spread when it was
seen that no bediamonded Oriental was with him,
but still they hoped and quite persuaded them-
selves that the fair-moustachioed swell was only a
chance friend, and that the best-man had yet to
come. Every door was kept doubly locked, but
the crowd rushed from one to the other, impo-
tently beating a tattoo upon them and demanding
admission. Then the guests’ carriages began to ar-
rive and to try and force a way through the crowd.
Presently some extra-shiny chimney-pots were
seen slowly making their way through the throng,
and the cricketers burst into view. There was a
gasp of disappointment when it was seen that
Ranji was not gorgeous in Eastern garb, but was
arrayed in the solemn glory of immaculate frock-
coat and topper. The bride, with her pretty little
attendants, drove up and was admitted at the big
door, then the crowd managed to get in, and the
interior was chaos, so much so that the clergyman
(he had been carried right off his legs during one
of the early meles) had to expostulate in the
midst of the ceremony and ask them to stand
down from the seats —and none too soon, for many
showed a decided disposition to mount even
higher, and pulled monkey-faces at the parson
when he ventured to reprove them. The bridal
dress was handsome and voluminous, and the
bridegroom was pale and nervous-looking, even
under his tan. A decidedly lengthy choral
service tied them up securely, then they es-
sayed the journey down the crowded church.
Outside once more the same scramble and push
were repeated. The cricketers thought to escape
by going out through the vestry to the side-street,
and their manccuvre would have succeeded but
that their wagonette, blocked by the crowd in
Toorak-road, was literally mobbed by numbers of
sweet young things who drove them back to the
vestry, where they waited until the vehicle could
be freed. Meanwhile, the professionals, who were
in the church, got away almost unnoticed. Ap-
parently not honored by an invite to the house,
they were not rigged out in tell-tale frock-coat
and tile, but bravely sported their straws with
the team’s colors and big St. Patrick’s badges.
“ Girls pretty and plain, old and young, from
Toorak as well as Collingwood, swarmed to that
wedding. For once a bride and her following
played second-fiddle, though the childish “maids”
made a bold bid for notice with their floral bats,
Lancastrian roses, and English cricket-badges
enamelled on presentation bangles. Petticoated
critics merely glanced at the heroine of the hour
to disparage her looks after the amiable habit of
the sex, and lavish gratuitous pity on the ‘poor’
26-year-old bridegroom who, judging by appear-
ances, had hastened to his wedding as short-
tempered as usual. On the other hand, Ranji,
beautiful as a bronze butterfly, flashed his ivories
good-humoredly throughout even when she-
worshippers, pressing close enough to detect his
pock-pits, audibly lamented his consequent ‘coarse-
ness’ of skin. His cab-companions were Stoddart,
Mason, and Druce. The English captain had not
treated himself to a new suit for the occasion.
People say (this is another version) that he himself
is to wed wealthy wicketkeeper Phillipson’s sister
as soon as he gets ’Ome, so p’r’aps he shrinks from
full-dress rehearsals. Gay young Druce ‘ had ’em
all on,’ and looked as blooming as a rain-washed
rose.
.“ As soon as the crowded-out bridal party had
struggled out to their carriages, the enthusiastic
scrowgers tore down the floral cricket-symbols used
to decorate the church. After 10 minutes of
steady demolition, the bats and balls and wickets
were carried away in little bits, and the scene of
vegetable wreckage was truly awful. For months
to come the bedroom mantelpiecein scoresof homes
will bear a treasured little faded flower as a
momento of the cricket wedding. An event of
that sort is Life to suburban girls. The
bride’s mother, Mrs. R. Power, was striped in
black and white, and several other matrons showed
the same colors. The superstition against black
at weddings is dead. A dashing young matron
in a velvet tail-coat and cocked hat made a fancy-
dress impression. Frocks were numerous in grey
—a devastating tint to sallow girls. It was a
silver wedding, as far as the presents could make
it so.
“ At the Maclaren marriage, Baronet Clarke’s
youngest sister was one of the child bridesmaids.
Miss Clarke, in the becoming blend of mauve and
grey provided for the Macdonald-Greene nup-
tials, sat outside in a carriage rather than face the
crush. As usual, the Brasseys sent along their
photographs, framed ‘for this occasion only.’ In
the gift line, Kumar Shri Ranjitsinhji came out
strong with a solid silver salver and coffee-service.
“Lent must be quite out of fashion, for, just
now, the Nicest are marrying and giving in mar-
riage like wildfire. At a recent extremely high-
class function of the kind, the bride wore the
gorgeous Court dress in which she looks forward
to being ‘ presented ’ when she gets to London.
Lady Brassey is said to have lent her own ‘ draw-
ing-room ’ rig-out as a model, and the result will
no doubt impress Y.R.I. properly when the time
comes—though, as a wedding-garment, it was only
glittersome, theatrical and pompous.
“‘Cricket-girls ’ see n as if they could neither
forget nor forgive the matrimonial capture of a
famous batsman. Numberless unkind explana-
tions are given of the hero’s undoing, but the
popular version has it that long ago, when her
cricketer first made her the subject of love’s
golden dream, the bride of nowadays was a very
thickly-gilt heiress, and in those days, so runs the
legend, an unfeeling parent forbade the match.
As for marriages in Lent, some people point to
the high-life tragedy of t’other week as an awful
warning. The bridegroom, parson, guests, etc.,
you remember, were gathered in Toorak church
waiting for the tardy bridal party, when word
came that the bride’s young brother had accident-
ally shot himself in the head. Everything was
put off for the moment. Report says, however,
that the ceremony was privately celebrated that
evening, and you can’t wonder, considering that
it’s nearly a year since the unlucky young things
began trying to get married —to be prevented,
first, by her sister’s death, then by a parent’s ill-
ness, and now by this boy’s fatal accident.
“ Elsie Hall played on her piano at the Town
Hall on Saturday night, before an audience so
chatterless and well-behaved that it probably
holds a good place for a world's record in that
respect. It was so still that it reminded me of a
Quakers’ meeting and ‘ the uncommunicating
muteness of fishes.’ And it didn’t demand a
second help of every dish offered it, but discrimi-
nated, knowing that a girl who has just worked
her passage through a Bach fugue must require a
short interval for rest and refreshment before she
tackles her next number. Elsie Hall treats the
last century music-writers in a spirit that makes
?ou want to curtsey and say, ‘I protest, madam,
am vastly obleeged for your fine performance.’
She isn’t at all a worshipper of ThortheHammerer,
or a follower of the cataclysmic modern key-crashing
school. Even Liszt, as she plays him, sounds
quite sane. Benno Scherek, whose hair fluffs out
like a German silver halo round his brow, helped
Miss Hall at a second piano in a concerted piece,
and their ‘ touches ’ agreed wonderfully. Ida
Osborne sang in a white-and-emerald St. Patrick’s
night frock ; and Gertrude Summerhayes fiddled
satisfactorily, also in white —which virgin tint was
worn by all the ladies performing. W. J. Turner
suggestively gave a slumber-song as an organ solo,
but the organ was quite wakeful. Signor Rebot-
taro’s son, a slim Italian youth, made a good
second to baritone Pauli in one of the new arrival’s
favorite numbers. Govt. House and the Mayor
and Mayoress made one party of it in the front
row.
“The Govt. House party came late, but the
offenders are leaving us on Tuesday (22nd), so it
won’t happen again in a hurry. The leading lady
of the group wore black, and almost atoned by
her animation for the lethargy of her lord. Poor
old thing ! he seemed suffering from his wonted
evening complaint, palpably pining for ‘ beddy-
bye ! ’ The most sensible thing for him to do
aboardship would be to roll himself up like a
dormouse and have his sleep out. But, of course,
he won’t. By the time this is printed, he’ll most
likely be helping to navigate the Ormuz to
Albany, and fussing like a cat on hot bricks.
At the concert he was only seen to smile twice.
First of all, when the excited young pianiste’s
nose began to bleed and her ma kept sooling
manager Turner on to the platform with relays of
handkerchiefs for her afflicted child. The per-
former stuck pluckily to her post until the clouds
rolled by. Scherek was at the other piano, and
perhaps his matter-of-fact German method of
beaming benevolently through his pince-nez upon
the progress of so minor a malady, had a soothing
effect. His placid demeanor recalled a Teutonio
teacher who said to his pupil when her hair came
down at lesson : * What is it if a lady’s hairs falls
down? Now, if a lady’s hairs falls off, it might
be somedings ! ’
“ At the Austral Salon convention on Thursday
evening last an extra frill was tacked to the pro-
ceedings as the occasion was the annual meeting
(they hold them once a month). So the minutes
were a programmed item, printed in large caps.
Lady Berry was in the chair in black silk, and it
was a cheerful gathering with Elsie Hall turned
on at the piano and some musical etceteras. A
bright spot in the evening’s proceedings was a
playlet, ‘Cornered, a.d. 1998,’ by Evelyn Gough,
performed by the authoress and a young
Bracy. This trifle is No. 3 of Mrs. Gough’s
efforts, all of which have been ‘ tried on
the dog ’at the Salon most successfully. The
lady has some original ideas, which ought to
work up well in the form of a drama.
Her first was a lively skit on the Brudenell-Bruce
damsel and the other girl who wanted to be
sailors. Her women-characters wore nautical
togs, and the dialogue was briny and breezy.
‘ Cornered ’ deals with the next century notions :
marriage is off, a social contract its substitute,
and suicide under certain conditions legal. The
hero and the heroine proceed to shuffle off this
mortal coil under the Act, but ultimately don’t.
Marriage and suicide aren’t remarkably farcical
topics, but Evelyn Gough’s little play rattled the
old bones to lively tunes. She wastes these clever
trifles on futile amateur shows. Isn’t there any
money in them as curtain-raisers ?
“‘Commencement Day’ gave the undergrads,
their yearly chance of being ‘as funny as they
can' without fear of retaliation. They worked
hard on Saturday to make the most of their
precious opportunity. Special credit is due to
‘ the Precentor,’ a hardy, vivacious person, for his
gallant efforts to broach the hidden fount of
laughter by means of a human shin-bone, festooned
with ribbons—not forgetting, of course, the jaw-
bone of an ass. Chancellor Madden was highly
impressive in his official array. The solemnity of
the black-and-gold magnificence counteracted to
a T the comic touch imparted to his every-day
aspect by a cut-away nose and funny little
curls, brushed up at the back to cover
his baldness, like ‘ pretty cocky’s ’ comb.
As usual on ‘Commencement Day,’ people took
pleasure in pointing out the plainness of girl-
graduates. For one thing, most of the damsels
are aware that hard, black trenchers are not be-
coming to their pale cheeks and unfrizzed hair, so
they stand about bareheaded in quadrangles and
places, while blazing sunshine shows up every
little freckle and crowsfoot. Florence Stillwell,
mathematical exhibitioner at last matriculation,
is the prettiest of this year’s fresh women. In her
case, gown and trencher make a becoming setting
for peachy cheeks, deep-blue eyes, and wavy
brown hair, but, to make assurance doubly sure,
she ties a rose-colored ribbon coquettishly round
her neck.
“ When Miss Greene married Baronet Clarke’s
brother-in-law, the Brasseys, as usual, cheerfully
contributed portraits of themselves and small
child to the gift-show. In olden times, the bride
was complimented by Gov. Hopetoun on her
effective dressing. Afterwards she turned her
talents to financial account, but her own wedding-
gown, exquisitely embroidered by hand in pearl
and silver, surpassed all previous triumphs.
Yours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULL.

are natives of the country.

Evidently a born policeman.

week in his 102nd year, was the oldest N.S. Wales
J .P.

irate aristocrat sums up a certain Australian
Governor.

nothing but cricket. It has been my chief object
m life.” A. 11 play and no work makes Jack a dull
man.

appearance ” the embezzling official of a South

According to astronomer Russell, we may
soon expect another attack of that disturbance
called “ Ranji. Bragge ” —er —that is, Wragge,
which is working down from the North.
“SHE KNEW A THING OR TWO.”
A veteran N.S.W. pressman, Mr. Wm.
Hanson, last week entered bis 82nd year. In ’53
Mr. Hanson arrived in Sydney and became Govt.
Printer. In ’59 he left the position, and joined
surly, burly ” Sam Bennett in resuscitating the
Empire, which had died in the hands of Parkes.
In '67 he left Mr. Bennett sole proprietor, and em-
ployed his time in compiling statistical works for
various publishing firms.
S.A. Chief Justice Way had the temerity
to initiate a Marcus Clarke memorial fund right in
the teeth of a movement for a Clem Hill testi-
monial. The latter boomed, and the former
promptly fizzled. Clem. Hill’s cricket deeds are
familiar to everyone in S.A., whereas Marcus
Clarke didn’t even run a street-tote, and there is
considerable doubt in the minds of most people if
such a person ever existed.
Cabled obit., Dr. Quain. He attended
Disraeli at the last when the other regular medi-
cos would not, because Dizzy refused to part with
a so-called homoeopathic quack, one Kidd. This
Kidd, with a dose of which he claimed to alone
possess the secret, wired Disraeli up to his last
great speech, in the Lords, on Afghanistan. A
reminder of Dr. Jenkins, with his pills of Para-
dise which put artificial life into the Due de
Mora in Daudet’s “Le Nabab.”
A large-bearded person who might have
been a prosperous cockie charged into Cole’s in
Melbourne one night last week, looking like a man
wno had travelled far under pressure of unwonted
excitement, and asked for a copy of “ The Man
from Snowy River.” Having been given the
book, he ran anxiously through it till he found
something that made him swell with large emo-
tions. That s all right, ’he said, handing over
the coin. “ I’m Kiley of Kiley’s Run ! ”
Curious that Australia, always ready to
give native musical talent a handsome monetary
send-off to England, is supremely neglectful of its
native artists. Minns, Streeton, Spence and
others were allowed, so far as the general public
is concerned, like shadows to depart, with no more
financial assistance than the trifle produced by
art-unions or clearance-sales of their own works.
Does Australia prefer music to painting, or is it
that singers are better “ pushers” than painters ?
The chief qualification for parsonhood is
an utter lack of humor. After a soulful address
on the Papuan mission-field, at Adelaide, the com-
fortable Bishop Stone-Wigg suddenly produced
from his coat-tails, anci held aloft, as he referred
to the Sacredness of the wurruk—oh, the bathos
of it!—-a small baking-powder tin with a slot in
the top ! A collection-box for pennies ! Shade
of Judge Gibson, that a baking-powder tin should
be used for anything but giving a man three years’
hard !
A word for phrenology— or an unusually
good and daring guess. A few years ago a travel-
og bump-reader, “showing” in the country dis-
tricts of S.A., had for one subject a “leading
townsman”—a councillor and local preacher ami
prominent lodge man. The bump-reader horrified
the audience by remarking that his subject was a
great hypocrite, although probably he was trying
to overcome the disposition. , The “ subject ” was
the teetotal light whose remarkable defalcations
have given people something to talk of for a month
past.
John Gale’s Record office, at Captain's
Flat, was lately burnt. John has spent nearly 50
years in N.S.W. journalism, varied with parsoni-
cal recreations. He published a little volume—a
very little one—of verse before he left England,
and lost all count of it, probably desiring to
forget it. But after years in Australia his sin
found him out. Some passenger in the ill-fated
Dunbar carried a copy which was found washed
up on the rocks. This saltwater evidence of his
youthful offence reached Gale’s hands, and, despite
the memories it awakens, is preserved by him as a
treasure.
A certain missing Rechabite secretary had
a keen sense of humor. His deficiency (caused
by wild-cat mines) amounted to <£2ooo odd, and
the only assets bequeathed to the society by the
missing psalm-grinder are certain furniture and
a brewery in full going. The society is now
confronted with the problem of whether to dis-
pose of the accursed business at a loss on the eve
of the winter, or accept the missing saint’s advice
and nurse the property until next summer when
the Great Public Thirst comes along. The Recha-
bites, however, now have a splendid chance, by
unanimously going on the drunk at their own
brewery, of paying off the local-preacher’s deficit.
Kernel Lassettah is going to resign com-
mand of N.S.W. Mounted Rifles. Bulletin is
tired of hearing of the Kernel, by cable and other-
wise—the Kernel’s horses, the Kernel’s visits to
Dukes, his dinings-out with the magnates of the
earth, the Kernel’s opinions and all the rest. It
was lately printed that the Kernel was going into
N.S.W. Assembly; now it is set forth that the
Kernel’s ironmongery and sugar business has
grown so big that to attend to it he is forced to
resign his command in the N.S.W. army. This
is good news ; the Kernel will now not seek a seat
in Parliament; he will stick close to the grocery,
instead of devoting the fag-end of his great in-
tellect to nation-building.
Commissioner Cody (Melb. Board of
Works) —a jintleman wid a mellifluous brogue—is
not “ agin the Government.” Said Government,
by the way, has been good to the Cody family.
Cody occasionally gives chairman Fitz Gibbon a
grease-bath. Fitz recently entertained Gov.
Brassey and a crowd of sycophants on a visit to
Yan Yean reservoir, and, as a quid pro quo, the
Board inscribed a vote of thanks to Fitz on
its minutes. Cody, enthusiastically supporting
this, said “ a jineral impression exists that these
intertainmonts are provided at the Board’s ex-
pense, but A till ye this Board spinds no money
foolishly— it is the chairman ! ” And not till the
snigger became general and he caught Fitz’s bale-
ful glare did it dawn upon Cody that something
had come loose.
Lambert Dobson, Chief Justice of Tas-
mania, who died last week, was chiefly remarkable
for the fact that he never, in the 28 years he spent
on the bench, had a judgment reversed on appeal.
Few people remember now that Dobson was once
an active politician—as active as politicians are in
Tasmania.. He was Attorney-General in four
Ministries —those of Weston, Chapman, Richard
Dry, and Wilson. Tasmanian politics were so
very obscure 30 to 40 years ago that hardly a soul
now living in Australia knows that there ever was
a Premier Dry. His Cabinet consisted of himself
and Dobson and another man, and, v hen Dry
dropped out, Dobson and the other man ran things
by themselves for a few days. The late C. J. was
leader of the Dobson clan, which pretty well runs
Van Dobson Land in one way and another.
The secret of Vic. Attorney - General
Isaacs’ success is his intense application to the
matter in hand. He makes a point of mastering
every subject he deals with. Thus, he is a first-
class shorthand writer, so good that a judgment
or a speech reported by him can be transcribed—
and has been, more than once—by other Pitman
writers. In the same way he has made himself a
fine French and German scholar, and a facile pen-
and-ink draughtsman. In the midst of an impor-
tant crisis, in which he was a central figure, some
few years ago, a visitor to his house on public
business was greeted on entering with dismal
wails from a back room. It was Isaacs practising
violin exercises. He had just started—at the age
of nearly 40 !—learning the most difficult of
instruments, and is now an efficient player.

The weatherboard house in Parramatta in
which the late N.S.W. Chief-Justice Martin was
born was visited lately by a number of admirers,
who endeavored to stand thelocal population on end
in an attitude of worship. The idea was to erect
a statue in the place, or otherwise make it a shrine
at which pilgrims might do their devotions on the
doorstep. But Australia doesn’t shrine with any
cordiality, somehow.
INFANTILE.
“ Mother , do the little birds fly away to heaven when they die? We never
find their dead bodies.”
There lately died in an Australian lunatic
asylum a once-prominent politician who dearly
loved his little joke. He once invited the Premier
of his province to “meet a few friends” at a
private dinner. The Premier was first on the
scene, and imagine his dismay when, one by one,
were ushered in most of the men he had bitter'y
quarrelled with during his stormy career ! That
was the quietest dinner on record ; there were no
jokes, most of the guests spent the time in glaring
at their Premier-enemy ; but the host — he reckoned
those the best two hours he had ever spent.
Dr. On Lee, whose counterfeit present-
ment appears in this issue, is an exceedingly
wealthy Chinese, who has lived in Australia for
40 years. Some years ago he was created a man-
darin by the Chinese Govt, as a recognition of
servicesrendered to his compatriots in Sydney. He
is no w en route for the Flowery Land commissioned
by his fellow countrymen to urge on the Chinese
authorities the necessity of appointing a Chinese
Consul-General for Australia ; and it is an open
secret that Quong Tart will be recommended for
the post. Dr. On Lee is married to a European
lady, and his daughters are the belles of the
Anglo-Chinese community in Sydney.
An old woman died the other day in Vic-
toria—one of three sisters who were raffled by
their mother in 1853. The speculative parent kept
a hotel in Bourke-street West, much frequented by
the swells of the period. Her three girls were
young, and good to look upon, and she “ put them
up” for distribution by art —not heart —union
among 25 members at £5O a ticket. One was won
by Tiger Nelson, who fought well in Maoriland.
Young Hotham secured another. The third fell
to a merchant, who is still living—a most honored
and wealthy member of society. This last was the
woman who recently died. **
Purtell, the poet who shares the honor of
Melb.’s laureateship with Sewer-Board Bard Fitz-
Gibbon, touches a responsive chord in the heart of
the democracy with a set of verses on the recent
tote-raids. Says Purtell:
The so-called clubs are all allowed
To gamble night and day,
And filthy Chows in Little Bourke
The game of fan-tan play.
But Wren, who runs an honest tote
Without a hitch or flaw,
Is crushed and persecuted by
A rotten, shameless law.
Purtell concludes with—
Special Notice.—Songs, poems or acrostics written on
any subject on reasonable terms. Poems respecting
death a speciality.
It was the same bard who once warned the public
to beware of unscrupulous poets who were passing
off their own work as his. “ Ask for Purtell
and see that you get him. None genuine without
the name blown in the bottle.” Who says there
are no humorists in Australia ?
G. H. Reid has already controlled the
appointment to office of nearly one-half N.S.W.
Supreme Court Bench. The question whether or
not a practising and struggling barrister should
be endowed with this far-reaching patronage is
well worth consideration.

the first R.C. Bishop of Rockhampton. Within
that period Bishop Cani, the Bishop who nomi-
nated him (Quinn, of Brisbane), and the Arch-
bishop who consecrated him (Vaughan) in St.
Mary’s, Sydney, have all died.

father was a Presby-
terian minister, and
used to live in Cum-
berland - street, Syd-
ney. He was a strong
advocate of total ab-
stinence, but had
sense for all that. A
poor devil of a re-
porter, who had “got
on the tear ”• and
wanted to be good
and rise in the world,
went to the old man
and asked him to ad-
minister the pledge.
Reid looked hard at

money, boy ? ” he
asked. The scribe
shook his head dole-
fully—he was an Em-
pire man, and it was
Monday. “ A-weel,”
said the good minis-

croon. Noo, ye’ll gie
me your word, as an

spend it as I direct.
Tak’ ane whusky
every three hoors. un-

The reporter followed
instructions as to
spending the coin.
But he never went

Gov, Hampden has had sport lately with
sharks at Chowder Bay. That is to say, a shark
was firmly noosed in ropes, and Gov. Hampden
stuck a lance into it—which was about as sportive
as shooting a firmly-tethered tiger. But some
Sydney sharks have been vainly hoping fo r quite
a time to return the compliment.
Dear Bulletin. —Canany Bulletin reader
tell me what became of Frederick Vern, who was
third in command at the Eureka Stockade? When
last I saw him, he was driving an engine for a
sluicing co. on the bank of the Macquarie River,
about a mile above the Mookerawa Junction.
That was in ’64.— Trotter Bethune, 16/3/98.
Sammy Beddome, first-appointed Adelaide
P.M. in 1857, and retired in ’92, still does Ade-
laide Block. His immediate successor, Pater,
died three years ago. Turning up an old file
awhile ago the writer came across protests against
his appointment in '57 because he was originally a
layman. But he turned out a model magistrate.
The son of a N.S.W. country magistrate,
famous for his rough and ready way of settling
cases, got mixed up in an affiliation matter, and the
townspeople rolled up to see the fun. Plaintiff
brought evidence which made things look bad for
defendant—who, however, denied everything, and
brought three of his mates to swear to certain
facts. The old man played the sphinx right to
the end, when he paralysed everybody with the
decision that defendant and his three witnesses
should contribute each half-a-crown a week
towards the maintenance of the cause of action.
Leopold Fane de Salis, on account of
whose serious illness a bankruptcy case was
recently adjourned, once, and once only, distin-
guished himself in N.S.W. Leg. Council as a
stubborn and aggressi ve law-reformer. He brought
in a motion for leave to introduce a Bill for the
removal of rape from the list of offences,
and supported it with a warmth and earnestness
which made old associates stare. What was
known as the Sod vails outrage then agitated the
public mind. Public meetings were being held
all over the country on behalf .of the two lads
lying under sentence of death, but the Judge was
obdurate, and the Executive stood behind the
Judge, The present Chief Justice, who was then
a barrister and had a seat in the Leg. Council,
attacked De Salis and succeeded in kicking his
motion out of the House. And the offence is
still capital in N.S.W.

Probably there never was a case of rheumatism
that was violent enough at its beginning to cause
death ; but, like a flame fanned by the wind, each
week and month increases its hold upon the
sufferer until it reaches a vital point, like the
heart or the kidneys.
The unfailing treatment, by using Warner’s
Safe Cure, will positively cure the worst cases of
rheumatism. It purifies the blood by inciting the
liver and kidneys to a healthy normal action.
The following testimony proves this statement.
“Having been a martyr to acute rheumatism
for many years ; after having tried all the acknow-
ledged remedies without relief, I was persuaded
by one of my employes to try Warner’s Safe
Cure. The second bottle added considerably to
my comfort and relief. Under such satisfactory
results I was induced to continue using this
miraculous mixture, and after using the fourth
bottle I felt a new man ; and, although this oc-
curred several years ago, I can now conscientiously
assert that since using Warner’s Safe Cure I have
not felt the slightest twinge of that dreaded com-
plaint, and, in fact, have never taken medicine of
any sort since, and have been in a perfect state of
health ever since. I have recommended Warner’s
Safe Cure to many sufferers since my own marvel-
lous cure for indigestion, dyspepsia, and other

heartily thanked for suggesting so simple, inex-
pensive and efficacious a remedy.— W. J. Kablb,

Aboriginalities.
We were coming down Lolwortli Range to
a big sheep-station after the wet season in ’72 —
bemg on a protracted bit of prospecting with a
small dray and three horses. The shafter was one
ot the best breeching horses in the district, but on
account of the steepness of the track we hitched a
° n behind. Th e grass was high and green,
nut the flying insects didn’t trouble us much till
alter turning the first bend down the range ; and
as we came down they got thicker still till at last
horses, dray, and log were slipping down the hill
at a terrific pace on the bodies of those infernal
insects. We could do nothing but curse, for the
clouds of grasshoppers had us completely blind,
but by good luck the track rose a bit to go over a
knob two miles further down, and there we found
the dray capsized and the horses with their hind-
quarters turned to the travelling cloud of hoppers.
We got to the station at last and told the boss,
but he reckoned we did not have them as bad as
he had, for they had kept hitting the wires in the
fences till at last 30,000 sheep had climbed over
their dead carcases into the open country.

Re the ferocity of the Australian native
bear. A 6-foot, 16-stone navvy, who was once
accused at a N.S. W. police-court of discharging
firearms on Sunday, pleaded that he had been
forced to fire at the bear in self-defence. The
magistrate, gave him “the rising,” and immedi-
ately did rise to get outside and smile.

He was travelling ror a grog firm, and
noticed that always, as soon as he entered this
particular country pub., the landlord would go
outside and pull a certain wire of an uncertain
wire fence close by. Two minutes afterwards the
village blacksmith, his four men, and all the
friends they could get hold of en route used to
walk into the shop, and then —the drummer had
to shout all round ! One day, however, when the
landlord was sneaking out as usual, the drummer
stopped him. “Don’t trouble, landlord —I’ve
rung the bell myself. They’ll all be here pre-
sently ” And so they were.

A.P.: “Re Australian record-starve (water
obtainable). Some years ago, a man left a W.A.
station one morning en route to Onslow, and, 19
days afterwards, he was found in an almost dry
well, nine miles from his starting-point. His
swag, lying on the surface, contained all the
rations he had when he left the station, and there
was no camp within miles at which he could have
eaten after starting. I saw him on the way to
hospital— mad, and badly hurt by his fall—but he
completely recovered in mind and body.”

A Burrumbeet land-owner had a favorite
black-boy, and on one occasion when he was to un-
dergo a dental operation at Ballarat took Tommy
along. The colored brother sat out the perfor-
mance in a condition of blank amazement. Ho
saw the boss chaired, witnessed the process of
syringing and lancing, and saw the final haul, and
the resulting bloodshed, and sat back with gaping
mouth and staring eyes. But when the squatter
took out his purse, and actually paid for the
wrongs that he had endured at the hands of the
stump-extractor, Tommy could not longer contain
himself. “My word ! ” he gasped, “my word !
Big one drunk you pay ten shilla that pfeller
smasha jaw. Gib it tsickpen mine break your
plurry head 1 ”

Alia; “In a Northern river town I
recently saw a lank bushman riding a half-broken
filly up the street when the blare of brass from a
travelling show set the animal performing, and,
after some wonderful gymnastics, she got through
the girths and threw the rider heavily on to the
metalled road. After a long interval of oblivion,
he languidly opened his eyes and remarked,
“Well, I rode the blanky saddle to the ground,
anyhow ! ”
* * * •
A gruesome story of endurance at North
Yanko, N.S.W. the other day; A teamster aged 66,
when crossing a bridge with a 3-ton laden waggon
was putting the “ break on ” when a horse
stamped on his foot, and caused him to fall. The
front wheel ran over him, catching by the leg and
passing upwards over his body ; and the hind
wheel followed over arm and shoulders. When
assistance arrived, the poor fellow was, by his own
direction (!) literally tied together to fit for trans-
port to the hospital. The broken leg was laid
over the sound one and bound to it; the smashed
arm was stretched over his breast and lashed
there. “ Now then,” said the game old
warrior, “ put me into the cart, and I’ll ride
pretty easy.” At the hospital it was found that
thigh was broken in two places, one arm smashed
into pulp, a shoulder utterly crushed, pelvis bone
broken, flesh on the thigh and leg frightfully
mangled—to say nothing of terrible internal in-
juries 1 The old man never uttered a groan, and

though recovery was hopeless, he was kept alive
and conscious by stimulants for over two days.
Before dying, the old man, who had no friends,
left his team of horses to Naraudera hospital.

“Tomahawk : ” —I don’t remember ever
seeing a jackass in company with a snake; but
about three years ago, during a dry spell, while
assisting to muster on a Clarence River cattle-run
I noticed a number of jacks fluttering about the
edge of an almost dry swamp, and when I went
over to investigate I found two jackasses vainly
endeavoring to swallow a small eel, one having the
head down and the other the tail, with about 6in.
of eel intervening. Three or four jackasses were
laughing fit to split at the tug-of-war, and occasion-
ally one would seize the eel by the middle and try
to get him “on his own.” Then would come a
general scrimmage from which, however, the two
principals would always emerge in full possession
of the prize. None of the birds noticed my pre-
sence until I turned away to rejoin the cattle,
when the spectators flew away and left the con-
testants at it as hard as ever.
* * * *
“Adventures of an Australian shearer.”
A N.S.W. shearer, swag-mate, by the way, of
Henry Lawson, left Sydney in ’95 as working
passenger on a N. German Lloyd steamer for Lon-
don en route to Lane’s Rue Australia. From
London he worked his passage to Montevideo on
an American sailing ship. ' Yellow fever was
raging at that port when the vessel arrived, and,
sailors being scarce, the Yankee skipper refused
to pay anyone off or let them go ashore. Being
chartered for New York the shearer was compelled
to travel away again from his land of hope, beans
and alleged brotherhood. On the voyage to
N. York dissatisfaction became rife amongst the
crew, and capstan bars, marlin spikes and belay-
ing pins were freely used on the sailors by the
“ old man ” and his officers. One day the second
mate had downed a Norwegian A.B. and was
hammering him unmercifully with a cap-
stan bar. This was too much for the man
from the Paroo and Warrego. Pulling out
a revolver he ordered the mate to “ stop or
become a dead man.” The officer heeded him
not, and was found dead—shot through the head
—when the smoke had cleared away. The shearer
was put into irons, and given over to the authori-
ties on arrival at New York. Was charged and
tried for wilful murder, but a grand jury found
“justifiable homicide.” Once more the knight of
the blade tried to find a way to Lane’s “Paradise
Lost.” Unable to obtain a boat for South
America, and shortness of funds forbidding to
wait, he took first chance and shipped—now as
ordinary seaman—for Glasgow, in hope of securing
a berth there for Buenos Ayres. But in Glasgow
Fate stepped in, in the shape of a bonnie Scotch
lassie. He chucked up Lane, Paraguay, brother-
hood, vessels, revolvers, and grand juries, and
married the girl and settled down right there in
Glasgow.

Ed. Bulletin. —Can you tell me why it is
that, among bush smokers, when one asks another
for a match to light his pipe, the lender almost in-
variably shakes the box, and the borrower, on
returning it, does precisely the same ? Is it merely
to attract the other’s attention, or is it to prove
conclusively that the box has not been emptied of
contents either before lending by the one or after
borrowing by the other; or is ita religious function
of some kind, or a mere courtesy ? It seems to be
an unwritten law among bush smokers that the
box must be shaken by both parties. I have not
noticed it among town dwellers.— Vestas.
* » * *
A sturdy old prospector and digger died
the other day at Eidsvold (Q.). When compelled
by pneumonia to take refuge in hospital, the old
hatter asked the doctor how long he had to live.
Oh, a few days at most, I fear,” was the answer.
“ Bet you a bottle of brandy, doctor, I last a fort-
night.” To please the old chap the doctor took
up the wager. Next morning, however, the medi-
cine man found his patient in a quandary : “ But
look here, doctor—who’s going to pay you if I
lose ? ” Question remained unsettled, for the old
fellow did lose.

Dear Bulletin, — Re the David Carnegie
extract in a recent issue, it is quite customary
to use the natives as described for the purpose
of finding water; and it may further interest you
to know that in order to help a nigger’s decision
as to whether or not he will lead you to the water, a
teaspoonful of table-salt, administered at intervals,
is an invaluable specific. I never knew of a black-
fellow taking “ a day or two to make up his
mind ” when such treatment had been judiciously
applied.—S.
Dear Bulletin.—l send you
photos, of four aboriginal rain-makers
of Farina, S.A. (408 miles N. of Ade-
laide), for comparison with your
white-chokered imported ditto; and
I hereby back my black-skins against
your black-coats for a stake of .£5
apiece, either Graeco-Roman supplica-
tion or catch-rain-as-catch-can —the
best of three showers to declare tho
winner. For your information, I beg
to state that the rain-maker to the left
of the picture is Rev. Billy (Angli-
can), and then follow in order Rev.
Bobby (Presbyterian), Rev. Tommy
(Hardshell), and Rev. Warrick (Pe-
culiar People). You will observe that
my clergy are ranged in order of prece-
dence as maintained at local Govt.
House banquets (at which there is
never any Melb. complaint of lack of
grub)— the parson with the tallestrain-
conductor in the lead. About the be-
ginning of last month these rev. gentle-
men led a rain-making corroboree
which lasted five nights, and brought
down nearly l|in. of liquid. Rev.
Billy then came to me and said:
“ You see, one been make urn rain,
but not nuff yet. Make urn more
bimeby— this one no corroboree; this
all same white pfeller parson pray
longa rain—only black-pfeller make
um best.” After the rain cleared off
they had another go-in for about a
week, resulting in nearly ljin. more,
on the night of Feb. 24th. Then the rainmakers
(after securing the best downfall in this part for
over three years) bade ns good-bye with mutual
felicitations.— Bing y-Bingy, Farina, S.A., March
7, 1898.

“ L.M.” to The Bulletin
Isn’t it time you gave the “ cheap” Chow, Jap., and
Kanaka a rest, and turned your attention to the white
crawler who takes work at a price that no yellowskin
would look at? In my district (Northern N.S.W.) an
alleged white man recently contracted to erect a number
of miles of fencing at £l2 per mile—station simply to
find team and wire on the line. The contractor has to
draw the timber—about two miles. Posts, if of split pine,
to be 9 x sin. ; if of box or coolibah, 6in., at the small end.
Every 20th post to be a “strainer," box or coolibah, and
llin. at small end. Posts to be sawn off. Running posts
to be 2ft. in the ground—strainers, 3; all 6 wires to be
put in and well strained, and each bend in fence to be
“ double stayed ”; posts to be 10ft. apart, centre to
centre ; all pine and “ strainers” to be barked. Now, let
any practical fencer ppt this into figures, and I’ll bet
“tucker only” is the answer—and mean “tucker” at
that. Contracts at £l2 10s. are now common about here,
but making it “ even money ”is the last straw. And yet
the white man finds time to talk about the Chinaman 1


“ SURPLUS.”
The term “ surplus ” is applied, in life insurance
affairs, to the balance, ascertained periodically,
between the assets and the liabilities. Out of this
balance or surplus, distributions of profits are
made to the policy-holders entitled to participate.
The natural tendency of distribution is to diminish
the surplus for the moment; but in the case of a
prosperous company, the-decrease is speedily more
than covered by accessions of profit from various
sources. It is clear, however, that the more
liberal the periodical distributions of profits, the
more gradual will be the growth of the surplus.
Conversely, if a life insurance company withho’ds
from its policy-holders the full benefit of the pro-
portion of profits immediately available, and
prefers to accumulate in the interest of future
participants, it follows that the surplus will in-
crease more rapidly in proportion. Of course it
is easy to keep in hand more than the future will
require under any possible cireuKVetances ; and,
when that course is followed, injustice to present
participants is inevitable.
Now, The Mutual Life of New York, while
careful to make abundant reserves for future
bonuses, has always been equally careful to dis-
tribute to its policy-holders the full immediate
benefits which they are entitled to claim. The
Company has never sacrificed the interests of
policy-holders entitled to immediate participation,
to the formation of an imposing but empirical
surplus, never likely to be required even in the
remotest future. The surplus shown by The
Mutual Life at the end of 1896 was ,£6,105,444.
It is a steadily increasing surplus, which, need-
less to say, is more than ample for all the
demands for which it has been created.
But, supposing the Company had, throughout
its history, acted on the principle which
has been adopted in, at least, one prominent
instance—of reserving a large proportion of the
surplus at every distribution, and, in addition,
reducing the surrender-values allowed for discon-
tinued policies, what does the reader suppose
would have been the amount of The Mutual’s
surplus in 1896 ? Not six millions, but upwards of
24 millions sterling I This is not guesswork. The
figures are based upon the official report issued by
the Insurance Department of the State of New
York, which gives annually the exact record of
every company doing business in that State.
Since its organisation, The Mutual Life has paid
in bonuses and surrender values to its policy-holders
£44,445,244, which is more than twice the amount
paid for similar items by the next largest company.
Observe, then, what a magnificent surplus The
Mutual could have exhibited to-day, had an
illiberal policy been pursued in relation to the
assured —a surplus exceeding that of any other
office by more than 150 per cent. But, fortunately,
the Company preferred to sacrifice a mere useless
and vainglorious display to the solid interests of
the policy-holders. Who shall say that, in taking
thiscourse, the Mutual Life of New York has
not chosen the better part? and that, in so doing,
it has not well earned the high reputation which
it has attained in every part of the world as “ the
Policy-holders’ C 0.,”“ the Co. which does the most
good ? ” The intelligent reader will not allow him-
self to be misled by the parading of sums of no
use to the present generation, and in excess of
any possible needs of the generations to come,*

No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed in,
and in no case will MS. be returned unless stamps
(of any province) accompany.

Warnambool (Vic.) badly wants Govern-
ment to let it stick to the sin. B.L. gun lent it for

U.S. output of coal in 1860 was only
16,000,000 tons, as against Britain’s 86,000,000,
but in ’96 the U.S. output was 170,000,000 tons, as
against Britain’s 195,000,000. In 1860, U.S. iron
production was little more than 20 percent, of the
British ;in 1895 it was 25 per cent, greater. The
recently-opened-up geological formation in Ken-
tucky and Tennessee contains a limitless supply of
iron ore and coal, from which it is anticipated
that the finest steel products can be placed upon
the markets of the world at prices with which no
other nation can hope to compete.
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years jiast,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 3 sizes, by post 1/4, 2/6,
3/8. Senior, chemist, George-street, Sydney.*
At Carlton (Melb.) police-court, recently,
a large and perfectly sound policeman charged a
small man with assault and other things. The
small man claimed to be the assaulted party, and
produced as evidence a closed and blackened eye,
an enormously swollen face, a wrecked nose, and
a shirt-front covered with blood. The Bench
looked at the human ruin before it, and then ap-
parently it decided that his awful condition was
proof positive that he had assaulted a policeman,
and he got three months without the option.
In Prussia, in the 70 years 1826-96, there
were three cases of five children at a birth —five
boys, four boys and one girl, and three boys and
two girls. In same period, there occurred 106
cases of four at a birth—l 2 times four boys, 20
times four girls, and 32 cases of two of each sex.
Triplets came home 7733 times ; twins, 696,831
times.
A VETERAN CRICKETER. —“ CHARLIE
LAWRENCE.
In Melb. city court. Magistrate Panton,
cross-examining witness : “ Now, sir, do you, on
your solemn oath, declare that this is not your
handwriting?” Witness: “Indeed I do, sir.”
Panton : “ Does it not resemble your hand-
writing ? ” Witness : “ Yes, sir, I think it don’t.”
Panton: “ Now, sir, I will have the truth;
answer me instanter. How do you know ” —(each
word well emphasised for effect)—“ How do you
know that this does not resemble your hand-
writing?” Witness (also very slowly): “Be-
cause, sir, I cannot write.” Collapse of beak.

MELBOURNIANA.


ROSPBCTUS of tile
Pikedale Proprietary Copper and Silver Mining* Company,
... . _. isro Liability.
To he Registered as a No-Liability Company under the Companies Act.
CAPITAL - - - £120,000, in 120,000 SHARES of £1 EACH.
60,000 Shares are offered for Subscription at 2s. on application and 2s. on allotment, and calls, if required, not to exceed 3d. per share per month.
The whole of the Subscribed Money to go to the credit of the Company, less flotation expenses.
60,000 Fully Paid-up Shares to be issued to the Vendors in full payment of the Property.
Son. JOHN ARCHIBALD, M.L.0., Warwick.
A. W. WHITTARD, Esq., Stanthorpe.
Provisional Directors
G. M. KIRK, Esq., J.P., Brisbane.
H. BRACKER, Esq., J.P., Brisbane.
FT. L. WHITE, Esq., Belltrees Station, Scone.
H. BENJAMIN, Esq., J.P., Warwick.
Bankers :
THE. BANK OF NORTH QUEENSLAND, LIMITED.
Brokers :
THE MEMBERS OF THE BRISBANE STOCK EXCHANGE.
Secretary (pro. tern.) z
HUBERT HARRIS, 33 Eagle Street, Brisbane.
fPHE Company is being formed for the purpose of acquiring all the right, title, and estate of the Vendors (The
Pikedale Copper, Gold, and Silver Mining Company) in portions No. 802 W. and 803 W. (each containing 80 acres,
making in all 160 acres), County of Bentinck, Parish of Stanthorpe, held by registered lease under the provisions of
“ The Real Property Acts of 1861 and 1877," from Frederick Robert White, the registered proprietor thereof, and
which lease is for the term of 21 years from the 30th day of October, 1896, and gives the lessee a right of renewal for
a further period of 10 years, together with all shafts, buildings, tools, &e., thereon. The principal conditions of the
lease are :—That Mr. White is to receive, by way of rent or royalty, one-quarter of the net profits of working the
properties comprised in the said lease, but which rent or royalty is to be reduced to one-sixth on the expenditure of
a sum, within three years, of £40,000 on the properties comprised in the said lease, which reduction is to have
relation back to the commencement of the lease. The agreement for sale provides (inter alia ) that the Vendors shall
obtain, if this Company should require it, an independent lease of the said portions from the registered proprietor,
containing similar covenants with such modifications as are applicable in respect to the difference in the property
comprised or to be comprised in the existing and new lease respectively.
The following documents can be inspected at the offices of the Secretary pro. tem. :—l._ Agreement dated the
Ist day of March, made between the said Vendors and Hubert Harris for and on behalf of this Company, being the
agreement for sale by the said Vendors; 2. Lease above referred to; 3. Proposed Memorandum and Articles of
The Mine has been actively at work for the last 12 months, a number of shafts sunk on the line of lode exposing
very large bodies of ore of very high values. A reverberatory furnace has also been erected, and about £3OOO worth
of matte shipped to London ; but it was found that the reverberatory furnace, although smelting the ore, did not
give satisfaction, as it did not treat anything like the quanity that was expected, is old-fashioned, got continually out
of order, and lost a large percentage of metal in the slags. The Management have therefore decided to raise larger
capital, so as to acquire the latest kind of furnaces and machinery. . . . .
Appended are the reports of Mr. G. H. Irvine, Metallurgist, of Brisbane, the Mining Commissioner, and Mr. W.
G. Rodda, the Managing Director. Also list of all assays of ore and matte made by Mr. Robert Mar (late Govern-
ment Analyst). As, however, the mine is situated only 20 miles from the Stanthorpe Railway Station, and only one
day’s journey from Brisbane, it can easily be inspected by anyone desirous of doing so.
The new Company to take over the two blocks as before mentioned, with all buildings, tools, machinery, &0., ana
all liabilities, which will probably amount to £5OO, but will certainly not exceed £lOOO.
At any future time the property can be subdivided into four blocks of 40 acres each, and formed into four
separate companies, as 40 acres with the large quantities of ore contained therein is quite large enough for any one
company to work. . .
As the present proprietors intend to take up a good nnmber of the Contributing Shares, early application is
necessary. The allotment will be made according to priority of application. . . . .
The Provisional Directors reserve the right to declare the Company floated when in their opinion a sufficient
number of shares have been applied for. _
Should the Company not be floated the application money will be returned in full.
Report of 4th February, 1898, by G. H. Irvine, Esq. (late Manager Mt. Morgan G.M. Co.) :
The Directors, The Pikedale Copper, G., and S. Co., Ltd., City.
Acting under your instructions, I visited and inspected your copper and silver property (freehold) situated on
Pikedale Station, 21 miles W.S.W. from Stanthorpe.
The lode, which runs N.N.W., with a slight dip to the W., has been exposed for a distance of fully a quarter of a
mile by shafts and costeens. These have proved the width of the lode to vary from 2ft. to 7ft., carrying good copper
and silver contents. , „
Shaft C has been sunk at the extreme south, Shaft B 60ft. N. on the course of the lode, and Shaft A 92ft.
have been sunk to a depth of 20,15, and 38ft. respectively, with an average width of 7ft., and from which
good ore was obtained, averaging 8 per cent, copper and 30oz. silver per ton.
No. 1 is 85ft. N. of A., and has been sunk to a depth of 52ft. The ore body between walls has varied from
5 to 10ft. wide. , ... „
From 36ft. down a level S. was put in 38ft. and sloped to surface. At the same level N. was connected with No. 2
Shaft (56ft.), and stoped to surface.
From these stopes a large quantity of good ore has been obtained, varying from 2 per cent, copper and 20oz. of
silver to 13 per cent, copper and 90oz. silver, the average being 7 per cent, copper and 350 z. silver per ton.
On the face of the 52ft. level, going S., there is a fine chute of ore 4ft. 6in. wide, averaging 7 per cent, copper and
30oz. silver per ton. .
On the N. end of this level the formation is fully 10ft. wide, carrying a chute of fair ore 3ft. wide, varying from
2’7 ppr cent, oopper and 14oz. silver to s’B per cent, copper and 420 z silver.
No. 2 Shaft is 56ft. N. of No. 1, and is sunk to a depth of 80ft., and averages 3ft. to 9ft. in width between the
walls. The quality of the ore during the sink varied from 2 per cent, copper and 17oz. silver to 7'3 per cent, copper
and 60oz. silver per ton.
At the opening of drives N. and S. there is a good chute of ore, averaging 6 per cent, copper and 450 z.
silver, although the present faces, which are in 20ft. and 18ft. respectively, do not look so well, but will probahly
improve on driving. In the S., the ore body measures 3ft. 6in., averaging 3.4 per cent, copper and 12oz. silver.
No. 5 Shaft is sunk in disturbed country, about 900 ft. N. from No. 2. The reason of this disturbance is a
fault takes place in the proximity’ of No. 4 Shaft, and has thrown the lode about a chain to the west.
The shaft (No. 5) has been sunk 52ft., carrying good ore, varying from 1J per cent, copper and lOoz. silver
to 16 per cent, copper and 118 oz. silver. . .
At 27ft. down, a drive has been put in S. 40ft., and crosscuts E. and W. 14ft. and 16ft. respectively, without
meeting the walls. These have exposed a large body of gossany ore, averaging about 4 per cent, copper and 16oz.
Smelting and Fluxes. —The character of the ore at present exposed docs not lead me to expect that any
difficulty will be met with in producing a good matte. In fact, your ores are nearly self-fluxing, with the excep-
tion of a small addition of limestone, which you have already at hand in quantity.
At the same time, before considering and deciding the most economic mode of treatment, I would strongly
advocate sinking to a further depth of 100 ft. from the bottom of No. 2 Shaft and putting in the necessary drives,
to prove the quantity and nature of your ore at that depth.
In reviewing the state of affairs regarding the Pikedale Copper and Silver property, the good grade ore,
and their occurrence in considerable quantities, so far as oan be judged from the present workings, I am of
opinion that it warrants the further expenditure of a considerable sum for development as suggested. When this has
been accomplished, and the nature and body of ore exposed at this depth, it will be then time enough for you to
consider the best and most economic means for the treatment of same.
Mining Commissioner’s Report.
PIKEDALE SILVER MINE.
In my last report I referred to this mine as just opened and in its infancy, but since then it has been considerably
developed, and the mineral is similar to that found at the Silver Spur. It is known as the Pikedale Copper, Gold,
and Silver Mining Company, and is a freehold on the Pikedale Station, 20 miles from Stanthorpe. Active work was
commenced on the property just twelve months ago, and various shafts, five in number, have been sunk, varying in
depth from 40 to 100 feet. 'On the lode the two extreme shafts are a quarter of a mile apart. The ore exposed vanes
in width from sft. to 10ft., and averages 320 z. of silver per ton, and copper 6.72 per cent. A reverberatory furnace
was erected to experiment on the ore, but was not found to be successful, and the Company now contemplate
increasing the capital in order to more fully develop the mine, further experiment on the ore treatment, and obtain
the best metallurgical advice thereon before deciding on any particular method of treatment. Meanwhile, the work
is stopped, pending the completion of the newly-proposed financial arrangements. I may mention that something like
850 tons of ore has been put through the furnace, and 100 tons of matte obtained of an estimated value of £4 2s. 6d. on
the ton of ore. This is considered very satisfactory, especially when the mechanical troubles are taken into considera-
tion, with respect to the constant stopping of the smelting for repairs owing to defective bricks, and then again the
estimated loss in calcining before smelting is calculated at 20 per cent. This loss will be obviated should the ore be
found amenable to the Pyritie Smelting process, such as is used at the Mount Lyell Mine in Tasmania, and so far as
the experiments have gone they tend to prove that the above process will be adopted.
Manager's Report of July 19th, 1897 .
Summary of work done from the commencement (January last) to date.
Oosteening work was carried on for the first two or three weeks, which exposed the lode in various places along
the surface line, varying from two feet to seven feet wide for a quarter of a mile in length. Although we have a length
of fully one mile on the reef, operations have been necessarily confined to one fourth of this length, because of the
short time we have been working, besides it being advisable to concentrate the labor o order to get some stoping
ground open ; but I have no doubt, judging from the strong character of the lode, but what the ore will live through-
out the whole length. The most southern shaft (known as B shaft) is in about the centre of the property (comprising
160 acres), and is fully a quarter of a mile distant from the most northern shaft (No. 5), which is now down over 40ft.
There are intervening shafts down on the lode from 50 to 70ft. deep, and although the lode on the stirfaoe was only
three feet thick in places, yet the deeper we sink the wider the ore becomes.
B shaft is 16ft. deep ; lode 7ft. wide, of good value. No. 1 shaft which is two chains, Nof B shaft is down over
50ft., and carries 10ft. of clean ore in the bottom, and still widening; the first stope we took out over the 50ft.
averaged fully 10ft. wide. . „ .... , .. , .
No. 2 shaft 90ft., north of No. 1, is down 70ft., and has averaged from 3ft. in width to 9ft., the present width in
the bottom being Bft. between walls. These two shafts are still being sunk by two shifts of men and horse whip.
No. 6 shaft, at the north end of workings, we are sinking in the lode without any walls ; the ore body must, be of
immense width here, as at the 27ft. depth (sulphide level) a drive was put in South about 40ft., and crosscuts E. and
W. put in for 14ft. and 16ft. each, without meeting any walls. I am convinced there are vast deposits of ore at this
end of the mine, therefore stopped prospecting at the Shallow level and resumed sinking the shaft, which is now
down 40 feet; am still sinking. You will notice that at the'.27ft. level we have gone through ore 30ft. wide (all oxide)
without meeting eitherwall, the material assaying in bulk 16ozs. silver per ton, a little gold, and 4.0 per cent, of cop-
per. In the shaft the ore is sulphides, and is improving in value as sunk upon. The assays varying from the 27ft.
level to the 40ft., from 27 to 31oz. silver and 5.0 per cent, to 7.0 per cent, copper ; nothing like a wall has been seen in
this shaft since the 20ft. depth was passed, one wall going E. and the other W.
All the ore raised and exposed at the Southern portion of our workings (includes B, A, and Nos. 1 and 2 shafts)
are sulphides (even to the surface), and will average in width nine feet (with every appearance of still further widen-
ing as depth is attained), the value in bulk being over 30oz. silver per ton, a little gold, and 6 per cent, to 7 per cent.
CO,) *Good work is now being done with the reverberatory furnace, but I would like to draw your attent ion to the fact
“ that the plant is not near large enough to deal with the immense quantities of ore contained in the mine,” and I
would earnestly advise you to take the necessary steps to increase the capital of the Company, so that the mine
development may be pushed ahead and sufficient and suitable furnaces erected as such a mine as this assuredly
" ""a”present we are smelting about 70 tons per week, including fluxes, but arrangements should be made to deal
with ot least ten times this quantity.
Please find assaver’s list of mine and furnace work assays, from which you will gain some idea of the great value
of the mine I may mention that the mine samples are always taken in bulk.
Fluxhs.—We have plenty of caldte in the mine for this purpose, and the small quantity of iron that is required
we cart from a big ironstone lode seven miles distant.
W. G. RODDA.

AT THE CAFE.
“Hello! Have you taken a half-holiday?”
“No ; why did you think so ? ”
“Because I once heard you say it was a mystery
to you how people could keep awake after
dining at mid-day.”
“Ah! that was before I got LOASBY’S
‘WAHOO.’ You see, I was a dyspeptio
then. Now, I dine when I like, and eat
what I like ; but always with my eye on
the ‘WAHOO’ bottle.”

THE A.N.A.
Big patriots and smaller fry
Who, knowing they must talk or die,
Allow their vanity to balk
Their amor patriae—and talk !
D.
“The A.N.A. is a power,” says Melb.
Herald. So it is—a wind-power. The A.N.A.
is a remarkable body of young men who might be
content if one half were deaf or the other half
umb. It is composed of men whose speeches
endure, and others who endure them.
N.S.W. Minister Young: “£1,750,000
has been expended in reproductive railways dur-
ing the life of this Parliament, and I can see no
limit to the borrowing and spending capacity of
the province in this direction.” Minister Young
can see no limit to the borrowing capacity of the
province. The Bulletin’s large Australian oath !
The abolition of “ Black Rod ” should be
the irrepressible demand of N.S.W. political
economists The history of the office, the antics
of the performer, and the pretensions he sym-
bohses make him a solecism dear at any price.
Yet this doddering image is paid more than a
member of Parliament.
Approaching dissolution has cast its shadow
Stq and well-paid body,
N.S.W Public Works Committee. These (Trea-
Bury)-bleeding patriots now sit every day, except
Saturday and Sunday, and work like galley-slaves
—at A 3 3s. a day additional to their Parliamentary
pay. Chained to the Ore !
Yellow agony at Port Darwin :
Hang Kee, charged with the manslaughter of a lubra
at Borroloola, acquitted. Chew Horn, charged with stab-
bing with intent Hong Kim, found guilty and sentenced
to five years ; second charge of shooting Leung Hem was
withdrawn. Chong Yow, charged with seriously assault-
ing a lubra under the age of consent, found guiity.
Bet Pritchard Morgan bring ’em in !
A just-returned-from-India S. Australian
says that in the Calcutta riots, about a year ago
natives were shot down like dogs. And not a
word about it in the papers, “ out of respect for
the feelings of the Home Govt. ”! One man
boasted that he had accounted for 13 darkies ; and
next day you could not buy a firearm from the
flhops at any price. The whites had mopped them
all up.
S.A. L/abor-party nas decided to run nine
Labor-candidates for country electorates at next
Assembly elections. This move has caused dis-
satisfaction in Ministerial ranks, as many Liberals
who support the Kingston Govt, will probably be
opposed. Those behind the scenes aver that the
Labor crowd will have enough to do in defending
its city seats without splitting the democratic
vote in the provinces.
Two of the Westralian Federationists—
Venn and Briggs-broke silence in one week of
/Tif Y on y ent A° n T qu ' te a remarkable achievement
(the last). One has only to read any talk of the
members of the delegation from the West to
understand Forrest’s mastery. If these are the
best that Westralia could send, the rest of the
politicians in Forrestland can’t be up to the level
of a fifth-class shire council. Even Leake, nominal
leader of the Opposition (Forrest lets him call
himseif that), is a poor, puny thing, palpably
afraid of big John. Why don’t some of the out-
of-Parliament Vic. politicians go over to the West
and play the game there ?
