The Bulletin has received information of
this sort about fifty times :
The Irish are a stronger power than ever in Melbourne
Metropolitan Board of Works. To get a job on the sewer-
works, Pat has only to bring a note to the ganger from
P r jest to say that he is a good member (paying) of the
church, and on he goes, as a rule. Pat is expected, while
in work, to give 2s. 6d. a week to the church ,and some-
body comes along with a book to collect the weekly “ offer-
If Pat doesn’t ante-up, his spiritual adviser arrives
upon the scene—and then !
Truth or lies ?
T. Brown, M.L.A., who loses himself in
arithmetical admiration over the land-tax, but
doesn t like the income-tax, starts his race for
next N.S.W. Parliament astride the following
pathetic incident. He picked up two Condobolin
patients bound for Sydney Hospital. They were
his constituents, but, although they travelled
second-class, the great M.L.A., with the gold
pass on his waistcoat entitling him to a first-class
carriage, went into the second-class and stayed
. his constituents till Sydney was reached.
.Think of the heroic nobility of that! ” ory his
barrackers.
A PERMANENT ATTACHMENT .
BUSH TOWNSMAN (to actor): “I see that little ginger piece is up
with you again"
ACTOR : “ Yes; she’s my wife !”
Thus a New Australian pilgrim recently
returned to Bundaberg (Q.) :
But what was the use of working when there were so
many doing nothing and, apparently, never intending to
do anything? . . . About 50 per cent, were grand men
and the other 50 per cent, duffers. That’s where the
trouble came in. And then che Paraguayan Government
made things worse by offering, just when need was driv-
ing some of the lazy ones to work, to help us with a grant
of 800dol. a month. The result of this subsidy was that
some were content to sit down and eat the bread of idle-
ness—thus paralysing the rest of us. . . . I never ate
monkey ! ! I returned because I came to the conclusion
I could do more good for social democracy in Australia
than in Paraguay.
Which is just what this paper always told the
Paraguay pilgrims.
FREAKS OF FASHION.
BARBER (to young “ forty ” ) : “ Ho w will you have your hair
cut? ”
FORTY: “ Oh, like Cholly Smith—’e’s a loud bloke, ain’t ’e? ”
While most of the present Australasian
Ministries would hardly be recognised by Ballance,
Griffith and the other men who gave them exist-
ence, Reid’s N.S.W. Govt, remains almost the
same as when first constituted, four years ago.
Only two changes have taken place—in the substi-
tution of Vice-President Garran, for W. H. Sut-
tor, and that of Attorney-General Want for G.
B. Simpson, when the latter became a Judge. It
is significant that in nearly every N.S.W. Cabinet
the unpaid Vice-President of the Executive Coun-
cil has been first man to kick over the traces.
Those dreadful persons, “ Patriot ” and
“ Pro Bono Publico,” are having convulsions in
the Melbourne papers about the acceptance of the
lowest (an American) tender for steel rails, and
ask, in a bitter hunk of grief, if Victoria isn’t suffi-
ciently loyal to pay a little more in order to get
its rails from the mother country. Almost on the
same day it was announced that the British
Government railways in India had placed a large
rail contract in the U.S. for the sake of cheapness,
so it appears that the Victorian groveller grovels
to Bull even harder than Bull grovels to himself.

appointment to a salaried
office of an ordained clergy-
man by N.S.W. Public Ser-
vice Board, the clergyman
at non-official hours re-
taining the right to per-
form clerical duties, em-
phasises an important dif-
ference between N. S. W.
policy and that of Victoria.
In Victoria clergymen, un-
til they retire from the
ministry, are not eligible
for employment in the
public service.

perate attempts to get in-
to the police-force. The

English counters, ware-

tion.

A century ago Botany
Bay managed the Sunday
closing of its pubs, with de-
finiteness and decision.
The Licensing Ordinance
of 1801 ordained: “All licensed houses shall be
closed from daybreak on Sunday till 9 p.m. Any-
one selling spirits between these hours shall be
imprisoned and have his house pulled down.”
T.D. : “Have the police ever prosecuted
any of the big Sydney hotel proprietors for
Sunday trading, or selling after hours ? Yet these
places openly ply a trade which has to be done by
the small publicans with mysterious knock mgs and
countersigns and darkened bar-rooms. To-night
(Sunday) I was in the crowded smoking-room of a
leading hotel. Two prominent Govt, officials and
an M.P.—none of them lodgers—were of the com-
pany, and a policeman stood near the entrance
talking to one of the waiters ! ”
It is stated, by those who ought to know
that in proceeding against the Q.N.B. directorate
loinjoe Byrnes was acting in the face of the whole-
Cabinet. Moreover, M‘l]wraith’s slashing attack
on the Morehead crowd was published within a
week after Byrnes announced his determination

and Co. had incited MTlwraitli to urge his pro-
tege, ronijoe, to avenge him.
Ihe Rhadamanthine (Q.) law (now abro-
gated) against the sale of opium to Chinese exists
m spirit in N.S. Wales. Twelve months ago a
station-manager forbade the Chinamen on his run
to sell opium to blacks, males or females, though
numbers of them were as helpless slaves to the
vice as any Chinese. That manager) too, on one
occasion was summoned to court for thrashing a
Chinaman who offended in this way.
A member of N.S. W. Parliament thinks
of inviting the Leg. Assembly to pronounce upon
the action of Judge Backhouse in interdict-
ing, during the late . Carruthers v. Norton
criminal trial, the publication of Mrs. Black-
burn s evidence until after Mr. Carruthers’ evi-
dence in rebuttal was giveh. If he follow alO to
1 majority of counsellers lie will table his motion
within the first week of the new sitting.
At least three N.S.Vfy. Ministers—Car-
ruthers, Gas Garrard, and Sydney Smith—must
look forward apprehensively to the approaching
elections. Minister Smith has hopelessly offended
farmers in his electorate by putting pumpkins and
tomatoes from the local Govt, farm into the market
m competition with their produce. A man with-
out nous enough not to experiment on his own con-
stituents is better out of Parliament, anyway.
Bananaland expenditure on railways runs
about thus : Central District, 3 millions ;
Northern Q., 3| millions ; Southern Q., mil-
lions. Latest obtainable annual dividends ran—
Southern Q., £2 7s. Bd. per cent.; Central Q., £4
Is. Id per cent.; Northern Q., £3 Bs. percent.
Rockhampton to Longreach returned 5 per cent. :
Townsville to Hughenden, 10 per cent. What a
magnificent property this line will be in 20 years’
time!
The general opinion is that nothing short
of an earthquake, or a cannon-ball, or a regiment
of sand-shifters with a steam crane, will shift
George Reid from the N.S. Wales Treasury
benches. This probably accounts for the rumor
that there is a plot on foot in the House for
Kenneth Mackay to march on Parliament House
with his “ wild horse ” and puncture the aforesaid
George, and hang all the Labor-members, and
make Lyne King of Australia.
G. H. Reid’s refusal to dilate to Sydney
reporters upon the Melb. Convention fairly de-
monstrates that the N.S.W. Premier recognises
how badly his political nose is out of j'oint. Reid
jumped into the Federal breach only to find him-
self shoved aside by Barton, and when he tried to
assert himself in Melbourne the other delegates
didn’t even pretend to wait respectfully upon his
utterances—they were only too ready to bait him
with interjections as ironic as his own.
The Queensland Labor-party just now
doesn’t cut a very edifying figure. One section of
it goes round clamoring for a revival of the Opposi-
tion that died in ’96; another section loudly
asseverates that the Labor-party is all-sufficient to
•run the country itself ; a third faction is appar-
ently cutting itself adrift from the Labor platform
altogether. And, meanwhile, the lurid eloquence
of John Hoolan adorns the background with a
chaste and cheerful variety of rhetorical figures
worthy of a clown in a baokblocks circus.
Melbourne Argus is for once on the
Liberal side, and urges Victoria to accept the
Federal. Constitution and become part of a great
nation instead of the whole of a scrappy little
province. The Age covertly urges Victoria to
remain a free, disjointed scrap as heretofore. But
the Age isn’t an unbiassed witness in the matter.
A Federal Government means a Government
which David Syme couldn’t boss, and the obvious
reason why the Age should be against it is that
the stupendous David naturally bucks against his
own downfall. And if Turner and Isaacs are men
with big ambitions their obvious course is to help
Federation along all they can, then go ihto the
Federal Parliament, and leave their forgotten
Syme lamenting in the distance. They wouldn’t
need their Syme any longer then.
The N.S.W. Premier, who ought to be
an authority on such Parliamentary matters, has
declared that President Zeal, of the Vic. Leg.
Council, is “beneath contempt.” A Bulletin
minstrel is singing :
I did aspire, when I was young
And full of green ambition,
To cultivate the “ cheek ” and tongue
Which make a politician.
How foolish were the dreams I dreamt
If Zeal be far beneath contempt!
In Legislative Council chair,
Upon an elevation,
He sits with a commanding air,
A man of rank and station ;
And yet this Great One is condemn’t
To bear the brand “ Beneath Contempt.’*
One time I thougbt the Senate crowd
Were honored and respected,
But now with such as them I’m proud
To.think I’m not connected.
I’ll never make a wild attempt
To join the “ push ” beneath contempt.
Farewell ! my dream of black frock-coats
And shiny hats appalling ;
Let others scheme for voters’ votes,
Whilst I pursue my calling.
The bard, though grimy and unken pt,
Is not, in print, beneath contempt.


Yic. : “Obit. : Squatter Currie, of Larra,
in the West, one of the mightiest merino men.
The merino was planted by the Phoenicians,
Sidonians of the Bible, in Spain, nearly 2000
years ago, and jealously preserved there for the
future benefit of Australia, until the Peninsular
War broke the embargo.”
None of the swan-song speeches of the
Pederal Convention rose to the occasion. Even
the most sanguine of the speakers, Barton,
Symon, Baker and Dealcin, seemed to express
themselves with utterances weighted with doubts.
Trenwith was very disappointing. His apologetic
remarks on the absence of the Referendum were
abject. His part clearly was to declare boldly :
“ Without the Referendum the workers will
accept no cast-iron Federal Constitution!” It
would have come well from him, but he wasn’t big
and bold enough to say it.
In returning thanks for complimentary re-
marks, Convention-President Kingston spoke in
fenerous terms of the supreme egotist Chaffey
inker’s ability as Chairman of Committees.
Baker, speaking later on, failed to return the com-
pliment. He seemed to studiously avoid it. Old
wounds rankle !
By a firedamp explosion in the Dudley
colliery (Newcastle, N.S.W.), on 21st inst., 14 men
were buried ; and, at time of writing, there seems
no probability that any vvill escape alive. The
explosion wrecked the mine underground and
sent surface-machinery flying. By good chance,
the pit had suspended operations, and only a small
party of workmen were down. It is singular to
read that these carried naked lights, although the
colliery is known as a “ hot ” one — i.e., the coal-
seams are full of inflammable gas. Cne would
think that, at this day, the use of safety-lamps
would be made compulsory.

charge of Green Cape lighthouse (N.S.W.),' is the

1886.
Ed. Bulletin.—How would it be for
foreign-bound coal-laden vessels from Newcas+3 e,
N.S.W., to have the Burial Service read for them
as they pass the Nobbys ?—C.P.
Grandmaster and Masonic goat-actor-in-
chief Brassey got a hint to extend his hospitality
to the Wardens, who are the actual “grafters ” in
Masonic lodges, but it was no go. The claret and
buns cost quite enough as it was.
The taking of Edmund Barton’s final
speech in the Convention at Melb. was a triumph
of stenography on the part of Vic. Hansard
staff. Though he spoke for only an hour and a
half, Barton had the printed “galleys” of half
his speech in his hand before he resumed his seat.

you not have a cure—simple, harmless, effective,
of trifling cost, and occupying no more space than

Snelson (chemists to Lord Hampden), King-
street, Sydney. They have discovered a specific
which instantly relieves a headache of any origin,
and which never produces any of the injurious
after-effects so common with other headache cures.

ten doses —for Is. Id. by post.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
In this issue appears the portrait of 70-year-old
Charlie Lawrence, M.C.C. Junior Players’ coach. Law-
rence’s cricketing career began as far back as ’45, and,
playing four years later, at Edinburgh, for an All-England
11 against 22 of Scotland, he took the whole of the Cale-
donian wickets at a cost of only 24 runs made from his
bowling. Settling in Dublin later on, as cricketer to the
Lord-Lieutenant, Lawrence formed and captained the
United All-Ireland 11; and in ’6l, as one of the first All-
England 11, he came to Australia. In '6B, he trained and
took to England the first aboriginal cricket team,
entered the N.S.W. Govt, service upoii his return,
remained in it 24 years, and, upon his retirement, seven
years ago, was snapped-up as junior-coach by the M.C.C.
This body is giving its ground and patronage for a
match, on Lawrence’s behalf, on Easter Saturday and
Monday, between 15 out of Charlie’s 150 pupils and a Vic-
torian 11.
* * • »
Q.: “The peculiarity about Ranjitsinhji’scricket
criticisms is their grudging praise, amounting almost to
ungenerosity. When he gets out, it is never because the
ball beat him, but because ‘he made a bad stroke,’ ‘a
fly got in his eye,’ or ‘ a fieldsman happened to be there.*
Throughout his comments on the test-matches he has
never admitted that the Australian batting, bowling, and
fielding were good. On the contrary, he has repeatedly
stated that it was only ordinary. Apparently, however,
the Indian Prince overlooks the fact that his style of
criticism carries with it the very awkward implication
that the English play must have been very bad indeed.”
* * » *
Cricketer Mac!aren : “There is no man who is
better able to talk "about cricket than Prince Ranjit-
sinjhi.’’ Our colonial oath 1 By the way, a recent
Saturday Review quotes a private letter from Australia,
attributing the Englishmen’s downfall partly to team-
ructions caused by the belief that Ranji, in Stoddart’s
absence, bossed around altogether too freely.
* * * •
The English eleven are not the only cricketers
who get cross when they are beaten. There are in Vic.
Western district two teams of lady cricketers—the “For-
get-me-nots” and the “Sea-Foams,” bless their little
hearts. Recently the latter, whose headquarters is Nir-
randa, defeated the former for the fourth time in succes-
sion. Then the disgusted Forget-me-nots said that their
rivals were mean, nasty, spiteful things who didn’t play
fairly, and added that they (the Forget-me-nots) would
“never, never again visit Nirranda.” So there, now 1
This shows what a lot of the fietful masculine element
there is in the nature of some females.
* * * •
Melbourne University, in its match against
Essendon, has been knocking world’s records kite-high.
On the first day the score stood four wickets for 722,
and at close of play eight wickets were down for 1016
runs, the largest number ever put together in an innings.
So far, four centuries have been scored, and as another
man is 92 not out, there is every probability of still
another hundred being added.
* » * *
“Rap”: Betting being prohibited at certain
cycle sports, sweeps were in full swing—7s. winner, 2s.
second. I went into every sweep with one particular
promoter, and was so struck by the fact that one little
Hebrew either got Ist or 2nd prize nearly every time that
I made a point, after the races, of getting the strength of
it from the boy. The half-sov. was well spent to learn
that the promoter, before he puts the tickets in the bag,
gives the boy the two tickets which have the favorites’
numbers on. The public don’t know that two tickets are
out of the bag, and the promoter and boy whack up the
stake when it comes off, and save the risk of losing the
2s. by taking care that they have only starters whereas
the public generally have three non-starters distributed
amongst them on each race.
* * » •
The Englishmen’s second innings against Vic-
toria was undoubtedly the most spirited performance
they have given in Australia On a rather difficult
wicket, they started with 35 runs in 12min., and con-
tinued with another 14 runs in 6min. The change to
autumn (almost winter) weather appeared to have put
new life into Wainwright and Hayward, and even Ranji—
who professed to revel in summer heat—would seem to
be a more vigorous hitter when the wind blows cool. As
for little Board, the merriest batsman of the crowd, it is
impossible to say how he might have shaped in the games
played under typical Australian conditions.
• * • •
The Englishmen have got as far as Adelaide in
their lingering farewell, and were playing S.A. vigorously
when this paper went to press. Going in first, the
foreigners scored 222, and the home team retaliated with
287, Darling hitting a brilliant 88 and Lyons 79. Jones
took 7 English wickets for 80. When Stoddart’s men
went in again leather-hunting began in earnest. Mason
ran up 84 before his wicket fell; and Wainwright 105-
total, 2 for 193.
* * * •
After an easy victory by a Cairns (N.Q.)
cricket-team, at Cooktown, over the local team, the latter
protested on the ground that the visitors had occasionally
bowled two balls simultaneously. Cooktown, however,
was compelled to admit that towards the close it had it-
self had two wickets at each end to play at—so honors
were held to be easy.

London Pink-Un : “ Let us hear no more about
the bad luck of the Englishmen. They lost the toss, and
the services of their two best bowlers, and the game. But
the best men won 1 ”
• * • •
Ed. Bulletin. — He “Travelling Maorilander’s”
remarks as to baseball-players being able to curl the ball
in the air, writer begs to state that he has, “ without the
aid of whisky,” been deceived by the same optical delu-
sion, for such it is—as, in reality, no bias given to a
sphere by the hand can make the projectile change in flight
its line of direction. This has been conclusively proved
at Woolwich Arsenal by artillery experts.—Ballista.
* c * *
The cable reports that Ebor, on the strength of
several good steeplechase victories, has been sold by Mr.
Gollan for 1200 guineas, a much higher figure than the
horse could have commanded in Australia when he was
two or three years younger. It seems to The Bulletin
that a cool-headed speculator could hardly go wrong in
buying up the pick of Australian flat-racers and ’chasers
(sound limbs being a first consideration) for export to
Hingland, where a good performer is valued according to
his numerous chances of winning fat stakes. The crack
Australian horses are sold at a comparatively low price
because fat stakes are so few and far between.
» •» * *
“ The ‘ talent ’ who d,idn’t back Galway in the
Stewards Steeple at Kempton Park were much shocked
at his easy six-length victory,” writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent. “ Gollan’s progeny of Boolka-
carried list. 71b. of Hickey, &c., and, under the
heavy odds of 10 to 1, reduced to sixes at start, left the
even-money Sheriff Hutton and 6-to-4 Goldfish—the only
other starters—too far behind to see his heel-heavings.
Galway jumped clean and sharp, racing between the
hindrances, and going like a mechanic for the stable pot.
Innuendos shadier than a negro bootblack were slung
like hand-grenades all over the stand and fiat, and
Hickey’s chumminess with the downed favorite’s party
was referred to in comments that supplied in libel what
they lacked in ambiguity. But, nevertheless, I think
the best horse won, though there is lots of Klondyke
waiting Sammy Allen and co. here.”
* • • •
The well-bred True Blue, who has shown him-
self before now as very smart over all distances, is doing
so well at Randwick that his chances in Doncaster and
Sydney Cup look very rosy. He carries 9st. lib. and Bst.
101 b. in the respective events.
* * • •
“ Tut ” Ryan, erstwhile champion Australian
pug, is back in Melb. awaiting word from Frank Slavin to
proceed to Klondyke. Tut credits the resuscitated Slavin
with the possession of £lO,OOO, and states that Joe
Goddard is already on his way to the field. Regarding
Australian boxers in America, Ryan, who is himself a
non-smoker and a teetotaller, practically says they are
utter wrecks from liquor; and concludes by asserting
that the refereeing and general officialing of the game in
U.S. is glaringly cronk. Men down in “ Queensberry ”
bouts for five secs, are counted out by interested time-
keepers, and no pug can even trust his own seconds.
•** . *
Wonderful how the small horses last. Come-
well, 14 years old, is still winning races in India. And,
by the way, what has become of Len Want’s prodigy,
midget Mirabeau, who used to hump 13jst. to victory
round Lillie Bridge ?
» * * *
Shellback : “ Gov. Brassey’s yacht, Helen, is
manned by an amateur crew and has carried off several
money prizes. What has become of these? Have they
been distributed airong the crew or paid into his nibs’s
private banking account ?”
* • • *
Glebe Rowing Club has been steadily coming to
the fore, and at St. Ignatius College regatta on Saturday
it carried off both the maiden fours and senior eights.
Glebe had the misfortune to break the lashing of stroke’s
poppet-head in the eight-oar race, but for which it
would have had a much easier win. A. E. Sewell, the
Glebe coxswain, steered a splendid course, fairly out-
generaling his opponents, and but for his excellent work
the crew could scarcely have won, with the broken lashing.
• * • *
Interesting at Epsom (Vic.) races last week to
see a certain excommunicated plunger on the top limb of
a tree outside the course, instructing his satellites inside
the enclosure, by the tick-tack system, what gees to
invest his money on. It was also gratifying to see a
certain banned bookie punting in shilling sweeps at St.
Kilda bike sports last week.
• » * *
At Capetown, the other day, a horse called Free
Lance, 18 years old, won a double. His jockey was a man
of 56.
* • • •
To those in the know, a N.S.W. horse’s running
at a recent near-Melbourne meet supplied food for reflec-
tion. The party behind the horse was at sixes and
sevens, and when the horse went out it was not known
how the entire party stood. Whilst quoted as starting at
3 to 1, any amount at 100 to 5 was available at the start.
All the jocks, bar the one riding the animal, were “ on ”
at various prices, and the utmost pains were taken by all
but he to make way for the animal. In the end, he had
to win by a very short head, and the time taken —for five
furlongs—was just one second below the record slowest
time ever made in Vic. for six furlongs.
• » * *
Walne made a hack of Body at St. Kilda track
on Saturday, beating him with such ease in both the mile
and ten-mile events of the Vic. Brassard that the Maori-
lander gave the game best on both occasions. A crowd
of over 10,000 rolled up—the biggest this season except-
ing the “special” meetings of the Austral and the
A.N.A. —and showed the wooden-headed L.V.W. how
little the public care for the science of track building.
The League held its meetings this year on the up-to-date
tracks at the Friendly Societies Gardens and the Exhibi-
tion, with the result that the public stayed away vehem-
ently and just let the promoters get through. The St.
Kilda Cricket Club, with its unscientific but safe enough
track in a good situation and with fair conveniences, has
been left to snap a profit of £'3oo, purchasing the Bras-
sard and the ten-mile championship of Australia from the
unbusinesslike League for a trifle of £25.
Parsons made a reappearance on Saturday, but
showed himself far out of it. Though starting from
60yds. he could not live with the men near him, and was
broken up badly at the end.
Ken Lewis is a far better man than when he
beat Bilmartin on one smellful occasion which led to the
late Bill’s disqualification of two years (afterwards
shifted). He snapped the ten-mile championship in rare
style, and rode every inch a champion in the handicap
event.
* * * *
Tote-shop owners in Maoriland have closed-down
on the practice of receiving money after advertised time
of starting—a sure safeguard against being “ had.” But
when an apparently very drunken horse-follower of
means stumbled into one of these dens lately, and-planked
down £25 on a prad of whose victory he hid been advised
by urgent wire, the pile of wealth tempted poor bookie,
who thought it was a soft thing. Of course, intoxication
had been feigned. The prad paid the limit (£10). It was a
cruel thing to do ; but, in betting matters, bookie has no
friends.
* * * *
JFweedie has returned from the States after a
bitter experience. He was matched against one Lawler;
got all over his man in the first round ; played to the
gods ; left himself open ; and Lawler landed him on the
“ point.” Over-confidence has beaten better men than
Tweedie. * /
V * ♦ •
Pennefather, the Westralian Attorney-General
who assaulted his legal partner with a blood-stained
handkerchief and a pair of spectacles, was formerly
known in Vic. racing circles as the member for Alf.
Joseph. Under the patronage of fat Alfred barrister
Pennefather put up for Parliament, also for Bendigo City
Council, and on behalf of Alf. aforesaid he bombarded
the Melb. press with letters calling attention to the awful
immorality of the Tote.
• * * *
Ayrshire is a reminder of bis old man, Dunlop,
in more respects than family likeness. Dunlop was dis-
tinctly a non-stayer for a long time after he had started
business, and the public didn’t think him capable of
getting two miles until he won Melb. Cup, as a five-year-
old. Ayrshire’s stamina was equally unproven prior to
his taking the Australian Cup, as a five-year-old, under
exactly the same weight that Dunlop carried for the Mel-
bourne Cup of ’B7. In each case, the handicapper made
an allowance for the horse’s supposed want of stoutness,
and the starting price was 20 to 1 or thereabouts.
• * * *
A compositor in Grafton Grip office held third
horse in Tattersall’s Newmarket Handicap, value £lOOO.
Five years ago another comp, in the same office also held
third horse in the same handicap.
* » * *
Adelaide footballers are already making big
efforts to fight the lacrosse boom which threatens. For
several years there has been no junior football association
in Adelaide, and even some of the senior clubs will have
trouble in raising teams in the coming winter.
* * * *
The tars of the warship Royal Arthur played
a cricket-match on Sydney Domain the other day.
The umpires took things easy, and one bowler was not
“dead-on.” Wide after wide was sent down unchal-
lenged. At last, a ball pitched fully a yard out of reach
compelled the batsman to apologetically ask “if that
were not a wide?” Umpire Jack, quite unmoved,
shifted his pipe three teeth round, spat musingly, and
said— “ Never seen a widerer.”
• • * •
The easy annexation of the Maoriland 100yds.
amateur championship bv footballer Smith disproves the
theory that an apparently fast man in the Rugby game
is outclassed when lined up for a sports sprint. Rut
Smith is an exception. The Aucklander is one of the
dashing sort capable of making a final rush when his
opponents are tiring. Oliphant, the quarter-mile cham-
pion, is a policeman. Richards, the Australian, was quite
outclassed at the late meeting.
* * * •
The greatest blot on those dens of corruption,
the Sydney pony courses, is the filthy language shouted
on all sides during the running of a race. Not “good,
honest swear-words,” but expressions of the filthiest kind.
Police!
• * * *
Latest racing-swindle was to get a 10s. ticket in
a big sweep, have a dummy-ticket printed, facsimile,
save for absence of number, transfer to it first favorite’s
number from list issued to ticket-holders after drawing-
then sham imbecile drunkenness and sell to a smart book-
maker, who hasn’t cared to write himself down a mug by
making a splash about the matter.
* » * *
Said that a record-breaking cyclist recently
wept with disappointment when the Sydney League re-
fused to grant him “ pace ”to go for another record. In
what other branch of athletics would a man allow' him-
self to be publicly tearful over such a slight puncture of
his vanity ?
* * * •
In Adelaide betting is now carried on by means
of bogus “ clubs” with a nominal entrance fee, and the
new system already begins to flourish. One club, pre-
sided over by a leading bookmaker, has over 1000 mem-
bers. The old tobacconist tote-shop game has gone bung.
* » » *
Said that Brighton (Sydney) has again given the
trotting-men up in despair—after the last disgraceful ex-
hibition. Vic. owners have done better for themselves
and the sport. Richmond (Vic.) programme for 24th Feb.
has 20,18, 10 and 13 entries for the four trots.
* * * ♦
A pacing novelty will adorn London tracks next
season, in the shape of a team of negro multiplet pace-
makers. These swarthy braves are training now to get
used to curves and banking, and some of the larger speci-
mens are ransacking London in a futile endeavor to find
shoes and pedals of sufficient size to accommodate their
20-horse-power feet. All the machines to be ridden by
the blacks will be enamelled white, and there will be no
risk of the racers and pacers not being distinguished from
each other.
* • • •
The Charters Towers Jockey Club, Q., is
making the very large reduction of £lOOO in the added
money for its annual meeting this year. Indicative
that the only' really prosperous town in 'Stralia is feeling
the stress of the times —or merely an experiment, to see
if cutting distances and prizes will produce sufficiently
attractive sport ?
» * * *
Hollowing close upon the S. A. Crown Solicitor’s
letter claiming £49 odd from T. Coleman for the cutting
of the overland cable during the progress of the Dunlop
transcontinental ride, is a writ for £lOO and costs which
has been served upon Coleman within the last few days.
Coleman’s misfortunes seem on the increase—first a
serious operation, from which he has only just recovered,
then the Crown Solicitor’s letter claiming £49, and on
top of this a writ for £lOO. Coleman has not yet decided
what steps he will take in the matter. A strange thing
in connection with this affair is that Messrs. Coleman
and Mather waited upon E. T. Supt. Todd upon their
arrival at Adelaide and fully explained the incident of
the cable cutting, and as he at the time expressed his
satisfaction with the explanation, the whole matter was
thought to be over. Yet, after a lapse of months, the
matter is resurrected, and legal proceedings taken.
£7OO and Twelve Lives.
N.S.W. Marine Board on Monday shuffled for
the third time in the matter of the urgent need for
inquiry into the loss of the full-rigged ship
Atacama; which —sold by auction for £7OOl
patched for =£looo or less ; loaded with 1778 tons
coal and a score of human lives—sailed from New-
castle on Jan 29, and foundered in moderate
weather a few days out from port, drowning two-
tlnrds of her miserable crew. The Bulletin de-
sires to know whether or not evidence is available
to show that endeavours were made to insure the
vessel; and whether or not expert evidence is
available to say that the vessel was in such a con-
dition that she could not have been made sea-
worthy for the amount spent on her patches. The
Marine Board of N.S. Wales—which the Public
Service Board has not yet been permitted to re-
model, in spite of an awful report regarding
its maladministration appears to consist of
one middle-aged gentleman, three bright-look-
ing old gentlemen, three dull - looking old
gentlemen, and one betwixt-and-between old
gentleman. Judging by appearances, four of
the old gentlemen are well past the age of ad-
ministrative usefulness. The President draws
J 2740 a year salary ; the secretary <£47o ; and the
old gentlemen .£lO9 4s. apiece. Three of the old
gentlemen are elected by the ship-owners and five
by Govt. And on Tuesday last all the old gentle-
men, sitting in conclave, resolutely refused to
sub-poena witnesses whom the Govt, solicitor
desired to be brought to give evidence in
the Atacama case. If the witnesses liked to
come “on their own,” well and good; but the
Board wasn’t going to summon evidence against
the owners. Mind ! nothing had been said about
the owners; the question was about evidence
“material to the case”; but the Board was so
impressed with the necessity of protecting the
interests of the owners that it refused to exercise
the power of summoning witnesses conferred upon
it by sec. 27 of its constituting Act. In other
words, the Board, sitting as a court of enquiry,
refused to summon evidence which the Govt.’s
representative assured it was material to the
enquiry! What on earth are the old gentlemen
paid for? So, after a lot of palaver, the Govt,
lawyer undertook to politely beg the witnesses to
attend ; and the case stands adjourned. But the
last of it hasn’t been heard yet —no, not by a long
way. The Bulletin hopes to see Attorney-
General Want give this matter his personal
attention.
With the death of Arthur Palmer, a desper-
ately loud and aggressive bullock-driver drops
out of Australian politics. No politician, even in
Queensland, where the wildest variety of poli-
tician used to grow, was ever gifted with such a
fearsome streak of blue and green vocabulary as
Palmer. He was in Queensland politics for 32
years, his Parliamentary career starting in 1866,
and, as Parliamentary Government only began at
the end of ’59, Palmer dated back almost to the
beginning of things. In ’7O, he formed a dread-
fully profane Ministry, which lasted four years,
and, in ’Bl, he took the Presidency of the Upper
House, and held it till his death. Like almost
every other Q. politician, he was in the Q.N.
Bank trouble (probably it was the loom-
ing prosecution which killed him), and,
like all the other Northern boomers, he
died Very poor. Where the money went to
the Lord only knows, but all the millions which
vanished out of the Q.N.B. and the Q. Investment
Association seem to have left everybody ten
times more dead-broke than before. Palmer had
very little political ability and no great financial
ability outside bullocks and grass ; he was a loud,
fierce, combustible Tory of the primitive squatting
order, and a fair sample of the early Queensland
statesman. The Queensland statesman of the early,
stinkful days of boom and boodle and rum was an
institution such as Australia will never see again—
at least, it hopes not.

Purple patch in the sea of meteorology :
The sea in sympathy adds its stentorian bass to the
whirring of the wind, anon roaring round some cavernous
bluff, then lashing the cliffs in angry tones with supernal
cascades of majestic beauty—while modulating the sonata
in turn to the hissing, seething notes of bubbling foam.
Which the author’s name is Wragge.
Truth (some varieties of it) can beat fiction
hollow any day. The people of Maekay (Q.)
have been long agitating to get a channel cut
through the point south of Signal Station, but
Bananaland Govt., tired of attempting to create
artificial ports, has stubbornly refused assistance.
But the recent cyclone did the job for them —if
Capt. Meaburn, of the Wodonga, is to be believed.
A new channel in the desired location has cer-
tainly been cut, and time will show if it be a
practicable one.
“The one incident of the affair was just after we
realised that there was no danger from the carriages at
the rear. Picking himself up, Mr. Wood, an enthusiastic
amateur photographer, called out, ‘ Where is my
camera?’ It was fished out from among the ruins, and
Mr. Wood afterwards took several pictures of the scene.”
THE KODAK FIEND.
’Twas Judgment Day, and solemnly
The trumpet-call awoke the dead.
And, rising up excitedly,
“ Where is my camera? ” he said.
Millionaire Pullman, the American railway
car-man, cut off his two sons with an income of
•£6OO a year, stating in his last will and testament
that he considered them deficient in the “ sense of
responsibility requisite for the wise use of large
properties and considerable sums of money.” Poor
Pullman, to be so rich ! He seems to have died
of his own awful sense of responsibility for the
future preservation of his dollars.
The eldest daughter of the present Marquis
of Ailesbury has signified her infatuation for life
on the ocean wave by marrying the mate of a
trading schooner. The sister of the late Ailesbury
showed her love for the Turf in a union with
bookmaker Bob “Sutton.” Evidently a head-
strong lot, these Ailesburys. The Bulletin
wouldn’t be surprised to hear that one of the girls,
being “struck on” the Church, had given her
noble self to a curate.
One of the very few characteristic speci-
mens of the art of Turner (writes a Bulletin
correspondent) was sold at auction in Melbourne
the other day. It was a sunset study, all sky,
painted on wood, in size rather smaller than half
a page of The Bulletin, and evidently painted
direct from nature. Close to, it looked like the
bottom of a frying-pan in which meat had been
fried in grease. There were formless daubs of
white and yellow paint, streaked with blood-red
and smeared with brown and black—a confused
jumble of splashes and splutterings of color. But,
seen at a right distance, it was a vivid and glow-
ing bit of sky-painting which might have been a
study for the gorgeous “ Fighting Temeraire.” It
is a pity that such rare bits—richer in instruction
for the student than a room-full of ordinary can-
vases—are not snapped up for our National Gal-
leries. One could pick out a dozen pictures in
the Melbourne gallery not so precious in the
aggregate as this little bit of colored wood.

Chinese cookery is much iri vogue in Wes-
tern Q. Consequently the following item gleaned
by writ&t from a book of Chinese travel tnay
interest some folks :
At an inn in Yunnan we asked for eggs and were sup-
plied with some which were hard and black and rotten.
We were informed they had been kept underground a
year, the Chinese belief being that they go rotten in the
first six months and become sound again in the next half
year.
When the Australian Premiers and Pre-
mieresses occupied sumptuous rooms at the Hotel
Cecil, London, they were waited upon by State
flunkeys. On leaving they were seen to the re-
spective railway-stations by the flunkeys in the
State carriages, and it is understood they all
liberally tipped aforesaid flunkeys. But one lady
(from the West) ere parting went round to all the
flunkeys, shook hands with them, and individually
thanked them for their attention and kindness.
Which, it is said, moved the most superior one of
the lot to remark : “ Blessed if she ain’t the only
real toff hamong ’em.”

Rev. Dr. Bevan, of Melbourne, lately
expressed his opinion that the evening church
meetings, &c., at which young.people attended
were “conducive to the bringing about of man’s
natural state, namely marriage.’’ The doctor
remarked that “ it was really very nice this coming
together of the sexes, and it was a beautiful sight
to see young men and women strolling home
together when in the first bloom of love and fresh-
ness. And, besides, the churches are becoming
dormant and require to be resuscitated with new
blood.” The Fitzroy Gardens are close to Dr.
Be van’s church.—
“ Marie Corelli, to put it mildly, appear 8
to have fallen on evil days indeed,” writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent. “ She is not
advancing in the literary market, and now ‘ her
dear friend,’ Mrs. Tom Kelly, has hopelessly given
her away in the pet magazine of the fire-and-tongs
novelist. All * Tom * did was to publish a letter
amid her eulogistic taffy of M.C. in the Ladies’
Realm, which letter purported to be from Tennyson,
acknowledging the receipt of ‘ Ardath,’ and term-
ing it ‘a truly powerful creation.’ The dead bard
was further debited in the same letter with other
comments of a flattering and philosophic character,
showing strong approval of Corellian ways and
works. Unfortunately, one of Harms worth’s but*
tresses (Springfield, of the Mail) printed the letter
in full'with another, also from Tennyson, which
appeared (addressed to the author) in the second
edition of ‘ The Silver Domino ’ —a work Marie
was always accused of, and always, as now, fiercely
denied. The two letters are word and phrase
alike, and there is no doubt Mrs. Tom in her
desire to boom her friend has burst the bubble and
given the show away. Which is all very funny,
but Corelli is impervious to exposures or ex-
plosions, though many of her readers may not be.”
“ON THE BENCHES IN THE PARK."
(Encore Verses.)
On the benches in the park,
When you’ve had a walk,
After being- to service
Enjoy a pleasant talk
With a pious “ donah,”
Sitting in the dark ;
And you talk about the sermon
On the benches in the park.
On the benches in the park
Who is that we See ?
Is it Deacon Brown’s wife
That’s sitting on his knee I
And who is he that whispers,
“ Church meetings are, my dove,
The times to teach the young and chaste
The mystic art of love ”?

Sparrows have been introduced into Wes-
tralia in a curious way. It is stated by the
Acclimatisation Society that the sparrows which
lately made their appearance m Perth were npt
imported from the East, but came from London
as canaries, and that the Purchasers, on finding
that they had been imposed upon by the London
dealers, liberated the birds. Looks like an
attempt to get level with the dealers who ex
port wild cats from Westralia for the London
market.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opai direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they w
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.

At the Yards.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Waddy, in its decadence, lived through two days
of every week. The awakening began late on
Sunday night, or in the gloom of Monday morning,
with the sound of phlegmatic cursing, softened
and chastened by distance and the enfolding
darkness, the yapping of busy dogs, the pathetic
lowing of weary beasts, the marching of many
hoofs, the slow movements of big flocks and herds
on the ironstone road.
On Sunday evening Waddy was a mile-long
township facing an apparently interminable
post- and - rail fence and a wide stretch of
treeless country dotted with poppet-legs a
township of some fifty houses, a Sunday-
school and a chapel; grey, weather - board,
rainrwashed houses, and an old, bleached,
wooden day-school shored up on either side with
stays, but lurching forward and peering stupidly
out of its painted windows with a ludicrous sug-
gestion of abject drunkenness. On Sunday evening
Waddy was still, silent, and apparently deserted,
oppressed by the weight of a Cousin-Jack Sab-
bath, but on Monday morning tents and covered
carts linked the scattered houses, camp - fires
smoked everywhere, and bearded cattle-men,
making their scant toilet under the decent cover
of a cart-wheel or a rail-fence, enlivened the place
with light-hearted blasphemy and careless snatches
of song, whilst flocks of sheep drifted on the
common, fraternising with the local goats, and
mobs of cattle came slowly down the old toll-road,
sniffing at the bare, brown track; the dust-colored,
sleepy drovers, with sunken heads, nodding on
their limp horses.
Tuesday was sale-day. Monday afternoon was
devoted to the yarding of cattle and the yarding
and drafting of innumerable sheep, the former a
comparatively easy and decorous undertaking, the
latter a clamorous and arduous business provoca-
tive of disgust, dust and madness, and inducing a
thirst that afflicts the toiler like a visible disease,
making him an object of pity to all humane
beholders. The average bullock is of incalculable
mental density, but he has the virtue of faith, and
if you wish him to go through a gateway he goes
in blind confidence; but you may pack a mob of
five or fifty thousand sheep hard against a 10ft.
opening in a fence, and you may yap yourself
hoarse, and beat your trousers to rags, and your
dogs may bark their lungs up, without inducing a
single monumental idiot of the whole flock to
venture through.
* * • *
It is a hot afternoon —it always was a hot after-
noon it seems to me at this distance ; and scores of
thousands of sheep are being hauled, and bullied,
and cursed, and cajoled into the yards, and from
pen to pen. There is a decided substratum of
sound, the ceaseless, senseless bleating of sheep,
low and unvarying; above this the “ yap, yap,
yap ” of the men and boys, the sharp barking of
the dogs, and the lowing of the cattle in the high
yards beyond. Over all, the dust and the sun —a
burning, yellow sun, and a rolling cloud of
powdery dust and everywhere in the air the
taint of sheep, the pungent smell of the beasts,
and the taste of them if you open your lips to
breathe.
It is a great time for several of the boys of
Waddy ; it means half a day from school and the
opportunity of earning a shilling or two playing
at work. Every healthy boy in the township is
ambitious to be a drover, and have a black pipe, a
wonderful horse, and a fabulous dog. Working
at the yards is an approach to the ideal, and
confers a dignity obtainable in no other way. lo
the boys penned in the stuffy, little, drunken school-
house, the others who have a job at the yards are
kings and objects of an envy unspeakable, lhey
command humble service, and awe and admiration
always. , , ,
With treasured whips, home-made of many
fragments, the boys are busy helping with the
drafting and penning of the cattle, or, coated
half -an - inch deep with yellow dust they
are rushing the sheep up and down, bleary
black patches indicating where their eyes maybe,
and muddy circles the probable situation of their
mouths. It is hot, hard work, but, oh ! the glory
of it, and the pride of walking home with money
in one’s pockets, and covered with heaped-up,
honorable dirt 1 t
On sale-day the Drovers’ Arms is hemmed in
with conveyances of all sorts and sizes, each with
a sober horse or two tethered to a wheel, dreaming
with drooped heads under the scorching sun, or
patiently foraging for the last oat in the corner o
the feed-box. The heat is the same, so are the
dust and the smells, but the noises of yesterday
are supplemented by the continuous rattle of the
auctioneers’ voices. Over the simmering stew
sound the voices like the crackling of gum-twigs
in an open fire. j
“ Fo’rteen fi’! fo’rteen ft’ I fo’rteen fi 1 , . „
“ All done eighteen, done eighteen, dono eighteen !
“ Fo’r pounds, I’m bid. Fo’r two six ! fo r two six !
“ Fo’r two six! Goin’ fo’r two six !
There are a few “ drunks ” sleeping in attitudes
of absurd abandon along M’Mahon s fence coated
with flies; and out on the common, with the whole
waste to themselves, two men, stripped to the
buff are engaging in a dull, boozy, interminable
fight. They have been fighting ineptly for many
hours, and their punctilious observance of the
rules of the ring is the wildest farce; but the
comedy is wasted on Waddyand the drovers are
*°?„ bU Z ,0 .& h c,? d the cattle begin to move out
again and off by the roads they came, but the
babel continues, and the buyers, stout, red,bibu-
lous men of one pronounced type, follow the auc
Seers in knots along the platforms over the
vards. The cattle are not the meek, weary
animals of yesterday ; they have been hustled from
one yard to another, rushed around, whipped, and
prodded, packed into small pens, and cursed and
orated to the complete loss of heir fevv poor
wits, and there is now a decided note of revolt
the lowing that rises up from the yards like a
lingering curse. In every lot turned out there
or two beasts filled with blind, blundering
hate - they swing up the road, leading the mobs,
SeyedTnd possessed of devils; cards of saliva,
hang from their muzzles, and their moaning is
ominous, suggesting the vacuous complaining of a
m The°youngsters coming from school kee P s ®
to the rail-fence, but they delight to run deadly
risks, and fill the air with the mofane shrieks of
the drovers. Often a goaded, homicidal bullock
bolts, and then young hearts are glad. It w won-
derful sport to behold that frantic race for the
distant timber, the long, rolling plunge of the
bullock, with the good horse working on his
shoulder, and the volleying whip kicking up dust
and hair from the hollow ribs.
The clatter of the auctioneer grows fainter and
hoarser, and the perishing beasts in the pens below
toss up their heads like the branches of wind-
blown trees, and push hither and thither cease-
lessly with a pitiful “ mooing.”
Marks is selling a pen of Bellman s stock in JNo.
26. The buyers cluster about him on top of the
fence, and bidding is brisk. One bullock, a fine
red beast, has knocked himself about badly ; his
tail has been torn clean away, one horn is cracked
close to the head, and the thick, red blood oozes
out and blackens, rolling sluggishly down the
white blaze of his face. It is a large yard, and
there is plenty of room for the brute to charge,
which he does several times, now and then driving
blindly into another bullock, but generally crack-
ing his skull sharply on the great posts of the fence.
Suddenly a portly, helmeted buyer, leaning over
to get a better view, misses his centre of gravity,
and goes after it, the whole 16st. plumping solidly
into the slush of the yard below. The red bullock
is at him instantly. The good horn takes Langley
in the back of the trousers, and the drive rips him
bare to the collar, leaving him unmarked, but
prone in a condition of unseemly nudity.
The beast backs away for another drive, shaking
his head and uttering his low, tigerish bellow, the
expression of all malevolence; but at the same
moment a figure drops smartly from the platform,
and a ragged, smudgy, red-headed small boy
is riding astride the animal’s neck, diverting his
attention by battering him over the eyes with an
old felt hat. Dicky Haddon has performed
this feat often for his own amusement, to
the amazement of staid and matronly cows, but
this is a beast of another color, and the trick is
done in response to an involuntary heroic impulse.
The bullock backs about the yard, tossing nis
head in an effort to be rid of his mysterious
burden, and many hands clutch stout old Langfey
from the other, pen, and tow him along through
the mire to the gate, the bottom rail of which is
high enough from the ground to enable them to
null him out of danger. Then he is borne away, un-
nerved and invertebrate. The boy seizes his
opportunity, drops from the bullock and slips
under the gate like a cat. Then he follows Lang-
ley, and stands in eager expectance whilst the
damaged grazier is being bundled into his buggy.
Really, Langley suffers from nothing more serious
than blue funk, but is oblivious of everything ex-
cepting a great craving for neat brandy, and is
driven off without bestowing even a glance on his
small preserver. . .
This base ingratitude inspires the youth with a
loathing that can only be expressed m the choice
idiom of the yards, and the disappointed hero
yells bitter and blasphemous insults after Jabez
Langley, moneyed-man and M.JL.A. _
EDWARD DYSON.

Clearly they don’t put too fine a point upon it, up
North.
Every year Bananaland hatches out, a
fresh plague. ’95 and ’96 brought the ticks ; 97
swarms of grasshoppers; ’9B (m coastal districts)
is breeding a fearsome variety of mosquito that
laughs the ordinary curtain to scorn. Fine muslin
alone seems able to keep the pest at a distance.
This is probably Nature’s way of squaring
accounts with the province that doesn t get any
heat-wave.
A Chance Move.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The high-wheeled dogcart swept out of the
open gates into the dark road. The.man.had beer
kept waiting, and was angry. Bidding him good
bye ? ” he muttered, and the woman quivered. A
solitary tear rolled down her cheek, and the man
stooped his head to kiss her. She moved her head
aside-he thought by accident; but he tried again,
and this time he knew it was by design. . Lure
you !” he said, fiercely. “ I have done all I could ,
all but cut a friend’s throat.”
“ And I,” said the woman—“ for you I have be-
come a wanton.”
* * * *
The man did not reply, but a vicious cut
of the whip made the mare leap forward, and
the dogcart swayed from side to side of the rou h
road, as the trees flew by m the half-darkness like
huge shadow banks. Still he lashed the mare, and
the woman clutched his arm and shrieked He
laughed. “ I’ll frighten the soul out of you ! he
said. “I am different from him.
* • » *
They reached a rise on the road, and, like
a flash, she looked back. A lonely, desolate spark,
seemingly miles away, was the only light visible.
The woman caught the man s arm again. I
she cried, “ I won’t go on. I am going back
Then she broke down, sobbing, and the man shook
her off with a curse. With difficulty, he pulled
up and looked. The red light winked in and out
at the station, a mile past the bridge at the bottom
of the hill. “ Come on ! he said ; then, more
gently, “ the light in front is Love.
g ‘‘lt is reef,” the woman said, “ but the white
light behind is Honor.” ,
Instantly the man gathered up the reins
again, and lashed the quivering mare into a
gallop down the steep road to the bottom of the
hdl The cart swayed wildly from side to side,
but still the man used the whip, and the woman
shrieked, till, just at the bottom, one wheel jerked
high into air—and over they went !
* * * *
The man never moved ; the splash-board
had split his skull. The woman was little hurt.
Honor had won by a chance move ; and now
the man is forgotten !

Evidently in ignorance that a small cottage
belonging to a particularly pious Perth (W.A.)
church was rented to a foreign lady of unexacting
morals, the pastor of said church has been vio-
lently denouncing all such unspeakable creatures
from the pulpit. And, now that somebody has
jogged his memory about the matter, the result,
he says, is that the agent can’t get the dreadful
person out; whereas the d.p.’s version is that
“ zey did vant to rise my rent just two times ! ”
At latest accounts Delilah was still in situ, and
the church was still profiting only half as much by
her industry as it wants to.

THE CASE OF MR. FREDERICK MARKS.
SUFFERED FROM BILILOUSNESS, SICK HEADACHE, AND LOSS OF APPETITE*
BILE BEANS EFFECT ANOTHER MIRACULOUS CURE.
“All through the stifling muggy days of the present summer,” said Mr. F. Marks, of Park-
road, Moore Park, Sydney, with a smile to a reporter, “ I have suffered severely and continually from
repeated attacks of biliousness and sick headaches. Being recommended by a friend to give Bile Beans
a tria rpT SO * an( * ai *J a PPy s^ate that lam in every way completely and perfectly cured.
Ihe excessive heat, and those warm, muggy days upon which the atmosphere in the city is
largely imbued with moisture from the sea were undoubtedly the cause of my afflictions, for my con-
stitution is not prone to these ailments.
With the advent of summer came the bilious attacks, and these gradually became worse and
more frequent as the heat continued, until at last I came to expect an
attack about every three days, and I always got it. I tried patent
medicines and some pills, which have been largely advertised for
many years, but I found I derived no benefit from them. The
juice of a lemon and seidlitz powders also failed to afford me relief.
Sometimes the biliousness became so bad that when I was at work
in the office I would be unable to read my correspondence and papers
correctly, for the lines would dance and quiver before me until the
letters seemed merely an indistinct mass. Always a good sleeper,
my rest was somewhat disturbed, and when I rose in the morning I
would have an unpleasant taste in my mouth, attended by a horrid
sick headache. Under these circumstances my appetite would at
times completely fail me, and sometimes I took no food at all, merely
drinking water in an endeavor to wash the biliousness out of my
system. Needless to say, this proved quite ineffective, and I found
jfty physical virility deserting me, leaving in its place an unaccus-
. tu j 4-u u • tomed and distasteful feeling of listlessness. At this juncture,
however, I had the happiness to try a course of BILE BEANS, with the gratifying result that
notwithstanding the heat and muggmess, I now feel as full of vitality and as free from all listlessness
as if we were in the depth of winter. Ihe biliousness, the headache, the loss of appetite, and the
disability to read small print have all completely vanished. In fact, BILE BEANS cured me com-
pletely, and in a very short time, too, and especially do I recommend them during the summer
months, when the distressing heat causes us more trouble and brings on more ailments than we are
aware of.”

PRESSURE OF BUSINESS CAUSE OF HIS ILL-HEALTH.
HIS SYSTEM ENTIRELY RUN DOWN.
The Manager, 248 Cardigan-st., Carlton, Melb., Dee. 81, 1897.
Bile Bean Manufacturing Co., Sydney, N.S.W.
Dear Sir —Owing to an exceptionally heavy and protracted pressure of business during the past
tew months, I became very low-spirited, my whole system being fairly run down. I suffered a great
deal from insomnia, followed by loss of appetite, and in consequence my nerves were completely
unstrung ; in fact, my nervous system was so much upset that I became totally unfit for business. A
tew weeks back a friend recommended a course of Bile Beans, and I am pleased to say that after
using them for a short period the insomnia entirely disappeared, and my appetite returned. Since
taking this medicine my weight has increased nearly a stone, and I am now in the best of health and
spirits, and have no hesitation in recommending them in all cases similar to my own.—Gratefully yourst
(Signed) T. A. CHAPPEL.

In the Cathedral.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The yearning organ sobbed within the fane,
The Asaph voices sang up to the stars,
As though to lift Earth’s sorrow and her pain
Beyond all earthly bars.
The mounting treble made the dome its eoi»u.
And the religious chords
Swayed in the chancel— swung up to the groin—
And gave their place to words—
To words that mated Love and Genius bore
In times of m artyr’d stress,
Till, polished by the centuries’ long lore,
They shone in holiness.
My soul became the emperor of my will;
I bore the fardel of the wide World’s woe;
I felt of life the dolor and the thrill,
The tear-gush and the throe.
The fine romances of the pulpit-preaching
1 heard with ears a-dulled ;
Accepting— n °t believing in—the teaching
Gold reasoning annulled.
The kindly promise of the resurrection,
The ending of all pain,
The errors of the flesh, dead past correction,
The sprouting of dead grain . . .
A dark-tressed angel shares my book—her tender,
.Beloved, clear-belled voice uplifts the hymn ;
1 lie grave shall clasp her, palpitant and slender •
Her eyes the dust shall dim !
Her secrets sweet the eager clods shall ravish ;
Her heart the worm shall rust;
And all the lover tricks I on her lavish
Forgotten be in dust.
We die like poppies in the midst of beauty ;
We early die and late ;
And t^l ' s frozen Heaven, past barren dutv,
Not Time nor Place, but State.
A grim Dead-Level of machine-made singers—
No individual care:
All minor bells rung all by minor ringers—
Great Love nor Great Hate there !
The wheat is sown and in the Spring is sprinrino-
And Earth is green again ;
But, if the grain forget its first up-bringing,
What joy can sway the grain ?
The preacher of the Newer Life is droning,
After we ripe and rot:
I hear no comfort, for my soul is moanin°-
Forgotten ! Self-forgot!
Oh, Shadow-Heav’n, spun of frayed threads of
Time,
Built by Man’s longing in a many lands !
Oh, lying sanguineness ! Oh, dreain sublime !
Oh, house not made with hands !
The creeds with all their details I compare;
Their miracles I put to evidence,
And find them falsely great and lying fair—
Sweet foes of thought and sense.
No sun e’er faltered in young Joshua’s path ;
No multitude miraculously fed ;
No beast saved Balaam from Jehovah’s wrath ;
No quick rose from the dead !
' No cloudy presence hovered o’er the ark ;
No sacrifice has carried human pleas ;
No human feet sped to the storm-stressed bark
On high o’ertoppling seas !
Ah, angel by my side, whose bell rings out,
Whose voice high in the choral hymn rejoices,
The weary World and I are full of doubt—
Oh, drown the doubting wailing of our voices !
I doubt ! I die ! She faith has in the prophet,
And yet she too shall die ;
Vain shadows all, and liars born of Tophet 1
The promise is a lie !
And so I wish, my doubts and fears then over,
I lie near to her hand,
Under a green tree and bee-raided clover
In a fair sunny land . . .
The sobbing voluntary seeks the chancel;
We walk the qpiiet street —
Dear lovers twain whose love the Grave shall can-
cel,
Silencing kisses sweet ?
I know not, but my doubtings all I know;
I burn in wondering as the moth in light . . .
Her love is told me, tremulant and low —
I have my Heaven to-night /
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
Frost, Famish and Filthy Lucre.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Told to the writer, in Dawson City, last August,
by a miner who has been alternately freezing and
thawing in Alaska for seven years.
I had taken advantage of clear weather and
perfect sledding to start to Circle City for grub
enough to last over the spring clean-up. The glass
registered only 38 below—quite mild compared
to the 65 and 70 of a few days before. My dogs
were fat. ‘ Shorty,’ my Siwash helper and side-
partner, always saw that there was plenty of dog-
feed (fish) laid by in summer. ‘ Shorty ’ stayed
behind to look after things, and I set out on my
90-mile journey with sled and dogs, and the last
pint of whisky out of my demijohn. After getting
over the hills, I went down a creek that runs
northerly into the big bend of the Birch.
“ There wasn’t a cabin or a prospector that I
knew of along that creek that winter—not a soul
between me and Pokey Creek, 50 miles north
The ice was pretty rough, but ‘ Shorty ’ had made
my sled, and the moose thongs allowed enough
give ’ to keep it from racking to pieces. Dark-
ness came on early, and the sky threatened a
snow-flurry, so, about three o’clock, I decided to
be on the safe side, and looked for a place to
camp. With the rising wind, it was rapidly
growing colder, and the pebble-like ice had
cut the dogs’ boots and feet. That settled
it I couldn’t camp a minute too soon.
Luckily, the snow wasn’t more than 10 or 12in.
deep. I worked desperately and soon had a big
pile of branches on the lee side of the creek bench.
But I had to pull back my face furs to gather the
wood, and my beard was soon caked with ice. The
dogs were whimpering—a sure sign of extreme tem-
perature. The trees were cracking like a thousand
Winchesters; every minute or so came the artil-
lery—the ice and the big trees. I don’t believe I
was more than five minutes over the job, for I
knew it was a race against time to save the dogs’
feet, but when it came to matches—l hadn’t one
about me ! Box must have been jolted out of my
pocket! I was scared; I was numbing and my
head begun to feel queer. I thought about brain-
chill, and I jumped and yelled for fear. Brain-
chill first makes a fellow as crazy as a loon ; then
a^jr ’ U P an -d around—if he does get up
and around —he is just no account whatever;
mopes about, not caring much whether he’s dead
or alive !
It was awful! —no fire—the trees and ice
cracking, the wind howling, the dogs crying like
children ! No time to think, however, so I just
grabbed ‘ Tige ’ by his throat latch and started
down thecreek. The dogs were still hitched together
and fora wonder they came along willingly. When
dogs stop once, if it is only 12 o’clock, it is
almost impossible to get them to move again
that day ; but the intense cold seemed to freeze
the cussedness out of them. I struggled on, hardly
conscious, dragging the dogs after me, till sud-
denly I heard a terrible noise, a roar like the
ocean, then crack ! crack ! boom ! The ice rose
under my feet, and I was pitched violently for-
ward on my head and stunned.
“ W hen I came-to I was in a bunk, covered with
furs, and, turning round, I saw right by me one of
the sheet-iron stoves that the Alaska Co. had
brought in the summer before. There were three
men in the cabin—Joe Henderson, and Ell. and
Bill Mentzer, and, seeing I was coming round,
they dosed me with soup and whisky.
“ They had pitched camp there since I had been
along that trail before. They had struck pay-dirt,
and, having plenty provisions, they concluded to
winter there. I was in front of their cabin when
the ice-quake came. The shock threw me on the
bank, and then I must have staggered, uncon-
sciously up to the cabin, and fallen against the
door. It was the second day after when’ I came-
to in that bunk. We found the dogs frozen hard
as rocks a quarter-of-a-mile up the creek. The
ice in the creek had been bodily lifted out on to
the bank, and lay there like a monster white
snake, as big around as a house and nearly half-a-
mile long.
“ There wasn’t a thermometer from Sixty Mile
to Fort Hamlin that didn’t freeze that night except
the one hanging on the North-Western Company’s
store at Circle. It marked 92deg. below; never
make me believe but what it froze at that point,
for we all knew it was way below 100 that night.
A whole village-full of ‘ Injins ’ were frozen to
death up near Fort Yukon—7o all told, and a dozen
or more strong men got ‘brain chill’ before they
could reach shelter. Now, here comes the point !
Henderson and the Mentzers cleaned up nigh
onto 24,000 dollars in the bed of that creek in two
days. And that ice-quake was the cause of the
discovery of the richest diggings ever found along
the Birch.”
• * • *
Whether or not “ Big Sandy’s ” yarn was em-
bellished in other respects, the writer can testify
that his “ cold spell ” is not worse than the reality.
A record of 60, 70, and even 80 below zero is not
uncommon, but even this severity is not dangerous,
unless there is a lack of proper clothing and
shelter, or of fat-producing foods.
February and March will seem the longest
two months in the lives of the 4000 people huddled
in Dawson City and the diggings -up along the
Bonanza and Eldorado. The brief, dim day-
time, from nine to three o’clock, only empha-
sises the long night. Artificial light is scarce;
candles are five dollars apiece, and few to
be had. The. Alaska Commercial Co. and
the N.A. Trading and Transportation Co. (the
twostores ”) couldn’t sell you a gallon of
mu you **ad the wealth of a Vanderbilt.
The saloons have been without liquor since
January, and the camp was put on limited rations
shortly before Christmas. Now, short rations are
bad enough in a warm climate, as any inhabitant
of Sydney Domain can testify, but in a cold
country like Alaska a shortage is Death ! You
eat twice or three times as much there as in a
temperate climate. You eat fat as a Fiji islander
eats bananas—at any and all times. It is neces-
sary to eat so much, and on what we here would
consider ample rations all sustained outside work
must cease—the weak will die and the strong
become weak. The latest reliable news said
they would be on “short rations long before
spring!” And how in all that cold, hunger;
misery and death the plentitude of gold
will mock them. Big-hearted Alick M‘Donald
will sit and look at his piled-up millions,
and curse them because they cannot relieve
the sufferings of scores of women and children
in the camp, nor prevent the slow and hor-
rible torture of hundreds, whose pinched faces
and shivering forms, under loads of furs, tell
plainly that they are shivering to death from the
inside , It’s not the outside cold that is the trou-
ble now—it’s the lack of- life within.
There is only one thing left to do with their
gold—gamble. And gamble they do, day in and
day outsuch high stakes were never seen before,
for gold has lost its value. Dogged, haggard, and
unkempt, they sit over their cards like so many
jewellers peering into watches. The door opens,
and a shaggy giant comes in and crowds his way
to the stove. The men look at him, and he
shakes his head. “ It’s all over, fellers,” he says;
“ she and the little ’un died a half-hour ago.”
That is Dawson City “life” this month of
March. Not quite so bad at the mines, where
one has work to break the monotony. But there
is not a man there digging who wouldn’t rather
that stone just thrown up by his shovel was,a
potato than a lump of gold as big as his fist.
